# 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

## 1.1 Icing Physics and Regimes

* **Binary classification of icing**
  The study explicitly considers only **dry rime** and **wet glaze** icing.

  * Rime is assumed to correspond to **100% freezing fraction**
  * Glaze corresponds to **partial freezing with water runback**
* **Fixed representative conditions**

  * Rime: −20 °C, 70 m/s, LWC = 0.35 g/m³
  * Glaze: −5 °C, 77 m/s, LWC = 0.35 g/m³
    These single points are treated as representative of broader regimes.

## 1.2 Aerodynamic Representation

* **Angle of attack fixed at 0° during icing accretion**

  * Ice growth physics is decoupled from operational pitch variation.
* **Two-dimensional blade section**

  * Spanwise flow, centrifugal forces, and Coriolis effects are excluded.
* **Ice accretes primarily at the leading edge**

  * Secondary accretion farther downstream is considered negligible in performance impact.

## 1.3 Numerical Modeling Assumptions

* **Eulerian droplet phase**

  * Droplets assumed spherical, non-interacting, and continuum-averaged.
* **Quasi-steady multi-shot accretion**

  * Icing is modeled as discrete steady states rather than fully transient growth.
* **Shin sand-grain roughness model**

  * Ice roughness mapped to equivalent sand-grain height via empirical correlation.
* **k-ω SST turbulence model**

  * Assumed sufficient for separated flow with evolving roughness.
* **ALE mesh deformation**

  * Ice growth does not invalidate mesh topology or numerical stability.

# 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

## 2.1 Thermodynamic Simplifications

* **Uniform substrate temperature**

  * Blade material thermal inertia and internal conduction are not explicitly modeled.
* **No internal heat sources**

  * No latent heat transport into the blade structure is considered.
* **No radiation effects**

  * Long-wave radiative heat transfer is neglected.

## 2.2 Droplet and Film Physics

* **No splashing or secondary breakup**

  * High-speed droplet impact is assumed to either stick or run back smoothly.
* **Runback modeled as laminar film**

  * Turbulent film breakup, rivulet formation, and bead instability are excluded.
* **No re-entrainment**

  * Runback water does not re-atomize into the airflow.

## 2.3 Surface and Material Assumptions

* **Galvanized steel as surrogate for composite blades**

  * Differences in thermal conductivity, surface energy, and roughness evolution are ignored.
* **Surface roughness evolves monotonically**

  * Ice erosion, shedding, or fracture is not modeled.
* **Ice density uniform within regime**

  * Density gradients within glaze horns or rime feathers are neglected.

## 2.4 Operational Assumptions

* **Blade remains operational throughout icing**

  * No shutdown, load limit, vibration threshold, or ice-throw constraints are modeled.
* **Aerodynamic degradation assessed post-icing**

  * Feedback of degraded aerodynamics on further ice accretion is weakly coupled.

# 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

## 3.1 Conservation Laws

* **Mass conservation**

  * Airflow: ∂ρ/∂t + ∇·(ρv) = 0
  * Droplets: Eulerian continuity with collection efficiency
* **Momentum conservation**

  * Navier–Stokes with evolving rough boundary
* **Energy conservation**

  * Balance of:

    * Convective heat flux
    * Latent heat of fusion
    * Evaporative cooling
    * Kinetic heating

**Validity regime:**

* Subsonic, incompressible flow
* No shock formation
* Moderate Reynolds number (Re ≈ 3×10⁶)

---

## 3.2 Phase Change Physics

* **Messinger-type icing model**

  * Freezing fraction F governs morphology
  * Rime: F ≈ 1
  * Glaze: F < 1 → runback
* **Latent heat bottleneck**

  * For glaze, heat removal rate limits freezing location, not water availability

---

## 3.3 Boundary Layer Theory

* Surface roughness increases:

  * Momentum thickness
  * Skin friction
  * Convective heat transfer
* Leads to:

  * Earlier separation
  * Higher drag
  * Reduced lift

# 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PHYSICAL PROCESSES

| Interaction                              | Effect                               |
| ---------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------ |
| Roughness ↑ ↔ Heat flux ↑                | Accelerates ice growth at stagnation |
| Ice shape ↔ Droplet collision efficiency | Alters future accretion              |
| Glaze runback ↔ Aerodynamic heating      | Promotes horn growth                 |
| Ice geometry ↔ Flow separation           | Dominates drag increase              |
| Heat flux ↔ Freezing fraction            | Controls rime–glaze transition       |

This feedback loop is **strongly nonlinear**, especially for glaze conditions.

# 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

### Example: 100% Freezing in Rime

* **Assumption:** No runback
* **Violation:** If freezing fraction < 1
* **Consequence:**

  * Film flow appears
  * Horns form
  * Aerodynamic penalty increases dramatically

### Example: Eulerian Droplet Model

* **Assumption:** Mean behavior sufficient
* **Violation:** Droplet inertia dominates
* **Consequence:**

  * Splashing
  * Secondary atomization
  * Misplaced ice growth regions

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Icing Environment

* Atmospheric icing is adequately represented by **mixed glaze ice** accreted in an icing wind tunnel.
* A single test condition of **−10 °C, 80 % RH, 25 m/s wind speed** is representative of operational icing scenarios.
* Ice accretion from **deionized water droplets** captures the essential physics of natural icing.
* Leaving ice to solidify for **~16 hours** produces a stable, fully consolidated ice body suitable for adhesion testing.

**Consequence**
Results are explicitly bounded to one icing regime. Rime-dominated icing, freezing rain, or transient temperature cycling are not represented.

---

### 1.2 Ice Adhesion Measurement

* Ice adhesion strength can be expressed as:

  τ = F / A

  using centrifugal force as a proxy for interfacial shear failure.
* The centrifugal ice adhesion tester (CAT) produces **pure shear detachment**, with negligible peel or tensile components.
* Constant angular acceleration of **300 rpm/s** does not introduce rate-dependent fracture artifacts.

**Consequence**
Measured adhesion implicitly assumes quasi-static fracture mechanics and uniform stress distribution across the ice footprint.

---

### 1.3 Coating and SLIPS Function

* Porous LPCS coatings act as **oil reservoirs** without structural collapse during icing cycles.
* Silicone oil remains chemically stable, immiscible with water, and mechanically retained by capillary forces.
* Side sealing fully eliminates oil loss from lateral drainage, isolating surface-controlled effects.

**Consequence**
Oil depletion is treated as a surface phenomenon, not a bulk structural limitation.

---

### 1.4 Materials

* PEEK retains its mechanical and chemical integrity under:

  * LPCS impact,
  * substrate preheating to ~250 °C,
  * repeated icing and centrifugal loading.
* Al₂O₃ reinforcement improves adhesion through mechanical interlocking without introducing brittle failure paths.
* SS316L substrate behavior is passive and does not influence ice adhesion outcomes.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

---

### 2.1 Manufacturing and Structural Assumptions

* LPCS produces **repeatable porosity morphology** independent of minor spray fluctuations.
* Embedded Al₂O₃ particles do not create stress concentrators that evolve under cyclic loading.
* No micro-debonding occurs between PEEK and Al₂O₃ during centrifugal testing.

**Hidden risk**
Ceramic particle cracking or polymer creep could gradually alter pore connectivity and oil retention.

---

### 2.2 Oil Transport and Retention

* Capillary pressure within pores exceeds:

  * centrifugal expulsion forces,
  * droplet impact shear,
  * cloaking-induced oil removal.
* Oil viscosity of **50 cSt** is near-optimal across all operating conditions.

**Hidden risk**
Capillary retention is temperature-dependent. Lower temperatures increase oil viscosity and reduce replenishment kinetics.

---

### 2.3 Wetting Regime Interpretation

* Cassie-Baxter analogies applied to SLIPS assume:

  * a continuous oil film,
  * negligible solid asperity contact with water.
* Transition from cloaked to partially exposed surfaces is gradual and reversible.

**Hidden risk**
Once oil coverage falls below a percolation threshold, wetting transitions become abrupt and non-recoverable without refilling.

---

### 2.4 Time and Aging

* No oxidative degradation, chain scission, or embrittlement of PEEK occurs during testing.
* Silicone oil does not chemically age, volatilize, or absorb contaminants during icing cycles.
* Mechanical properties after months of storage remain unchanged.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

---

### 3.1 Cold Spray Deposition Mechanics

**Law**

* Particle adhesion occurs when kinetic energy exceeds a critical velocity for plastic deformation:

  ½ m v² → plastic work + heat

**Application**

* PEEK plastically deforms and embeds Al₂O₃ particles.
* No melting or oxidation occurs.

**Validity regime**

* Solid-state deformation only.
* Fails if particle temperature approaches melting or if velocity falls below critical.

---

### 3.2 Capillarity and Oil Retention

**Law**

* Capillary pressure:

  ΔP = 2γ cosθ / r

**Application**

* Retains silicone oil within porous PEEK matrix.
* Governs resistance to centrifugal expulsion.

**Limits**

* Effective only while pore radii remain small and continuous.
* Breaks down under high centrifugal acceleration or pore coalescence.

---

### 3.3 Interfacial Energy and Cloaking

**Law**

* Spreading parameter:

  S = γ_water-air − (γ_oil-air + γ_oil-water)

**Application**

* Positive S enables oil cloaking of water droplets.
* Explains oil depletion during repeated droplet shedding.

**Second-order effects**

* Cloaking accelerates lubricant loss while reducing instantaneous adhesion.

---

### 3.4 Ice Adhesion and Fracture

**Law**

* Adhesion failure occurs when applied shear stress exceeds weakest of:

  * ice cohesive strength,
  * oil-mediated interfacial strength,
  * coating cohesion.

**Application**

* SLIPS reduce solid-solid contact area, lowering τ.
* Increased τ during cycles reflects lubricant depletion, not substrate change.

---

### 3.5 Wetting Physics

**Law**

* Wenzel regime: full liquid penetration of roughness.
* Cassie-Baxter or lubricated regime: interposed phase reduces pinning.

**Application**

* Explains high WCA but strong pinning on porous coatings.
* Explains low CAH and sliding on SLIPS despite lower WCA.

---

## 4. Interdependencies

* **Porosity ↔ oil retention ↔ ice adhesion**
  Porosity enables SLIPS but also increases oil loss pathways.
* **Oil viscosity ↔ durability ↔ anti-icing performance**
  Low viscosity improves initial performance but degrades faster.
* **Centrifugal loading ↔ oil depletion ↔ measured adhesion**
  Test method itself accelerates SLIPS degradation.
* **Preheating ↔ coating compactness ↔ durability**
  Improved interlocking delays cohesive failure under cyclic loads.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Violated Assumption                     | Resulting Breakdown                       |
| --------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------- |
| Uniform shear stress                    | Local peel fracture dominates CAT results |
| Stable oil chemistry                    | Ice adhesion increases irreversibly       |
| Capillary retention > centrifugal force | Rapid SLIPS failure                       |
| No polymer creep                        | Pore collapse reduces oil reservoir       |
| Constant wetting regime                 | Sudden transition to Wenzel state         |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* **Cohesive failure of SLIPS** after oil depletion.
* **Gradual pore closure** due to polymer viscoelastic creep.
* **Ceramic particle fracture** acting as crack initiators.
* **Test-induced degradation** masking real service performance.
* **Temperature-dependent viscosity mismatch** under colder climates.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* LPCS chosen to avoid polymer melting, sacrificing coating smoothness.
* Porosity optimized for oil retention, not mechanical strength.
* CAT testing favors comparative ranking, not absolute field performance.
* SLIPS reusability assumes **maintenance access for oil refilling**, not always feasible in real systems.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* No aerodynamic shear during service modeled.
* No UV, chemical, or particulate erosion considered.
* No coupling to structural vibration, bending, or fatigue.
* Ice accretion occurs on flat coupons, not curved or rotating components.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution

* Ice adhesion increases monotonically with lubricant loss.
* SLIPS performance is **state-dependent**, not material-constant.
* Refilling resets system state, indicating reversible but not self-sustaining behavior.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

* Statistical robustness via parallel samples, not physical redundancy.
* No explicit margin against:

  * oil depletion rate,
  * coating delamination,
  * extreme centrifugal loads.
* Single-point failure: loss of lubricant continuity.

---

## 11. Design Regime vs Real Operation

**Designed for**

* Controlled icing.
* Periodic inspection and maintenance.
* Flat or mildly stressed surfaces.

**Real operation**

* Continuous droplet impingement.
* Rotational and vibrational loads.
* No practical lubricant refilling.

**Robustness**

* Mechanisms are physically sound.
* Long-term deployment requires active lubricant management or self-replenishing designs.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Environmental and Climatic Assumptions

* Cold-region tunnels experience **predictable seasonal and diurnal temperature variations** that can be represented using a composite sinusoidal function combining annual and short-term cycles.
* Tunnel portal air temperature and wind speed are the **dominant boundary drivers** of the internal tunnel temperature field.
* The **ground temperature is constant** at approximately 6.5 °C and can be treated as a fixed thermal boundary.
* February 6 represents a **conservative extreme low-temperature condition** suitable for freezing risk prediction.

**Consequence**
Transient meteorological extremes, cold snaps beyond historical averages, and long-term climate variability are excluded from the design envelope.

---

### 1.2 Structural and Geometric Assumptions

* Tunnel drainage systems can be categorized into three discrete configurations: insulated side ditches, insulated central ditches, and deeply buried central ditches.
* Drainage ditch walls are the **critical interface** governing freezing behavior due to water-wall heat exchange.
* Insulation layers, concrete linings, and surrounding rock are **layered, homogeneous, and isotropic** materials.
* A boundary distance of **three times the tunnel diameter** is sufficient to eliminate boundary effects in numerical simulations.

**Consequence**
Local heterogeneities, construction defects, cracks, and material aging effects are not represented.

---

### 1.3 Thermal and Flow Modeling Assumptions

* Water freezes when the **ditch wall temperature reaches −1 °C**, establishing a practical freezing criterion.
* Water flow in drainage ditches is assumed **laminar**, with Reynolds number below 2000.
* Flow velocity is **uniform across the cross section** and constant along the longitudinal direction.
* Heat transfer between water and air above the ditch is **negligible** due to cover plates.
* Drainage ditches can be modeled as **equivalent circular pipes** using equal wetted perimeter and flow rate.

**Consequence**
Turbulence, partial flow, air entrainment, splashing, and intermittent flow conditions are neglected.

---

### 1.4 Freezing Criterion Assumptions

* Freezing is considered critical if water freezes within a **flow distance less than 20 m**, corresponding to drainage spacing requirements in design codes.
* Partial freezing that impedes flow is considered functionally equivalent to complete freezing.

**Consequence**
Hydraulic blockage is prioritized over thermodynamic completeness, biasing predictions toward conservative maintenance decisions.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### 2.1 Material and Interface Assumptions

* Thermal properties of concrete, insulation, and rock remain **constant with temperature** and unaffected by freeze-thaw cycling.
* No moisture migration, ice lens formation, or frost heave alters material properties or geometry.
* Insulation layers remain intact and continuous over the tunnel service life.

**Hidden risk**
Freeze-thaw damage can degrade insulation performance, increase thermal conductivity, and invalidate modeled temperature fields.

---

### 2.2 Measurement and Instrumentation Assumptions

* Temperature sensors mounted at 3 m height on the lining accurately represent **local air and boundary temperatures**.
* Sensor accuracy of ±0.5 °C is sufficient relative to the −1 °C freezing threshold.
* Sensor attachment does not introduce thermal bias due to conduction or radiation effects.

**Hidden risk**
Measurement uncertainty approaches the freezing criterion magnitude, potentially misclassifying marginal freezing zones.

---

### 2.3 Flow and Hydrology Assumptions

* Groundwater inflow temperature is spatially uniform and centered around 3 °C with sinusoidal fluctuation.
* Flow rate is externally specified and does not respond dynamically to partial freezing, clogging, or upstream pressure changes.
* Ice formation does not significantly alter hydraulic radius or wetted perimeter prior to freezing classification.

**Hidden risk**
Ice accretion reduces cross-sectional area, slows flow, increases residence time, and accelerates freezing in a nonlinear feedback loop.

---

### 2.4 Temporal Assumptions

* Six months of monitoring captures all relevant thermal regimes.
* Long-term trends such as climate warming or infrastructure aging are excluded.
* Short-term thermal diffusion effects are negligible compared to convection over engineering timescales.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### 3.1 Heat Conduction in Solids

**Law**
Fourier’s law:
q = −k ∇T

**Application**
Determines temperature gradients through lining, insulation, and surrounding rock.

**Validity regime**
Linear conduction with constant material properties.

**Limitations**
Cracking, moisture migration, and phase change in materials violate homogeneity assumptions.

---

### 3.2 Convective Heat Transfer

**Law**
q = h A (T_surface − T_fluid)

**Application**

* Water-wall heat exchange dominates due to large convective coefficient of water.
* Air-water convection is neglected due to low airflow under cover slabs.

**Validity regime**
Laminar flow with steady coefficients.

**Second-order effects**
Ice roughness increases effective h and local heat flux.

---

### 3.3 Energy Conservation in Flowing Water

**Law**
ρ c_p v A dT/dx = h p (T_wall − T_water)

**Application**
Forms the basis of the analytical water temperature evolution model.

**Limitations**

* Neglects axial thermal diffusion.
* Assumes steady-state conditions.

---

### 3.4 Phase Change Thermodynamics

**Law**
Latent heat release maintains temperature near 0 °C during freezing.

**Application**

* Explains temperature plateaus near freezing.
* Justifies conservative predictions when latent heat is neglected.

**Limits**

* Partial freezing causes hydraulic failure before full phase change.

---

### 3.5 Dimensionless Analysis

**Péclet number**
Pe = v L / α ≫ 1

**Application**

* Justifies neglect of thermal diffusion relative to convection.

**Boundary**

* Breaks down at very low velocities near stagnation or blockage.

---

## 4. Interdependencies

* **Temperature field ↔ drainage configuration**
  Burial depth and insulation thickness shift freezing fronts.
* **Flow velocity ↔ freezing distance**
  Lower velocity increases residence time and freezing risk.
* **Partial freezing ↔ hydraulic resistance**
  Ice formation slows flow, reinforcing freezing.
* **Portal climate ↔ longitudinal temperature gradient**
  Portal conditions dominate freezing risk zones.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Violated Assumption                | Resulting Physical Breakdown                     |
| ---------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------ |
| Laminar flow                       | Turbulent heat transfer alters freezing distance |
| Negligible air-water heat exchange | Underprediction of cooling rate                  |
| Constant material properties       | Insulation degradation shifts freezing zones     |
| Uniform flow velocity              | Local stagnation accelerates freezing            |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* Progressive clogging from partial ice formation.
* Insulation delamination or water ingress.
* Sensor drift leading to delayed mitigation.
* Overreliance on electric heat tracing with delayed activation.
* Localized freezing at joints, bends, or construction defects.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* Analytical tractability favored over full multiphysics realism.
* Conservative freezing criterion prioritizes safety over energy efficiency.
* Heat tracing assumed available and reliable.
* Maintenance access assumed feasible in predicted zones.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* No coupling to structural stress, frost heave, or lining cracking.
* No feedback between ice formation and tunnel ventilation.
* No interaction with traffic-induced vibration or airflow beyond thermal effects.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution

* Freezing risk migrates seasonally along the tunnel.
* Warming season presents higher freezing risk due to residual cold walls.
* Long-term degradation of insulation likely increases freezing extent over time.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

* Conservative freezing threshold of −1 °C.
* Conservative assumption that partial ice equals functional failure.
* Sensitivity analysis shows low dependence on ground temperature and convection coefficient.
* Single-point failure remains delayed heat tracing activation.

---

## 11. Design Regime vs Operational Reality

**Designed for**

* Predictable cold climates.
* Controlled drainage geometry.
* Scheduled monitoring and intervention.

**Operational reality**

* Highly variable inflow rates.
* Partial blockage and feedback effects.
* Aging materials and changing climate.

**Robustness**

* Trend predictions are physically sound.
* Absolute freezing distances are condition-dependent and should be updated with monitoring data.

## 1. Explicit assumptions

### 1.1 Engineering context assumptions

* Cold-region open-pit mining and blasting impose **dynamic disturbances** that are safety-critical.
* Saturation plus freezing meaningfully changes pore structure and therefore dynamic failure modes.
* Dolomite at Barun Iron Mine is representative of low-porosity slope rock in that mine.

### 1.2 Specimen assumptions

* Specimens are standard cylinders (50 mm diameter, 50 mm length) and represent bulk rock behavior.
* Visible defects are removed; specimens are treated as “intact” rock aside from intrinsic pores and microcracks.
* “Fully saturated” is defined operationally by a mass-stability criterion (successive mass difference < 0.02 g).

### 1.3 Thermal conditioning assumptions

* Holding at target temperature for 24 h yields a uniform internal thermal state and stable ice distribution.
* Transfer from the cold chamber to SHPB and the short test duration makes ambient warming negligible.

### 1.4 SHPB method assumptions

* One-dimensional elastic wave theory applies in the bars.
* Stress equilibrium in the specimen is achieved (validated via incident+reflected matching transmitted).
* The three-wave method produces valid stress, strain, strain-rate histories.
* “Representative strain rate” is the plateau average of the strain-rate curve.

### 1.5 Energy accounting assumptions

* Energy conservation across incident, reflected, transmitted components is valid for the system.
* Dissipated energy in the specimen is approximated as absorbed energy:

  * Fracture energy is about 95 percent of dissipated energy
  * Fragment kinetic energy is about 5 percent
* Energy dissipation density normalizes size effects adequately.

### 1.6 Fragmentation and fractal assumptions

* Post-impact fragment size distributions are self-similar and fractal over the sieve size range used.
* Fractal dimension can be derived from a linear fit of log cumulative mass fraction vs log size.

### 1.7 Constitutive interpretation assumptions

* The dynamic stress-strain curve can be segmented into four phases: linear elastic, nonlinear elastoplastic, plastic yielding, post-peak softening.
* Dynamic “elastic modulus” can be defined as slope from origin to the point at 50 percent of peak stress.

---

## 2. Hidden or implicit assumptions (the ones that can silently break the model)

### 2.1 “Saturation” is actually meaningful at very low porosity

They report saturated moisture content ω ≈ 0.583%. Hidden assumptions:

* This small water content is still enough to create an ice network that meaningfully alters stiffness, impedance, and crack mechanics.
* Saturation achieved by vacuum method fills the mechanically relevant pore network, not only larger connected pores.

Failure mode: If most water is in isolated pores, you can get local ice effects but not the strong global strengthening mechanisms they attribute to “ice bonding” and “crack filling.”

---

### 2.2 Thermal state uniformity inside the specimen

Freezing and ice distribution depend on pore size and connectivity. Implicitly assumed:

* 24 h is enough for the specimen to reach near-uniform temperature at the test temperature.
* The internal ice fraction is consistent across specimens.

Failure mode: Thermal gradients or partial freezing cause scatter in strength, modulus, and fragmentation trends, especially near −10 °C where some water types may remain unfrozen.

---

### 2.3 Ice formation does not introduce dominant pre-damage in the tested range

They argue no deterioration is observed from 25 °C to −40 °C given low water content. Implicitly:

* Ice expansion does not generate enough tensile stress to nucleate substantial new microcracking prior to impact.
* Any frost-induced microcracking is smaller than the strengthening from matrix shrinkage and ice support.

Failure mode: If ice-induced cracking occurs, you can see higher fragmentation and higher energy dissipation while strength could either rise or fall depending on which mechanism dominates.

---

### 2.4 Strain rate and loading history equivalence

They vary strain rate by changing impact pressure. Implicitly assumed:

* Changes in striker velocity only change strain rate, not other confounding factors like pulse shape, rise time, dispersion, or frictional heating at interfaces.

Failure mode: Differences in wave shape can change measured peak stress and apparent modulus even at the same “strain rate plateau.”

---

### 2.5 Interface conditions in SHPB are stable across temperature

Implicitly:

* Friction, end lubrication, and contact conditions between cold specimen and bars are consistent at 25 °C vs −40 °C.
* Thermal mismatch does not significantly alter contact stiffness or reflection behavior.

Failure mode: A colder specimen can change contact compliance and thus energy partitioning (reflected vs transmitted), which would contaminate energy-based interpretations.

---

### 2.6 Dynamic modulus definition represents material stiffness

They define dynamic elastic modulus using a secant to 50 percent peak stress. Implicitly:

* Early-time non-equilibrium and microcrack closure effects are negligible or consistent across conditions.
* The chosen point is within a regime where the specimen response is truly elastic-dominant.

Failure mode: For brittle materials under impact, early response can be strongly affected by wave dispersion, crack inertia, and rate-dependent crack friction, so “modulus” becomes an operational index, not a pure material property.

---

### 2.7 “Energy dissipation density” maps cleanly to damage

Implicitly:

* Most dissipated energy is indeed fracture creation, not other sinks like frictional sliding on microcracks, grain boundary debonding without new area, acoustic emission, or heat.

Failure mode: At lower temperatures, more brittle cleavage can create more new surface area per unit dissipation, changing the mapping between η and actual crack density.

---

### 2.8 Fractal dimension is a stable proxy for damage state

Implicitly:

* Fragmentation is produced by the same underlying breakage mechanism across all temperatures and strain rates.
* Sieving resolution is sufficient and fines losses are negligible.

Failure mode: If failure transitions from mixed-mode crushing to cleavage-dominant splitting, fractal fits can still look good (high R²) while representing a different mechanism entirely.

---

## 3. Physical principles and the regimes they require

### 3.1 Wave impedance controls energy partitioning

At the bar-specimen interface, reflection depends on acoustic impedance Z = ρ c.

* They interpret reduced reflected energy at lower temperature as higher specimen impedance due to densification and ice.

This requires:

* Wave speed and effective stiffness actually increase with freezing.
* The interface is well-coupled and does not add compliance.

---

### 3.2 Thermodynamics of freezing in pores

* Freezing point depression is pore-size dependent.
* Bound, capillary, adsorbed water freeze progressively with temperature.

This requires:

* Pore size distribution and mineral surface chemistry allow a meaningful unfrozen film and temperature-dependent adhesion.

Important hidden coupling: Unfrozen film can increase apparent cohesion and shear resistance, but also introduces rate-dependent frictional sliding depending on film thickness.

---

### 3.3 Fracture mechanics with “crack filling”

They claim ice filling reduces effective crack length and lowers Mode I SIF.
This requires:

* Ice provides sufficient tensile and shear transfer across crack faces.
* The ice interface does not debond under dynamic tensile components.
* Crack faces are geometrically compatible with bridging.

Failure mode: Under high strain rate, inertia and mixed-mode loading can cause interfacial debonding, turning “crack filling” from strengthening into a damage initiator.

---

### 3.4 Strain rate strengthening

They observe strong rate strengthening in strength and especially modulus.
This typically requires some combination of:

* Microcrack growth kinetics
* Inertia-limited crack propagation
* Rate-dependent frictional resistance on pre-existing microcracks

Freezing can change all three by altering microcrack friction (ice or water film), crack tip toughness, and elastic wave speed.

---

## 4. Where conclusions are robust vs fragile

### Robust trends (likely stable)

* Lower temperature increases brittleness indicators in SEM (more cleavage steps, river patterns).
* Higher strain rate increases fragmentation and energy input, producing finer fragments.
* Saturated at room temperature is weaker than dry at room temperature due to lubrication and reduced cementation.

### Fragile or condition-dependent claims

* Linear strength increase with decreasing temperature for saturated samples: can flip if ice-induced pre-cracking dominates in another dolomite with different pore network.
* Strong modulus increase (over 100 percent across strain-rate range): may reflect methodology and wave effects as much as intrinsic stiffness.
* Using a single “saturated moisture content” as a global descriptor: small shifts in pore connectivity can change freezing mechanics disproportionately.

---

## 5. Key unstated constraints and design assumptions (engineering translation)

### 5.1 Representative mine-scale inference

They implicitly assume lab intact dolomite behavior scales to:

* Jointed slope rock masses
* Blast-damaged rock
* Repeated freeze-thaw conditioning

That scaling is not guaranteed because joints dominate stiffness, strength, and fragmentation at field scale.

---

### 5.2 “24 h freeze then test” approximates field conditions

Mine slopes experience:

* Thermal cycling
* Partial saturation
* Freeze-thaw fatigue
* Stress confinement gradients

The experiment isolates temperature and water content but does not reproduce these coupled processes.

---

## 6. Most important hidden failure mode: competing effects of freezing

Freezing produces at least two opposing mechanisms:

**Strengthening mechanisms**

* Matrix thermal contraction increases contact stiffness
* Ice bonding and crack bridging reduce crack opening
* Increased impedance increases transmitted energy

**Weakening mechanisms**

* Ice expansion generates tensile microcracks
* Differential contraction between minerals creates residual stress
* Interfacial debonding between ice and matrix under dynamic loading

Their tested dolomite (very low ω) sits in a regime where strengthening dominates. A more porous dolomite or a more connected pore network can shift the balance.

---

## 7. What a careful engineer would treat as “model assumptions to verify”

If you were using these results to tune blast parameters in a cold mine, the assumptions you would want to validate first are:

* Actual pore network connectivity and water distribution at ω ≈ 0.6%
* Internal temperature uniformity during transfer to SHPB
* Contact friction and coupling changes with temperature
* Repeatability across more than three replicates for scatter-dominated properties like fragmentation

## 1. Explicit assumptions

### 1.1 Problem framing assumptions

* Accurate measurement of dynamic compressive strength (DCS) of frozen rocks **requires maintaining an ambient sub-zero temperature during impact loading**, not merely pre-freezing.
* Rapid cooling methods such as liquid nitrogen introduce **non-representative thermal shock damage**.
* Changes in unfrozen water content during specimen handling meaningfully alter dynamic mechanical response.

---

### 1.2 Material and specimen assumptions

* White lithic-quartz sandstone is representative of **porous frozen rocks** in cold-region engineering.
* Specimens prepared from the same block, with similar density, porosity, and P-wave velocity, are mechanically comparable.
* Vacuum saturation for 72 h achieves near-uniform saturation of the pore network.
* Heating dry specimens at 105 °C with a slow rate avoids thermally induced microcracking.

---

### 1.3 Thermal conditioning assumptions

* A cooling rate of **1 °C/min** is slow enough to:

  * avoid thermal shock
  * allow quasi-uniform internal temperature
  * minimize non-equilibrium freezing damage
* Holding at target temperature for **24 h** establishes a stable ice–water distribution.
* Maintaining specimens, bars, and chamber at the same temperature for 30 min prior to testing ensures negligible thermal gradients at interfaces.

---

### 1.4 SHPB and wave mechanics assumptions

* One-dimensional elastic wave theory applies to aluminum bars even with local temperature gradients.
* The pulse shaping technique generates stress waves suitable for achieving stress equilibrium in the specimen.
* Rubber pulse shapers and insulating plugs do not significantly distort stress wave propagation.
* The loading rate derived from the transmitted wave slope accurately characterizes specimen strain rate.

---

### 1.5 NMR interpretation assumptions

* T2 relaxation spectra reliably distinguish:

  * bulk water
  * capillary water
  * adsorbed water
* Changes in T2 distribution directly correspond to changes in unfrozen water content.
* The extrapolated paramagnetic regression line reasonably represents FIDsaturated below 0 °C.

---

### 1.6 Mechanical interpretation assumptions

* Dynamic compressive strength is adequately represented by peak stress in the stress–strain curve.
* Differences in DCS between conditions are primarily attributable to:

  * mineral shrinkage
  * pore ice strengthening
  * frost-induced damage
  * unfrozen water effects
* Other potential contributors, such as bar–specimen interface effects or wave dispersion, are secondary.

---

## 2. Hidden and implicit assumptions

These are not stated explicitly but are required for the logic to remain valid.

---

### 2.1 Temperature homogeneity inside the specimen

* Internal temperature gradients during freezing and testing are negligible.
* Ice formation is spatially uniform at a given nominal temperature.

**Risk**
In porous sandstones, freezing fronts propagate from the surface inward. Partial freezing or temperature gradients can cause heterogeneous stiffness and localized damage.

---

### 2.2 Ice morphology and connectivity

* Ice formed in pores acts as a **mechanically load-bearing phase**, not as isolated inclusions.
* Ice–mineral bonding strength is sufficient to transfer compressive and shear stress.

**Risk**
If ice debonds dynamically or fractures early, it may act as a flaw rather than reinforcement.

---

### 2.3 Minimal specimen damage during freezing

* The controlled cooling rate prevents the formation of dominant frost-induced microcracks.
* Any frost damage that does occur is repeatable and systematic across specimens.

**Risk**
Even slow freezing can induce microcracking in high-porosity sandstones. Such pre-damage can both increase ductility and reduce peak strength, confounding interpretations.

---

### 2.4 Stability of unfrozen water during testing

* Once stabilized in the environmental chamber, unfrozen water content remains constant throughout impact loading.
* Frictional heating, adiabatic compression, or rapid crack propagation do not induce local melting.

**Risk**
Dynamic loading can produce localized temperature rises and pressure-induced melting at crack tips.

---

### 2.5 Bar material behavior under temperature gradients

* Aluminum bar elastic modulus, wave speed, and damping are insensitive to the modest temperature gradients present.
* The bars remain linear elastic throughout testing.

**Risk**
Even small gradients can alter impedance and reflection coefficients if not symmetric.

---

### 2.6 Rate and temperature effects are separable

* The loading-rate dependence parameter α can be treated independently from temperature-dependent microstructural changes.
* The empirical fitting formula remains valid across the tested temperature–rate space.

**Risk**
Rate sensitivity itself can be temperature-dependent due to changes in crack viscosity, ice fracture behavior, and unfrozen water lubrication.

---

## 3. Governing first principles

### 3.1 Heat transfer and phase change

* Fourier heat conduction governs cooling and warming.
* Pore water freezing obeys Gibbs–Thomson relations, with freezing point depressed in small pores.
* Latent heat buffers temperature during phase transition.

**Validity regime**
Slow cooling, small specimens, no strong convection.

---

### 3.2 Thermo-mechanical deformation

* Mineral grains contract upon cooling.
* Ice expands approximately 9 percent on freezing.
* Differential strain induces internal stress fields.

**Emergent behavior**
Competition between matrix densification and frost-induced cracking.

---

### 3.3 Dynamic fracture mechanics

* Stress concentration at crack tips scales with effective crack length.
* Ice filling reduces effective crack opening and stress intensity.
* Under high loading rates, inertia and crack-tip kinetics dominate over subcritical growth.

---

### 3.4 Wave impedance and energy transfer

* Energy partitioning at the bar–specimen interface depends on impedance mismatch.
* Increased stiffness and density of frozen specimens increase transmitted energy.

---

### 3.5 Poromechanics

* Unfrozen water acts as a viscous, lubricating phase.
* Ice acts as a stiff, load-bearing phase.
* The balance depends strongly on pore size distribution.

---

## 4. Interdependencies

* **Cooling rate ↔ microcrack density**
  Faster cooling increases frost damage, reducing DCS even if ice strengthens locally.
* **Unfrozen water ↔ ductility ↔ post-peak response**
  Higher unfrozen water promotes strain softening and energy dissipation.
* **Temperature ↔ rate sensitivity**
  Increasing ice content reduces rate dependence by promoting brittle crack propagation.
* **Porosity ↔ freezing sequence ↔ DCS trend**
  Larger pores freeze earlier, dominating Stage I behavior.

---

## 5. Assumption–principle failure mapping

| Violated assumption          | Physical consequence                                        |
| ---------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------- |
| Uniform internal temperature | Mixed frozen and unfrozen zones distort stress distribution |
| Ice–matrix bonding integrity | Ice acts as flaw, not reinforcement                         |
| Negligible frost pre-damage  | Apparent ductility increases while strength drops           |
| Stable unfrozen water        | Local melting alters crack friction                         |
| Linear bar behavior          | Misinterpretation of transmitted stress                     |

---

## 6. Hidden failure modes

* Microcrack networks generated during freezing dominate dynamic failure.
* Ice debonding under rapid tensile components.
* Localized melting at crack tips during impact.
* Bar–specimen interface slip altered by temperature.
* Overfitting of empirical DCS formula outside tested range.

---

## 7. Unstated constraints and tradeoffs

* The system prioritizes **thermal fidelity** over experimental simplicity.
* Cooling rate chosen to minimize damage may not match field freezing rates.
* Porous sandstone behavior cannot be generalized to low-porosity rocks.
* The testing temperature range is constrained by ground temperature assumptions.

---

## 8. Boundary conditions and system context

* No confining pressure is applied.
* No freeze–thaw cycling is considered.
* Specimens are intact and joint-free.
* Dynamic loading is uniaxial compression only.

---

## 9. Temporal evolution assumptions

* Microstructure is static after 24 h freezing.
* No aging, creep, or sintering of ice occurs during holding.
* Repeated impacts or cyclic loading are not considered.

---

## 10. Safety and robustness margins

* Temperature control precision ±0.1 °C provides robustness against phase transition sensitivity.
* NMR verification explicitly bounds unfrozen water variation.
* Single-point failure remains frost-induced microdamage not directly observed prior to testing.

---

## 11. Design regime vs operational reality

**Designed regime**

* Controlled freezing
* Stable sub-zero ambient temperature
* Short-duration dynamic loading

**Operational reality**

* Thermal cycling
* Partial saturation
* Pre-existing joints and stress fields
* Repeated blasting loads

**Robustness**

* The system robustly isolates temperature effects.
* Extrapolation to field rock masses requires explicit consideration of damage accumulation and heterogeneity.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### Materials

* The sandstone is treated as a **representative porous, brittle geomaterial** with fixed mineralogy and porosity.
* Ice within fissures is assumed to behave as a **solid load-bearing phase** at −10 ℃.
* Ice and rock are assumed to form a **mechanically bonded interface** with finite cohesion and stiffness.
* Sandstone mechanical properties are assumed **time-invariant** over the test duration.

### Geometry

* A **single, straight, planar fissure** of fixed length (20 mm) and width (2 mm) is assumed sufficient to represent fractured rock behavior.
* Fissure inclination angle α is assumed to be the **dominant geometric control parameter**, with no interaction effects from fissure roughness or curvature.

### Environment

* Temperature is assumed **uniform and stable** throughout the specimen at −10 ℃ or 25 ℃.
* Phase change effects are assumed complete before loading, with **no ongoing freezing or melting** during compression.

### Loading

* Uniaxial compression at 0.05 mm/s is assumed to represent **quasi-static conditions**.
* End friction and platen effects are assumed negligible or consistent across tests.

### Measurement

* High-speed camera observations are assumed sufficient to capture **macroscopic crack initiation and propagation**.
* UCS and elastic modulus are assumed to fully characterize mechanical response.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### Manufacturing and Preparation

* Precut fissures are assumed to be **geometrically identical and repeatable**, ignoring machining-induced microdamage.
* Water injection into fissures is assumed to produce **fully saturated, void-free ice**, neglecting trapped air or partial bonding.

### Material Homogeneity

* Sandstone is implicitly treated as **homogeneous and isotropic**, despite known bedding-plane anisotropy.
* Ice is assumed isotropic and defect-free, ignoring grain boundary sliding, creep, or microcracking.

### Ice Physics

* Ice is assumed to remain in a **brittle–elastic regime** under applied stresses.
* Creep, pressure melting, and regelation are implicitly neglected.

### Time Effects

* No time-dependent degradation is assumed.
* Fatigue, subcritical crack growth, and stress corrosion cracking are ignored.

### Interface Behavior

* Ice–rock adhesion is assumed **rate-independent** and insensitive to loading history.
* Debonding is assumed to occur suddenly rather than progressively.

### Numerical Modeling

* PFC particle sizes and bonding laws are assumed sufficient to reproduce **fracture mechanics behavior**, despite being discrete and 2D.
* Trial-and-error calibration is assumed to uniquely map microparameters to macroresponse.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### 3.1 Continuum Mechanics

**Equilibrium:**
∇·σ + b = 0
Applies to stress redistribution around fissure tips and ice inclusions.

Validity:

* Linear elastic regime prior to cracking.
* Violated locally at crack tips where stress singularities form.

Second-order effects:

* Stress shadowing due to ice infill.
* Redistribution of tensile versus compressive zones.

---

### 3.2 Fracture Mechanics

**Stress Intensity Factor:**
K ∝ σ√(πa)

Application:

* Ice infill effectively reduces the **effective crack length a**, lowering K.
* Crack initiation depends on whether K exceeds fracture toughness.

Limits:

* Assumes sharp cracks and linear elastic fracture mechanics.
* Violated once large-scale plasticity or crushing of ice occurs.

Emergent behavior:

* Transition from tensile to shear cracking with angle.
* Crack initiation away from fissure tips in frozen cases.

---

### 3.3 Contact and Interface Mechanics

**Coulomb friction and cohesion:**
τ = c + σₙ tanφ

Application:

* Governs ice–rock debonding and shear failure at higher fissure angles.
* Determines when shear cracks dominate.

Limits:

* Assumes constant c and φ.
* Ignores temperature-dependent frictional softening.

---

### 3.4 Thermodynamics of Phase Change

* Ice strength and stiffness increase with decreasing temperature.
* Presence of unfrozen water films enables stress transmission.

Implicit assumption:

* Thermal equilibrium with no latent heat effects during loading.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Thermal state ↔ fracture mechanics**
  Lower temperature increases ice stiffness, reducing K and delaying crack initiation.

* **Interface mechanics ↔ stress redistribution**
  Strong ice–rock bonding alters force chains and shifts tensile stress zones.

* **Geometry ↔ dominant failure mode**
  Fissure angle controls whether tensile or shear criteria govern failure.

* **Discrete modeling ↔ continuum assumptions**
  PFC captures force chains but approximates continuum fracture behavior.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Assumption Violated      | Consequence                                         |
| ------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------- |
| Ice remains brittle      | Creep would relax stresses and reduce strengthening |
| Perfect ice–rock bonding | Early debonding eliminates binder mechanism         |
| Uniform temperature      | Local melting weakens support and adhesion          |
| Homogeneous sandstone    | Crack paths would preferentially follow weak layers |
| Quasi-static loading     | Rate effects could change cracking mode             |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* **Ice crushing before peak load**, eliminating support abruptly.
* **Interface peeling or mixed-mode debonding** not captured in 2D.
* **Thermal cracking** due to differential expansion during freezing.
* **Out-of-plane fracture propagation** ignored in 2D PFC models.
* **Scale effects**, where laboratory fissure sizes do not extrapolate to field rock masses.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* Choice of −10 ℃ implies a regime where ice is strong but unfrozen water still exists.
* Single-fissure geometry optimizes interpretability, not realism.
* 2D PFC favors visualization over accurate 3D stress states.
* UCS focus ignores post-peak energy dissipation and brittleness.

Not optimized for:

* Cyclic freeze–thaw.
* Long-term slope stability.
* Dynamic or seismic loading.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Platens assumed rigid and frictionless.
* No lateral confinement, despite field relevance.
* No coupling to thermal gradients or groundwater flow.
* No interaction with adjacent fractures or joint networks.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* No fatigue accumulation.
* No progressive ice damage.
* No change in interface properties with repeated loading.
* Crack initiation treated as instantaneous threshold event.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

* Implicit safety factor comes from ice strengthening.
* Single-point failure: loss of ice integrity collapses all three strengthening mechanisms.
* No redundancy once fissure ice is crushed or debonded.

---

## 11. Operative vs Design Regime

### Design Regime

* Fully frozen, static, intact ice–rock bonding.
* Single dominant fissure.
* Short-term loading.

### Likely Operative Regime

* Temperature cycling.
* Partial melting.
* Multiple interacting fractures.
* Time-dependent degradation.

Robustness:

* Strong for controlled laboratory conditions.
* Fragile under environmental variability or long-term service conditions.
