## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Vessel motion modeling

* Vessel motion along the acoustic propagation direction can be approximated as **uniform acceleration** within the pulse duration.
* Uniform speed motion is treated as a special case of zero acceleration.
* Rotational motion of array elements contributes negligibly to Doppler-induced sounding errors and is therefore ignored in Doppler analysis.

**Consequence**
The Doppler model includes first-order velocity and second-order acceleration terms but neglects higher-order jerk. This constrains validity to pulse durations short relative to the heave period and excludes impulsive motion events.

---

### 1.2 Acoustic signal model

* The transmitted signal is a **linear frequency-modulated (LFM) pulse** with rectangular envelope.
* Doppler effect modifies **carrier frequency only**, while the envelope shape is assumed unchanged.
* Long pulses are treated as a sequence of infinitesimal short pulses, each experiencing a different instantaneous Doppler shift.

**Consequence**
Assumes narrowband Doppler behavior relative to bandwidth. Envelope distortion due to severe mismatch or nonstationary scattering is excluded.

---

### 1.3 Propagation environment

* Sound speed during transmission and reception is treated as **piecewise constant**.
* Over short two-way travel distances, ray bending is ignored and straight-line propagation is assumed.
* Rotational Doppler and shear-induced frequency spreading are neglected.

**Consequence**
Sound-speed gradient effects are decoupled from Doppler correction and deferred to homing calculations. Strong thermoclines or internal waves violate this separation.

---

### 1.4 Signal processing architecture

* Beamforming is performed prior to pulse compression.
* Bottom detection provides time of arrival (TOA) and direction of arrival (DOA), which are then corrected.
* Doppler correction is applied **post-estimation** via time shift and angle compensation.

**Consequence**
Assumes that Doppler does not prevent reliable bottom detection. Severe sidelobe growth or SNR loss could invalidate TOA and DOA estimates before correction.

---

### 1.5 Validation scope

* Validation relies on simulation, semi-physical simulation, and sea trials under moderate to rough sea states.
* Long-pulse data under extreme sea states are synthetically generated.

**Consequence**
Empirical validation of worst-case nonlinear hydrodynamic–acoustic coupling is incomplete.

---

## 2. HIDDEN / IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Motion smoothness

* Vessel heave is assumed sinusoidal and differentiable.
* Acceleration is assumed approximately constant over the pulse width.

**Evidence**
Taylor expansion of heave velocity and truncation at second order.

**Risk**
Slamming or wave-impact events introduce jerk terms that invalidate uniform acceleration modeling.

---

### 2.2 Sensor fidelity

* INS, GNSS, and sound-velocity measurements are assumed accurate enough that interpolation errors are negligible.
* Timing synchronization across subsystems is assumed perfect.

**Risk**
Small timing biases directly translate into depth bias at long ranges.

---

### 2.3 Array behavior

* Array geometry is rigid and invariant during the pulse.
* Element spacing and phase centers are assumed fixed.

**Risk**
Structural vibration or thermal expansion introduces phase errors that Doppler correction cannot remove.

---

### 2.4 Scattering assumptions

* Seabed scattering amplitudes follow Rayleigh statistics.
* Phase is uniformly distributed.

**Risk**
Specular or anisotropic seabed responses alter envelope peak behavior and phase detection reliability.

---

### 2.5 Noise characteristics

* Noise is stationary and additive.
* Doppler-induced error dominates other error sources during analysis.

**Risk**
Bubble clouds, cavitation, or flow noise may dominate error budgets in severe seas.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Doppler effect

**Law**
Observed frequency shift proportional to relative radial velocity:
[
f_D = \frac{v_r}{c} f_c
]

**Application**
Extended to time-varying velocity via uniform acceleration.

**Validity regime**
Low Mach number, small fractional bandwidth.

**Boundary**
High accelerations introduce frequency chirp distortion beyond linear correction.

---

### 3.2 Kinematics

**Law**
Position and velocity under uniform acceleration:
[
v(t) = v_0 + at
]

**Application**
Maps emission and reception times to instantaneous Doppler offsets.

**Limit**
Neglects jerk and higher-order vessel dynamics.

---

### 3.3 Matched filtering

**Law**
Correlation maximizes SNR when received signal matches transmitted waveform.

**Application**
Doppler mismatch shifts envelope peak and degrades main-to-sidelobe ratio.

**Second-order effects**

* Envelope peak delay scales with Doppler frequency slope.
* Phase distortion affects DOA estimation.

---

### 3.4 Beamforming

**Law**
Constructive interference occurs when phase delays match steering geometry.

**Application**
Incorrect sound speed or Doppler-shifted carrier causes beam pointing bias.

**Limit**
Assumes plane-wave approximation and narrowband steering.

---

### 3.5 Error propagation

**Law**
Depth error scales linearly with TOA error:
[
\Delta z = \frac{c}{2}\Delta t
]

**Application**
Explains amplification of small Doppler delays into meter-scale depth errors at great depth.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Vessel kinematics** directly modulate Doppler frequency.
* **Doppler-induced frequency shift** alters matched filter output timing.
* **Timing bias** propagates into both depth and steering angle.
* **Beam angle error** further modifies effective radial velocity, feeding back into Doppler estimation.

This closed-loop interaction explains why post-estimation correction must address both TOA and DOA.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption  | Principle impacted | Outcome                |
| -------------------- | ------------------ | ---------------------- |
| Uniform acceleration | Kinematics         | Residual timing bias   |
| Constant sound speed | Beamforming        | Angle mis-steering     |
| Envelope invariance  | Matched filter     | TOA bias               |
| Negligible rotation  | Doppler law        | Phase distortion       |
| Accurate INS data    | Error propagation  | Systematic depth drift |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Pulse-length resonance** where acceleration changes sign within the pulse.
* **Sidelobe-driven false bottom detection** under Doppler mismatch.
* **Angle-dependent bias** leading to ripple-like artifacts in swath maps.
* **Depth-dependent amplification** of small timing errors in deep water.
* **INS dropouts** causing correction instability.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS & TRADEOFFS

### Algorithmic

* Post-estimation correction prioritizes **low computational load** over waveform-level optimality.
* Frequency-domain correction is avoided due to sensitivity to attitude errors.

### Hardware

* LFM is favored over HFM despite higher Doppler sensitivity, due to ADC and processing limits.

### What is not optimized

* Robustness to impulsive motion.
* Adaptive handling of mixed acceleration regimes within a single pulse.
* Joint correction of Doppler and sound-speed gradient effects.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS & SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Method assumes compatibility with existing MBES processing pipelines.
* Homing calculation is treated as downstream and independent.
* No coupling to real-time motion prediction or control.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Motion state is treated as deterministic within a pulse.
* No accumulation of correction error across pings.
* Long-term drift in sensors or calibration is not modeled.

**Consequence**
Short-term accuracy improves, but long-term consistency depends on external calibration.

---

## 10. SAFETY & ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Uniform acceleration assumption introduces a **bounded modeling error** rather than unbounded bias.
* Post-estimation correction allows reprocessing if improved motion data become available.
* Single-point failures include:

  * Incorrect acceleration estimation
  * Incorrect sound velocity at surface
  * Bottom detection failure prior to correction

Graceful degradation is limited but improved relative to uniform speed methods.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Long LFM pulses
* Deep water
* Moderate to rough seas
* Smooth vessel motion

### Operative extremes

* Breaking seas
* Slamming-induced jerk
* Rapid speed changes
* Strong sound-speed gradients

### Robustness assessment

The uniform acceleration framework is a **physically justified intermediate-order model**. It significantly extends validity beyond uniform speed assumptions while remaining computationally tractable, but it will fail in jerk-dominated motion regimes.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Environmental loading

* The marine environment is represented using **irregular wave theory** derived from a **JONSWAP spectrum**.
* Wave loading is assumed **unidirectional**, aligned with the global x-axis.
* The governing extreme condition corresponds to **100-year return period** waves, wind, and current conditions at a site in the Persian Gulf.
* The dynamic response is dominated by **wave loading**, while wind effects are treated quasi-statically.

**Consequence**
Directional spreading, wave–wave interaction, and multi-directional loading are excluded. This concentrates demand into a single load path and systematically biases failure toward one-sided mechanisms.

---

### 1.2 Wave kinematics and hydrodynamics

* Irregular waves are generated as a **linear superposition of 1000 Airy wave components** with random phases.
* Water particle kinematics are computed using **linear Airy wave theory**, extrapolated into the splash zone.
* Hydrodynamic loading on structural members is governed by the **Morison equation**, with constant coefficients:

  * Drag coefficient Cd = 1.1
  * Inertia coefficient Cm = 1.2
* Members satisfy the **slender-body assumption** with wavelength-to-diameter ratio greater than 5.

**Consequence**
Wave nonlinearity, crest asymmetry, breaking, and slamming loads are not modeled. Hydrodynamic coefficients are assumed invariant under Reynolds number changes and flow separation.

---

### 1.3 Structural modeling

* Only **primary structural members** of the jacket are explicitly modeled.
* Structural elements are represented using **force-based nonlinear beam-column elements** with fiber discretization.
* Plane sections are assumed to remain plane and perpendicular to the element axis.
* Local shell buckling of tubular members is not captured.

**Consequence**
Failure is governed by global plasticity and brace buckling, not local wall instability. This biases collapse toward ductile global modes.

---

### 1.4 Material behavior

* Steel behavior follows the **Menegotto–Pinto constitutive law**, capturing cyclic plasticity and the Bauschinger effect.
* Grout is modeled as unconfined concrete with simplified nonlinear behavior.
* Fatigue damage is accumulated using **Miner’s Rule** with Coffin–Manson parameters taken from laboratory tests.

**Consequence**
Material degradation is cumulative and scalar. Load sequence effects, crack closure, and corrosion-fatigue coupling are excluded.

---

### 1.5 Foundation modeling

* Soil–pile–structure interaction is modeled using a **Beam on Nonlinear Winkler Foundation (BNWF)** approach.
* Soil response is represented using:

  * PySimple1 for lateral behavior
  * TzSimple1 for shaft friction
  * QzSimple1 for end bearing
* Soil properties are depth-dependent but time-invariant.

**Consequence**
Continuum soil behavior, pore pressure generation, cyclic degradation, and soil liquefaction are not explicitly modeled.

---

### 1.6 Analysis procedure

* Dynamic analysis is limited to a **600-s window** centered on the maximum surface elevation of a 3-hour sea state.
* Significant wave height is increased incrementally in 1-m steps until collapse.
* Structural collapse is identified by loss of load-carrying capacity.

**Consequence**
Rare cumulative damage outside the selected window is excluded. Collapse is defined structurally, not functionally.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Wave memory and stationarity

* The selected 600-s window is assumed to capture the governing extreme demand.
* Structural “memory” effects are assumed bounded within this window.

**Evidence**
Peak response is associated with the highest wave crest, with prior waves acting as preload.

**Risk**
Earlier damaging cycles outside the window may govern fatigue and stiffness degradation.

---

### 2.2 Hydrodynamic uniformity

* Marine growth thickness is constant along member length.
* Cd and Cm are independent of flow regime, Keulegan–Carpenter number, and vibration amplitude.

**Risk**
Member-level force prediction error increases under large oscillations and partial submergence.

---

### 2.3 Soil behavior

* Soil properties are assumed homogeneous within each defined layer.
* No strain-rate dependency or cyclic softening is included.

**Risk**
Extreme cyclic loading may reduce lateral and axial resistance faster than predicted.

---

### 2.4 Structural redundancy

* Jacket redundancy is assumed sufficient to allow load redistribution after brace failure.
* Connection failure is not modeled explicitly.

**Risk**
Joint fracture or weld failure could create brittle collapse paths absent from the model.

---

### 2.5 Damping representation

* Global damping is modeled as **2 percent Rayleigh damping**.
* Radiation damping from soil and fluid is embedded implicitly in dashpots.

**Risk**
Frequency-dependent damping behavior and amplitude-dependent dissipation are not captured.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Conservation of linear momentum

**Law**
[
\sum F = m \ddot{x}
]

**Application**
Governs global platform dynamics under combined wave, wind, and inertia forces.

**Validity regime**
Large displacement, nonlinear stiffness, but classical continuum mechanics.

**Boundary**
Large rotations and member fracture challenge the assumed kinematic compatibility.

---

### 3.2 Linear wave theory

**Law**
Surface elevation and particle kinematics derived from harmonic wave components.

**Application**
Used to generate irregular wave elevation, velocity, and acceleration time histories.

**Limit**
Breaks down for steep, breaking, or strongly nonlinear waves.

---

### 3.3 Morison hydrodynamic loading

**Law**
Force equals drag plus inertia based on relative motion between fluid and structure.

**Application**
Primary mechanism for wave–structure interaction on slender members.

**Second-order effects**

* Relative motion coupling introduces added damping and inertia feedback.
* Force nonlinearity induces higher harmonics in response.

---

### 3.4 Plasticity and stability

**Law**
Yielding occurs when stress exceeds yield surface defined by material constitutive law.

**Application**
Predicts formation of plastic hinges, brace buckling, and post-buckling redistribution.

**Limit**
Local buckling and fracture mechanics are outside the formulation.

---

### 3.5 Fatigue damage accumulation

**Law**
Miner’s linear damage rule:
[
\sum \frac{n_i}{N_i} = 1
]

**Application**
Tracks low-cycle fatigue damage in braces.

**Limit**
Nonlinear damage interaction and load sequence effects are ignored.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Wave kinematics** determine Morison forces.
* **Foundation stiffness** alters natural periods and dynamic amplification.
* **Plastic hinge formation** redistributes forces into redundant jacket members.
* **Soil strength** shifts failure from foundation-dominated to jacket-dominated modes.
* **Fatigue degradation** accelerates global instability once plastic mechanisms initiate.

These interactions produce **path-dependent collapse**, where failure location depends on the balance between foundation and superstructure capacity.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption   | Governing principle   | Consequence                 |
| --------------------- | --------------------- | --------------------------- |
| Linear wave theory    | Fluid kinematics      | Underestimated crest forces |
| Constant Cd, Cm       | Momentum balance      | Force misprediction         |
| No soil degradation   | Soil mechanics        | Overestimated stiffness     |
| Plane sections        | Structural kinematics | Local buckling missed       |
| Linear fatigue damage | Damage mechanics      | Life misestimation          |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* Premature **pile head fracture** due to combined bending and axial load.
* **Brace low-cycle fatigue rupture** preceding global instability.
* **Soil spring softening** leading to sudden period elongation.
* **One-sided ratcheting** from asymmetric geometry and unidirectional loading.
* **Progressive collapse** triggered by loss of a single critical brace.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Geometry

* Increasing pile diameter constrained by minimum annular gap requirements.
* Jacket geometry favors ductile brace failure over brittle pile rupture.

### Materials

* Yield stress increase chosen over thicker sections due to constructability and weight.
* Corrosion allowance not explicitly optimized.

### Not optimized for

* Multidirectional sea states.
* Long-term corrosion-fatigue interaction.
* Post-collapse survivability or reparability.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Platform assumed fixed in plan with no global sliding or overturning.
* Interaction with topside process equipment modeled only through lumped masses.
* Electrical, operational, and human factors are outside system boundaries.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Structural stiffness degrades via plasticity and fatigue.
* Soil properties remain constant over time.
* Hydrodynamic environment is stationary within each analysis.

**Consequence**
Time-dependent degradation mechanisms are only partially captured, primarily through structural fatigue.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Reserve Strength Ratio (RSR) provides an implicit safety margin.
* Stronger soil and pile sections increase redundancy and ductility.
* Single-point failures include:

  * Pile head plastic hinge
  * First-story brace rupture
  * Soil lateral capacity loss

Graceful degradation is observed only when failure shifts into the jacket rather than the foundation.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Linear to mildly nonlinear response
* Moderate irregular waves
* Balanced foundation and jacket strength

### Operative extreme regime

* Strongly nonlinear response
* Irregular extreme waves with memory effects
* Progressive damage accumulation

### Robustness assessment

The IIWA framework is **physically consistent and superior to pushover methods** for capturing collapse mechanisms under irregular waves. However, robustness depends critically on soil strength assumptions and directional loading simplifications.


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Flow regime and environment

* The surrounding fluid is **incompressible water** with constant density and viscosity.
* Reynolds numbers fall within **8.0 × 10⁴ to 3.5 × 10⁵**, placing the flow in a **transitional to turbulent regime**.
* Free-surface effects are neglected. The robot operates sufficiently deep to avoid wave–surface coupling.
* The ambient flow is uniform at the inlet, with no background turbulence or stratification.

**Consequence**
Density stratification, salinity gradients, thermoclines, and surface-wave interactions are excluded. Hydrodynamic performance is therefore representative of mid-water, benign conditions rather than coastal surf or thermally layered environments.

---

### 1.2 Motion and kinematics

* Robot motion is prescribed as **pure surge or pure sway**, without coupled pitch, roll, or yaw.
* Acceleration is uniform during startup and transitions smoothly into steady motion.
* Structural deformation is neglected; the robot is treated as **rigid**.

**Consequence**
Fluid–structure interaction is one-way. Any feedback from hydrodynamic loading into structural vibration, thruster misalignment, or control instability is excluded.

---

### 1.3 Turbulence modeling

* Turbulence closure uses the **SST k–ω model**, blending near-wall k–ω and free-stream k–ε behavior.
* Wall-resolved simulations are assumed valid with **y⁺ < 1**.
* Second-order spatial and temporal discretization are sufficient to capture unsteady vortical structures.

**Consequence**
Large-scale vortex shedding and boundary-layer separation are captured, but fine-scale coherent turbulence, intermittent transition, and vortex pairing phenomena remain modeled rather than resolved.

---

### 1.4 Geometry and porosity definition

* The open-frame geometry is parameterized by:

  * A **frame ratio λ = L₁ / (L₁ + L₂)**
  * A **sidewall porosity k**, defined by area ratio
  * A **blockage ratio β**, defined via internal obstacles
* Sidewall ribs are rigid, fixed, and evenly distributed.
* Perforations are idealized as sharp-edged openings with no fillets.

**Consequence**
Manufacturing tolerances, rounded edges, weld beads, and surface roughness are not explicitly represented, yet they strongly influence separation and vortex strength at this Reynolds number.

---

### 1.5 Performance metrics

* Hydrodynamic performance is assessed using:

  * Lift-to-drag ratio
  * Vorticity magnitude
  * Turbulent kinetic energy
  * Wake length
* Structural performance is inferred indirectly from flow stability, not from stress or fatigue calculations.

**Consequence**
Hydrodynamic optimization is decoupled from structural durability. A configuration that reduces drag may still be vulnerable to fatigue or impact damage.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Scale independence

* Results obtained on a **0.45 m scale robot** are implicitly assumed scalable to similar ROVs.
* Reynolds number similarity is assumed sufficient for dynamic similarity.

**Evidence**
Design guidelines are stated broadly for “small- and medium-scale ROVs”.

**Risk**
Geometric scaling alters relative rib thickness, perforation edge radius, and structural stiffness, breaking strict Reynolds similarity and changing vortex behavior.

---

### 2.2 Structural sufficiency

* Sidewall ribs and baffles are assumed strong enough to resist:

  * Vortex-induced vibration
  * Thruster-induced pressure fluctuations
* No resonance between vortex shedding frequency and structural natural frequencies is considered.

**Risk**
Secondary vortices identified as beneficial for hydrodynamics may become excitation sources for fatigue or acoustic noise.

---

### 2.3 Environmental confinement

* Tank experiments assume wall effects are secondary and correctable.
* Near-wall amplification of turbulence is treated as an experimental artifact rather than an operational condition.

**Risk**
Real missions in pipes, tanks, ship hull gaps, or seabed trenches closely resemble confined conditions where blockage-induced amplification dominates.

---

### 2.4 Control system neutrality

* Thrusters are modeled only as velocity boundary conditions.
* Control feedback, thrust vectoring errors, and actuator dynamics are ignored.

**Risk**
Hydrodynamic asymmetry induced by porosity gradients may interact with control loops, causing oscillatory or biased motion.

---

### 2.5 Long-term operation

* No biofouling, sediment accumulation, or clogging of perforations is considered.
* Porosity and blockage ratios are assumed invariant over the robot’s service life.

**Risk**
Even partial clogging shifts the system toward the high-β regime where performance degrades rapidly.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Conservation of mass

**Law**
[
\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u} = 0
]

**Application**
Governs redistribution of flow through perforations and internal cavities.

**Limit**
Local acceleration through pores increases shear and may induce unmodeled micro-separation.

---

### 3.2 Conservation of momentum

**Law**
[
\rho \left( \frac{\partial \mathbf{u}}{\partial t} + \mathbf{u}\cdot\nabla \mathbf{u} \right)
= -\nabla p + \mu \nabla^2 \mathbf{u}
]

**Application**
Explains drag growth, wake formation, and pressure gradients across baffles.

**Second-order effects**

* Porosity redistributes pressure gradients spatially.
* Blockage modifies effective inertia of internal flow.

---

### 3.3 Boundary-layer theory

**Law**
Wall shear stress scales with near-wall velocity gradient.

**Application**
Justifies the importance of y⁺ < 1 and prismatic layers.

**Limit**
Rib edges introduce three-dimensional separation not captured by classical boundary-layer assumptions.

---

### 3.4 Vortex dynamics

**Law**
Vorticity transport equation governs generation, stretching, and diffusion.

**Application**
Explains formation of secondary vortices induced by ribs and perforations.

**Emergent behavior**
Intermediate blockage stabilizes vortex cores by balancing stretching and diffusion.

---

### 3.5 Turbulence energy cascade

**Law**
Energy injected at large scales cascades to smaller scales and dissipates.

**Application**
Used to interpret TKE growth with increasing velocity and blockage.

**Boundary**
SST model assumes equilibrium turbulence, which may not hold during startup or rapid maneuvers.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Porosity alters continuity**, redistributing momentum internally.
* **Blockage modifies pressure gradients**, influencing vortex stretching.
* **Vortex stabilization reduces TKE**, lowering drag.
* **Velocity increase raises Reynolds number**, accelerating transition and amplifying separation.

These coupled mechanisms explain why an intermediate porosity and blockage ratio yields optimal performance.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated  | Principle impacted  | Consequence           |
| -------------------- | ------------------- | --------------------- |
| Rigid structure      | Momentum balance    | Unmodeled FSI         |
| Constant porosity    | Continuity          | Sudden flow choking   |
| SST adequacy         | Turbulence cascade  | Vortex misprediction  |
| Deep-water operation | Boundary conditions | Free-surface coupling |
| Clean openings       | Vorticity transport | Excess dissipation    |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE AND DEGRADATION MODES

* **Rib-induced fatigue** from persistent secondary vortices.
* **Acoustic noise amplification** due to coherent vortex shedding.
* **Porosity clogging** shifting β toward unstable regimes.
* **Control-flow coupling**, causing oscillatory sway during precision tasks.
* **Near-wall amplification** in confined environments leading to drag spikes.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Geometry

* Frame ratio constrained by internal equipment volume.
* Rib thickness chosen for stiffness, not hydrodynamic optimality.

### Hydrodynamics vs structure

* Higher porosity lowers drag but reduces impact resistance.
* Lower blockage improves efficiency but weakens internal flow control.

### Not optimized for

* High-speed transit.
* Impact with debris or seabed.
* Long-term fouling resistance.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Thrusters modeled implicitly, not as momentum sources.
* No coupling with onboard sensors, manipulators, or cables.
* External currents are steady and aligned with motion.

**Implication**
Real missions with cross-currents, tether drag, or tool deployment may experience different flow regimes.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Startup transients show higher vorticity than steady operation.
* Flow field exhibits memory effects during acceleration.
* No progressive structural or hydrodynamic degradation modeled.

**Consequence**
Short-term stability is captured; long-term reliability is inferred, not proven.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Operating below **0.4 m/s** implicitly provides a hydrodynamic safety margin.
* Intermediate blockage ratio offers robustness against both vortex diffusion and excessive confinement.
* Single-point vulnerabilities include:

  * Rib failure
  * Porosity obstruction
  * Thruster asymmetry

Graceful degradation is limited if porosity is compromised.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Low-speed, precision operations
* Moderate Reynolds numbers
* Clean, open water

### Likely operative extremes

* Confined spaces
* Partial clogging
* Cross-flow and unsteady currents

### Robustness assessment

The optimized configuration is **efficient but condition-sensitive**. Performance depends critically on maintaining porosity and operating within the intended velocity envelope.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Operating environment and flow physics

* Seawater is modeled as an **incompressible, Newtonian fluid** with constant properties.
* Flow is **isothermal**, with no thermal gradients or heat transfer.
* Turbulence is represented using **URANS with SST k–ω closure**, implying time-averaged turbulence statistics.
* Flow velocities span **0.11–0.42 m/s (model scale)**, corresponding to **Fr ≈ 0.16** and **Re ≈ 1.19 × 10⁵**.
* Cavitation is inferred from TSR thresholds but **not explicitly modeled** in the CFD.

**Consequence**
Hydrodynamic loading excludes temperature-dependent viscosity changes, density stratification, cavitation erosion dynamics, and intermittent turbulence bursts. Pressure pulsations represent averaged turbulence rather than extreme instantaneous peaks.

---

### 1.2 Structural modeling

* Blades, hub, and shaft are modeled as **linearly elastic solids** made of **6061 aluminum alloy**.
* The blade behaves as a **cantilever beam**, fixed at the hub.
* Static strength is evaluated using a **global safety factor of 2.0** relative to yield strength.
* Structural damping is not explicitly modeled.

**Consequence**
Plasticity, residual stresses from manufacturing, and stiffness degradation due to micro-cracking are excluded. Crack growth occurs entirely in the elastic fracture mechanics regime.

---

### 1.3 Fluid–structure interaction

* FSI is **one-way coupled**.
* Hydrodynamic pressures from CFD are mapped onto the structural model using nearest-neighbor interpolation.
* Structural deformation does not feed back into the fluid solution.

**Consequence**
Load redistribution due to blade bending or crack-induced compliance changes is neglected. Stress intensity factors are therefore evaluated under fixed load spectra.

---

### 1.4 Fatigue and fracture mechanics

* Crack initiation sites are **pre-defined** at the blade trailing edge.
* Crack growth is governed by the **Walker equation**, accounting for stress ratio effects.
* Load history dependence, overload retardation, and frequency effects are neglected.
* A finite number of cycles, **2 × 10⁴ to 4 × 10⁴**, is treated as representative of damage evolution.

**Consequence**
Fatigue behavior is modeled as deterministic and monotonic. Realistic tidal variability, storm loading, and sequence effects are not represented.

---

### 1.5 Threshold definition

* A **critical crack ratio a/b = 0.05** is identified as the fatigue threshold.
* Below this ratio, cracks are assumed to be **self-arresting**.

**Consequence**
The threshold is geometry-specific and implicitly material-specific. It is treated as universal for the blade, regardless of local stress gradients or environmental effects.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Scale transferability

* Model-scale hydrodynamic pressures are assumed transferable to full-scale blades via Fr and Re similarity.
* Crack growth behavior in laboratory aluminum specimens is assumed applicable to real turbine blades.

**Risk**
Full-scale blades experience higher absolute stresses, longer exposure times, corrosion-fatigue interaction, and variable amplitude loading, potentially lowering the effective threshold.

---

### 2.2 Manufacturing quality

* Initial cracks are idealized as smooth, planar defects with known geometry.
* No porosity, inclusions, weld defects, or residual stresses are included.

**Risk**
Real manufacturing defects produce mixed-mode stress intensity factors and accelerate early crack growth beyond XFEM predictions.

---

### 2.3 Environmental degradation

* No corrosion, hydrogen embrittlement, or biofouling is modeled.
* Material properties remain constant over the fatigue life.

**Risk**
Marine corrosion reduces crack closure effects and increases FCGR, effectively shifting the critical crack ratio downward.

---

### 2.4 Load stationarity

* Pressure pulsation spectra extracted at CH3 are assumed statistically stationary.
* The selected monitoring point governs global fatigue behavior.

**Risk**
Localized turbulent bursts, blade-to-blade interaction, and wake-induced asymmetry may introduce higher stress cycles elsewhere.

---

### 2.5 Structural redundancy

* Blade failure is assumed to occur only through progressive crack growth.
* Sudden brittle fracture, hub-interface failure, or shaft-induced overloads are excluded.

**Risk**
Multi-axial stress states at the blade root may trigger failure modes not captured by trailing-edge crack analysis.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Conservation of momentum (fluid)

[
\rho \left( \frac{\partial \mathbf{u}}{\partial t} + \mathbf{u}\cdot\nabla \mathbf{u} \right)
= -\nabla p + \mu \nabla^2 \mathbf{u}
]

**Application**
Determines pressure distributions and pulsations driving cyclic blade loading.

**Validity regime**
URANS-averaged turbulence. Instantaneous coherent structures are filtered.

---

### 3.2 Structural equilibrium

[
\mathbf{K}\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{F}*{\text{hydro}} + \mathbf{F}*{\text{centrifugal}} + \mathbf{F}_{\text{gravity}}
]

**Application**
Explains stress concentration at the blade-root interface and maximum tip displacement.

**Limit**
Nonlinear stiffness changes due to cracking are ignored until significant crack growth occurs.

---

### 3.3 Linear elastic fracture mechanics

[
K = Y \sigma \sqrt{\pi a}
]

**Application**
Stress intensity factor governs crack initiation and early propagation.

**Limit**
Valid only when plastic zone size is small relative to crack length and thickness.

---

### 3.4 Fatigue crack growth law (Walker)

[
\frac{da}{dN} = C \left( \Delta K \right)^m (1 - R)^\gamma
]

**Application**
Captures stress ratio sensitivity of crack growth.

**Boundary**
Does not include crack closure evolution, load interaction, or frequency effects.

---

### 3.5 Similarity laws

* **Froude similarity** preserves gravity-inertia balance.
* **Reynolds similarity** preserves viscous-inertial balance.

**Limit**
Simultaneous similarity is approximate. Small deviations can alter turbulence intensity and pressure spectra.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Hydrodynamic pressure pulsations** determine ΔK.
* **TSR** controls pressure amplitude and frequency.
* **Crack length** alters local compliance, increasing ΔK nonlinearly.
* **Stress ratio R** modifies effective crack driving force.

These interactions explain the observed transition from stable to accelerated crack growth beyond a/b = 0.05.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption | Affected principle     | Consequence              |
| ------------------- | ---------------------- | ------------------------ |
| Linear elasticity   | LEFM                   | Threshold overprediction |
| One-way FSI         | Structural equilibrium | ΔK underestimation       |
| No corrosion        | Crack closure          | Faster growth            |
| Stationary loads    | Fatigue mechanics      | Non-conservative life    |
| Ideal crack shape   | Stress intensity       | Path deviation           |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Corrosion-fatigue acceleration** reducing crack arrest capability.
* **Cavitation erosion pits** acting as secondary crack initiators.
* **Mode mixity at blade root** leading to faster fracture than predicted.
* **Overload events** during extreme tides invalidating Walker-based predictions.
* **Resonant excitation** from blade-passing frequency coupling with natural modes.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Materials

* 6061 aluminum prioritizes manufacturability and corrosion resistance over fatigue endurance.

### Geometry

* Thin trailing edge optimized for hydrodynamics sacrifices crack tolerance.

### Modeling

* XFEM enables crack tracking but increases reliance on idealized material behavior.

### Not optimized for

* Long-term corrosion exposure.
* Variable-amplitude load spectra.
* Post-crack load redistribution.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Shaft base is fully fixed, implying infinite foundation stiffness.
* No coupling with drivetrain dynamics or electrical load fluctuations.
* Marine growth and sediment interaction are excluded.

**Implication**
Real turbines experience additional bending moments and torsional fluctuations not represented here.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Crack growth is simulated over **10–20 h equivalent operation**.
* No aging, corrosion progression, or stiffness degradation modeled beyond crack extension.

**Consequence**
The identified threshold represents an **early-life criterion**, not a full design life boundary.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Static stresses are <3 percent of yield, providing large elastic margin.
* Fatigue margin is governed entirely by crack size control.
* Single-point vulnerability is **undetected crack growth beyond a/b = 0.05**.

Failure beyond this threshold is rapid and non-graceful.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* TSR near peak efficiency.
* Clean blades.
* Moderate turbulence.

### Likely operative extremes

* High TSR during control lag.
* Cavitation onset.
* Corrosion and fouling.

### Robustness assessment

The blade is **statically robust but fatigue-sensitive**. Reliability depends on early crack detection and strict TSR control.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Nature of ice loads

* Ice loads are inferred **indirectly** from ice-induced strains measured on hull structures.
* Ice loads act locally on discrete subpanels and can be **superposed linearly** to obtain total ice load.
* Ice load time histories exhibit **triangular-like profiles**, justifying Rayleigh separation.

**Consequence**
The method assumes ice–structure interaction is dominated by short-duration, impulsive contact events rather than long-duration creep or global ice pressure.

---

### 1.2 Structural behavior

* The hull structure behaves according to **linear elasticity**.
* A proportional relationship exists between ice loads and measured strains.
* All structural degrees of freedom in the FE model are **fully constrained**.

**Consequence**
Plastic deformation, local buckling, weld yielding, and stiffness degradation during repeated ramming are excluded from the identification process.

---

### 1.3 Measurement fidelity

* Fibre optic strain sensors provide accurate normal strain measurements after:

  * Zero-drift correction
  * Noise reduction
* Sampling frequency of **50 Hz** is sufficient to capture ice-induced dynamics.

**Consequence**
Higher-frequency stress waves, local shock effects, and sub-millisecond crushing phenomena are filtered out.

---

### 1.4 Identification methods

* The Influence Coefficient Matrix (ICM) is valid only when measuring points lie within or near the load action area.
* When sensors fail, far-field identification using an **RBF neural network** can reconstruct loads below the waterline using strains measured above it.
* The RBFNN mapping is **time-invariant** once trained.

**Consequence**
The identification implicitly assumes stationarity of structural stiffness and ice–structure interaction mechanisms throughout the voyage.

---

### 1.5 Statistical treatment

* Ice load events are independent after Rayleigh separation.
* Duration and occurrence of peak loads are well represented by **lognormal** and **exponential** distributions, respectively.
* Peak load statistics are derived from **902 selected icebreaking processes**, assumed representative.

**Consequence**
Long-range temporal correlations, memory effects, and seasonal evolution of ice properties are neglected.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Linear superposition under extreme loading

* Ice pressures from adjacent subpanels are assumed additive even during high-concentration ramming.

**Evidence**
Total ice load is computed by summing subpanel pressures at each time step.

**Risk**
Nonlinear load redistribution through plate bending or frame interaction may invalidate linear superposition during extreme events.

---

### 2.2 Structural invariance over time

* Hull stiffness and boundary conditions do not change over 79 days of operation.
* No cumulative damage or loosening of structural connections occurs.

**Risk**
Repeated ice impacts can alter local stiffness, biasing strain-to-load mapping over time.

---

### 2.3 Ice homogeneity

* Ice thickness is treated as approximately constant at **1.4 m**.
* Variability in salinity, temperature, brine volume, and internal flaws is ignored.

**Risk**
Local ice crushing strength can vary substantially even at constant thickness, affecting peak pressure statistics.

---

### 2.4 RBFNN generalization

* Training cases from numerical simulations and controlled experiments are assumed to span the full operational load space.
* The mapping learned by the RBFNN remains valid for real, stochastic ship–ice interactions.

**Risk**
Out-of-distribution load patterns may be misidentified with deceptively low apparent error.

---

### 2.5 Sensor survivability bias

* Failed measuring points are assumed random and not correlated with peak load locations.

**Risk**
Sensor failure is more likely where ice loads are highest, biasing training and identification toward lower-stress regions.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Structural equilibrium

[
\mathbf{K}\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{F}_{\text{ice}}
]

**Application**
Relates ice-induced pressures to measured strains through the hull’s stiffness matrix.

**Validity regime**
Linear elastic response with small deformations.

**Boundary**
Violates reality if local yielding or plate instability occurs during ramming.

---

### 3.2 Linear elasticity

[
\boldsymbol{\sigma} = \mathbf{D}\boldsymbol{\varepsilon}
]

**Application**
Justifies proportional mapping between ice loads and measured strains.

**Limit**
Ice loads approaching plastic capacity would invalidate the ICM and degrade RBFNN predictions.

---

### 3.3 Contact mechanics of ice crushing

* Ice failure governed by localized compressive crushing, spalling, and non-simultaneous fracture.
* Contact area evolution controls pressure magnitude.

**Emergent behavior**

* Reduction of contact area leads to **pressure spikes** even as total force decreases.

---

### 3.4 Statistical independence

* Rayleigh separation assumes distinct load events once force drops below a fraction of the peak.

**Limit**

* Continuous interaction with intact level ice violates independence assumptions.

---

### 3.5 Kinematics of ship–ice interaction

* Relative velocity between ship and ice governs penetration rate and load duration.
* Ship speed reduction feeds back into load duration.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Ship speed** controls penetration rate, which affects:

  * Duration of load events
  * Peak pressure magnitude
* **Ice concentration** constrains floe mobility, increasing contact duration.
* **Contact area evolution** couples crushing mechanics with load time histories.
* **Structural stiffness** filters contact forces into measurable strains.

These interactions explain why duration dominates peak load magnitude more strongly than occurrence under real navigation conditions.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption | Physical principle affected | Resulting failure         |
| ------------------- | --------------------------- | ------------------------- |
| Linear elasticity   | Structural equilibrium      | Load underestimation      |
| Independent events  | Statistical mechanics       | Misleading fatigue inputs |
| Constant stiffness  | Constitutive behavior       | Drift in identification   |
| Ice homogeneity     | Contact mechanics           | Peak scatter inflation    |
| Stationary mapping  | System identification       | RBFNN extrapolation error |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Progressive hull softening** shifting strain-to-load mapping over time.
* **Extreme localized crushing** producing pressure spikes not captured at 50 Hz.
* **Sensor survivorship bias** underrepresenting the most severe load zones.
* **RBFNN overconfidence** under rare, high-nonlinearity ice impacts.
* **Unobserved mixed-mode failure** where bending and shear interact.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Measurement design

* Dense sensor arrays improve spatial resolution but increase failure probability.
* Normal strain sensing prioritizes load magnitude over precise load location.

### Identification

* RBFNN trades physical interpretability for robustness under sensor failure.
* Shallow architecture limits representational capacity for extreme nonlinear events.

### Not optimized for

* Long-term structural degradation modeling.
* Rare ridge or bergy-bit impacts.
* Real-time onboard decision support.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* FE model assumes fully fixed boundaries, implying infinite hull continuity.
* No coupling with:

  * Global ship motions
  * Propeller–ice interaction
  * Internal tank sloshing
* Ice–hull friction treated implicitly via strain response, not explicitly modeled.

**Implication**
Identified loads represent **local structural demand**, not global ship response.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Ice loads evolve over milliseconds to seconds.
* Hull properties are assumed time-invariant over weeks.
* Ice failure modes evolve during repeated ramming:

  * Wide loads → narrow loads
  * Continuous → detached interaction

**Consequence**
The system transitions between distinct interaction regimes without updating the identification model.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Redundancy provided by far-field sensing mitigates sensor failure.
* Statistical characterization provides probabilistic margins for design.
* Single-point vulnerabilities:

  * Simultaneous failure of above-waterline sensors
  * Out-of-distribution ice events
  * Structural nonlinearity beyond elastic range

Failure beyond these assumptions is **non-graceful**, with rapid loss of identification reliability.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Pack ice
* Moderate ship speeds
* Elastic hull response
* Short-duration impacts

### Likely operative extremes

* Thick level ice with ramming
* Reduced speeds with prolonged contact
* Progressive structural wear
* Ice ridges or spalling-dominated failure

### Robustness assessment

The system is **robust to sensor failure but sensitive to physical regime shifts**. Its reliability depends more on structural linearity and ice behavior consistency than on data quantity.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Nature of fuel consumption modeling

* Ship fuel consumption is treated as a **deterministic function** of ship operational parameters and environmental variables once inputs are specified.
* A **wide feed-forward neural network (FFNN)** can accurately approximate the nonlinear mapping between weather conditions, ship parameters, and fuel consumption.
* The trained FFNN remains valid when driven by synthetic weather scenarios generated via Monte Carlo sampling.

**Consequence**
Fuel consumption physics such as propeller–hull interaction, added resistance, and engine efficiency are embedded implicitly in data rather than modeled explicitly.

---

### 1.2 Weather uncertainty representation

* Long-term ECMWF ERA5 reanalysis data from **1940–2024** adequately captures the probabilistic variability of marine weather parameters.
* Fitted probability distributions represent the **true climatological variability** at each route point.
* Weather parameters at each route point are treated as **statistically independent** during sampling.

**Consequence**
Spatio-temporal coherence of weather systems is intentionally neglected, producing a spatial uncertainty envelope rather than a time-consistent meteorological realization.

---

### 1.3 Route discretization

* The voyage can be discretized into **150 independent route points** for uncertainty analysis.
* Fuel consumption uncertainty can be evaluated locally at each point without requiring continuity between points.

**Consequence**
Cumulative voyage-scale fuel uncertainty is inferred indirectly rather than simulated as an evolving dynamical process.

---

### 1.4 Ship operational parameters

* Ambient temperature, ambient humidity, and ship speed are treated as **deterministic** during uncertainty analysis.
* Passenger load is constant.
* Engine behavior follows fixed **Specific Fuel Oil Consumption (SFOC)** curves.

**Consequence**
Human decisions, operational adaptations, and speed adjustments in response to weather uncertainty are excluded.

---

### 1.5 Monte Carlo methodology

* Random sampling from fitted distributions is sufficient to propagate uncertainty through the FFNN.
* A sample size of **100,000 scenarios per route point** ensures convergence of output statistics.
* Statistical descriptors such as mean, standard deviation, confidence intervals, KDE, and box plots adequately characterize uncertainty.

**Consequence**
Rare but physically plausible compound extremes may still be under-represented.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Physical stationarity

* The statistical properties of marine weather from 1940–2024 are assumed representative of future operational conditions.
* Climate change effects are implicitly averaged into distributions rather than treated as non-stationary trends.

**Risk**
Future fuel consumption variability may be underestimated if climate-driven shifts alter wave or wind regimes.

---

### 2.2 Engine and propulsion invariance

* Engine efficiency, hull roughness, and propeller condition are constant over time.
* No degradation, fouling, or maintenance effects alter fuel consumption behavior.

**Risk**
Long-term operational variability unrelated to weather is excluded, biasing uncertainty attribution.

---

### 2.3 Independence of sampled variables

* Wind speed, wave height, wave period, and wave direction are sampled independently once distributions are fitted.

**Risk**
Physical correlations such as high waves coinciding with long periods or strong winds are partially broken, possibly producing non-physical combinations.

---

### 2.4 Neural network extrapolation safety

* FFNN predictions are assumed reliable for all sampled weather scenarios within the fitted distribution ranges.

**Risk**
Low-probability tails may drive the network into regions of feature space poorly represented in training data.

---

### 2.5 Deterministic ship response

* Ship speed through water is unaffected by sampled weather conditions.
* No control feedback exists between weather severity and operational decisions.

**Risk**
Real ships reduce speed in heavy seas, which would moderate fuel consumption peaks.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Conservation of energy

[
\dot{m}*{fuel} \cdot LHV = P*{propulsion} + P_{hotel} + P_{losses}
]

**Application**
Fuel consumption ultimately balances delivered mechanical and electrical power demands.

**Limit**
The FFNN does not explicitly enforce energy balance, relying on learned correlations.

---

### 3.2 Hydrodynamic resistance

[
R_{total} = R_{calm} + R_{wind} + R_{wave} + R_{added}
]

**Application**
Weather parameters influence added resistance, which increases required propulsion power.

**Boundary**
Added resistance physics are embedded statistically rather than mechanistically.

---

### 3.3 Engine efficiency and SFOC

[
\text{Fuel rate} = f(P_{engine}, \text{SFOC})
]

**Application**
SFOC curves link engine load to fuel consumption.

**Limit**
Transient engine behavior and partial-load inefficiencies are not modeled dynamically.

---

### 3.4 Probability theory

* Monte Carlo sampling propagates input uncertainty through nonlinear mappings.

**Validity regime**
Assumes sampled distributions reflect true uncertainty and independence assumptions hold.

---

### 3.5 Universal approximation theorem

* A sufficiently wide FFNN can approximate continuous nonlinear functions.

**Boundary**
Approximation accuracy depends on training data coverage, not only network width.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Weather variability** drives **added resistance**, increasing **propulsion power demand**.
* **Propulsion power** interacts with **engine efficiency**, amplifying fuel variability.
* **Statistical independence assumptions** decouple physically linked processes, broadening uncertainty envelopes.
* **Neural approximation** replaces explicit physics, trading interpretability for flexibility.

These interactions explain why uncertainty spreads can reach up to **14 percent** in exposed route segments.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption  | Physical principle affected | Consequence                |
| -------------------- | --------------------------- | -------------------------- |
| Weather independence | Hydrodynamics               | Non-physical scenarios     |
| Stationary climate   | Energy balance              | Underestimated future risk |
| Fixed ship speed     | Resistance balance          | Inflated fuel peaks        |
| Engine invariance    | Thermodynamics              | Bias in mean estimates     |
| FFNN extrapolation   | Approximation theory        | Unbounded prediction error |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Correlation collapse** producing unrealistic wind–wave combinations.
* **Climate drift** invalidating long-term fitted distributions.
* **FFNN tail instability** under rare extreme conditions.
* **Operational feedback omission** overstating worst-case fuel consumption.
* **Overconfidence in confidence intervals** due to structural modeling bias.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Modeling tradeoffs

* Wide FFNN chosen for representational power over physical transparency.
* Monte Carlo favored over analytical propagation for nonlinear complexity.

### Data tradeoffs

* Long climatological records prioritized over short-term forecast realism.
* Ensemble reanalysis used instead of operational ensemble forecasts.

### Not optimized for

* Real-time routing decisions.
* Time-consistent voyage simulation.
* Climate-scenario sensitivity analysis.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Ship operates in open ocean without port interactions.
* No interaction with traffic separation schemes or congestion.
* No coupling with navigational risk or safety constraints.

**Implication**
Fuel uncertainty is isolated from operational risk and scheduling considerations.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Weather variability is treated as **static distributions** at fixed points.
* No memory or persistence between successive route points.
* Fuel uncertainty is spatially resolved but not temporally accumulated.

**Consequence**
Results represent an **upper-bound envelope** rather than a realizable voyage trajectory.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Large Monte Carlo sample size provides statistical robustness.
* Multiple goodness-of-fit metrics reduce distribution misselection risk.
* Single-point vulnerabilities:

  * Non-stationary climate effects
  * Correlated extremes
  * FFNN generalization limits

Failure of these assumptions leads to **systematic bias**, not graceful degradation.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Long-term average climatology
* Fixed ship speed
* Stable propulsion system
* Independent spatial uncertainty

### Likely operative regime

* Temporally correlated storms
* Adaptive speed and routing
* Engine degradation
* Cumulative uncertainty across route

### Robustness assessment

The framework is **robust for strategic planning and sensitivity analysis**, but **not sufficient for operational forecasting** without temporal coupling.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 1.1 Structural idealization of SFT

* The SFT tube is modeled as a **beam element**.
* **Axial deformation and shear deformation of the tube are neglected**.
* Anchor cables are modeled as **linear springs** with axial stiffness only.
* Joints are represented by **six independent stiffness components**: axial, vertical shear, horizontal shear, vertical bending, horizontal bending, and torsion.

**Consequence**
The structure is treated as bending-dominated. Load redistribution through axial force, membrane action, or shear lag is excluded.

---

### 1.2 Segmentation and symmetry

* Midspan tubes experience **symmetric internal forces**.
* Revetment tubes experience **asymmetric internal forces**.
* After segmentation, each tube segment and its attached cables achieve **local force equilibrium**.

**Consequence**
Adjacent segments are weakly coupled in the midspan, enabling independent stiffness design of repeated segments.

---

### 1.3 Governing displacement criteria

* Maximum allowable deflection for the full SFT is **L/4000**.
* Maximum allowable deflection for a single tube is **l/4000**.
* Maximum allowable joint shear deformation is **3 mm**.
* Maximum allowable joint torsional rotation is **0.5 rad/1000**.
* Railway comfort and safety criteria govern all stiffness decisions.

**Consequence**
The entire design process is displacement-driven rather than stress-driven.

---

### 1.4 Load representation

* Loads considered include:

  * Self-weight
  * Buoyancy
  * Regular wave loads
* Dynamic wave loading is treated using **potential flow theory**.
* Earthquake and indirect inertial loads are excluded.

**Consequence**
The design is valid for marine-environment-dominated loading but not for seismic-dominated scenarios.

---

### 1.5 Material behavior

* Concrete tubes and steel cables behave as **linear elastic materials**.
* No cracking, plasticity, creep, fatigue, or degradation is considered.

**Consequence**
Long-term service effects and extreme overload scenarios are excluded from the stiffness design logic.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### 2.1 Negligibility of axial and shear deformation

* By neglecting axial and shear deformation, the model assumes:

  * Large diameter-to-length ratios
  * High axial stiffness relative to bending stiffness

**Risk**
For long spans or high BWR values, axial force and shear deformation may become non-negligible, invalidating displacement predictions.

---

### 2.2 Structural homogeneity

* All midspan tubes are assumed identical in geometry and stiffness.
* Cable properties are assumed uniform except where intentionally varied.

**Risk**
Manufacturing tolerances, construction deviations, or differential aging could break symmetry and invalidate segmentation assumptions.

---

### 2.3 Perfect load transfer at joints

* Joint stiffness components act independently and linearly.
* No coupling exists between bending, shear, and torsion.

**Risk**
Real joints exhibit stiffness coupling and nonlinear contact behavior, especially under cyclic loading.

---

### 2.4 Cable behavior

* Anchor cables remain in tension at all times.
* No slackening, snap loading, or geometric nonlinearity occurs.

**Risk**
Loss of tension under extreme wave troughs or accidental load redistribution could cause dynamic instability.

---

### 2.5 Prestress equivalence

* Designing cable stiffness directly is assumed to be superior and equivalent to prestress optimization.

**Risk**
Prestress provides controllable initial force states that stiffness alone cannot replicate under variable loads.

---

### 2.6 Hydrodynamic simplification

* Wave forces are applied directly as external loads.
* Fluid-structure interaction feedback is neglected.

**Risk**
Resonance, added mass variation, and damping effects may be misrepresented.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Structural equilibrium

[
[K]{x} = {R}
]

**Application**
The matrix displacement method enforces equilibrium between stiffness, displacement, and applied loads.

**Validity regime**
Linear elastic, small deformation regime.

**Limit**
Large rotations or geometric nonlinearity violate this formulation.

---

### 3.2 Euler–Bernoulli beam theory

[
EI \frac{d^4 w}{dx^4} = q(x)
]

**Application**
Tube bending response governs displacement and moment distribution.

**Assumption embedded**
Plane sections remain plane. Shear deformation is negligible.

---

### 3.3 Spring mechanics

[
k = \frac{EA}{L}
]

**Application**
Anchor cable stiffness relates geometry and material properties to displacement control.

**Limit**
Valid only for small strains and constant cable geometry.

---

### 3.4 Energy balance in dynamic response

* Dynamic amplification arises from conversion between:

  * Kinetic energy
  * Strain energy
  * Potential energy

**Observation**
Dynamic displacement slightly exceeds static design limits due to stored energy.

---

### 3.5 Compatibility and continuity

* Rotation and displacement continuity at joints governs joint stiffness requirements.

**Key result**
Joint bending stiffness ≥ 3EI/l yields near-uniform bending moments.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Tube bending stiffness** interacts with **cable stiffness** to control displacement envelopes.
* **Joint stiffness** redistributes bending moments between adjacent segments.
* **Boundary conditions** dominate revetment tube response but weakly affect midspan behavior.
* **Dynamic energy accumulation** amplifies displacement beyond static predictions.

These interactions explain why stiffness ratios, not absolute stiffness, govern performance.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption         | Principle affected | Result                    |
| --------------------------- | ------------------ | ------------------------- |
| Neglected shear deformation | Beam theory        | Underestimated deflection |
| Linear joint stiffness      | Compatibility      | Joint overstress          |
| Constant cable tension      | Spring mechanics   | Dynamic instability       |
| Symmetry                    | Equilibrium        | Load localization         |
| Linear material behavior    | Elasticity         | Progressive damage        |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Joint shear failure** if cable placement does not coincide with zero-shear locations.
* **Cable force redistribution** causing localized overstress.
* **Revetment tube moment reversal** under fixed boundary conditions.
* **Dynamic amplification exceedance** under wave resonance.
* **Construction sequence sensitivity**, where temporary states violate design assumptions.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Material utilization

* Uniform bending moment improves material efficiency but requires higher joint stiffness.

### Design philosophy

* Displacement-controlled design sacrifices local stress optimization.

### Excluded optimizations

* Fatigue life
* Redundancy under cable failure
* Progressive collapse resistance

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Shore connections modeled as hinged, fixed, or elastic springs.
* Elastic boundaries interpolate between free rotation and full fixity.
* Boundary stiffness dominates local behavior but decays rapidly into the midspan.

**System implication**
The SFT behaves as a quasi-infinite periodic structure away from shore.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* No degradation of:

  * Cable stiffness
  * Joint stiffness
  * Tube stiffness
* No accumulation of fatigue damage.
* Dynamic response assumed stationary after transient decay.

**Consequence**
Design is valid for initial service state, not end-of-life conditions.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

### Explicit margins

* Displacement limits based on railway standards.
* Joint deformation limits based on gasket watertightness.

### Implicit margins

* Overdesign of joint bending stiffness.
* Conservative dynamic load selection.

### Single-point vulnerabilities

* Joint shear failure
* Cable rupture
* Boundary condition degradation

No graceful degradation mechanisms are defined.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design regime

* Linear elastic
* Regular waves
* Fully tensioned cables
* Symmetric segmentation

### Likely operative extremes

* Irregular waves
* Cable loss or slack
* Joint nonlinear behavior
* Construction stage asymmetry

**Robustness assessment**
The method is robust for **global stiffness tuning** but fragile to **local nonlinear failures**.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### Materials and Microstructure

* Ice is assumed to be **polycrystalline**, homogeneous at the specimen scale, with a **constant density of 920 kg/m³** across all temperatures.
* The hydrogen bond network is treated as the **primary temperature-sensitive microstructural mechanism** controlling stiffness, strength, and brittleness.
* Ice crystals are implicitly assumed to have statistically similar grain size distributions across samples and temperatures.

### Geometry and Boundary Conditions

* Cylindrical samples with **diameter 50 mm and height 125 mm** satisfy uniaxial compression validity based on Schwarz et al. (1981).
* End faces are assumed flat to within **0.02 mm**, implying negligible bending or eccentric loading.
* Platens are assumed rigid relative to ice, with no compliance effects considered.

### Loading and Rate Effects

* A **single strain rate of 1.33 × 10⁻² s⁻¹** is treated as representative of brittle ice behavior.
* The strain rate is assumed sufficiently high to suppress ductile mechanisms such as basal slip at lower temperatures.
* Loading is purely axial, with lateral confinement neglected.

### Thermal Conditions

* Ice samples are assumed to be **thermally equilibrated** after 24 h of conditioning.
* Temperature within each sample is assumed spatially uniform.
* Five discrete temperatures from −12 °C to −28 °C are assumed sufficient to capture temperature trends.

### Measurement and Observation

* High-speed camera observations at **500 Hz** are assumed adequate to capture crack initiation and propagation.
* Fragment sizes obtained via sieving are assumed not to be significantly altered during handling.

### Numerical Modeling

* PFC2D is assumed capable of reproducing essential micromechanics of ice fracture despite being two-dimensional.
* The **parallel bond model** is assumed to represent hydrogen bond behavior in ice.
* Microparameters calibrated at each temperature are assumed physically meaningful rather than purely phenomenological.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### Manufacturing and Sample Preparation

* Freezing produces **minimal residual thermal stresses**, despite staged freezing and bubble removal.
* Ice purity and dissolved gas content are assumed consistent across samples.
* Cutting and polishing do not introduce microcracks that bias results.

### Material Symmetry and Homogeneity

* Ice is treated as **statistically isotropic**, ignoring crystallographic texture or preferred grain orientation.
* Grain boundary properties are assumed uniform and temperature-dependent only through scalar parameters.

### Environmental Variability

* No consideration of **temperature gradients during loading**, despite known sensitivity of ice to thermal heterogeneity.
* Humidity, sublimation, and frost effects during handling are assumed negligible.

### Human and Operational Factors

* Manual fragment screening is assumed repeatable and non-destructive.
* Operator skill variability is implicitly ignored.

### Aging and Time Effects

* Ice is assumed not to undergo significant **annealing, sintering, or microstructural evolution** during storage.
* Creep and stress relaxation prior to peak load are neglected.

### Numerical Calibration Philosophy

* Trial-and-error calibration is assumed to converge toward a unique or near-unique parameter set.
* Weak coupling between stiffness and strength parameters is assumed valid across temperatures.
* Friction coefficient fixed at 0.1 is assumed temperature-invariant.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### Linear Elasticity

* **Hooke’s Law** governs pre-peak response:

  * σ = Eε
* Valid in the small-strain, crack-free regime.
* Breakdown occurs near crack nucleation when damage localizes.

### Brittle Fracture Mechanics

* **Griffith energy criterion**:

  * σ_c ∝ (Eγ / πa)¹ᐟ²
* Crack propagation governed by energy balance between elastic strain energy release and surface energy.
* Fractal crack paths modify effective crack length and energy dissipation.

### Thermodynamics of Hydrogen Bonding

* Hydrogen bond energy increases as temperature decreases.
* Reduced vibrational entropy leads to higher lattice rigidity.
* Valid below the ductile-to-brittle transition regime.

### Contact Mechanics

* Normal and shear contact laws govern particle interactions in PFC.
* Parallel bond failure criteria represent tensile and shear decohesion.
* Valid while particle overlaps remain small.

### Statistical Fracture and Fractals

* Fragment size distributions follow power laws:

  * M(d)/M_t ∝ d^(3−D)
* Assumes scale invariance over measured fragment sizes.
* Breakdown expected at nano or grain scales.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Thermal stiffening** increases elastic modulus, which raises stored elastic energy and promotes brittle instability.
* **Reduced plasticity** at low temperature suppresses crack branching, reinforcing Griffith-type single-crack dominance.
* **Fracture mechanics and fractal geometry** interact, as simpler crack paths concentrate energy and increase strength.
* **Contact friction and bond strength** compete, determining whether cracks localize in tension or shear.
* **End friction and platen constraints** interact with bulk strength, influencing fracture plane inclination.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Connections

* If thermal equilibrium is violated, local modulus gradients invalidate uniform elasticity assumptions.
* If ice is anisotropic, Griffith predictions based on isotropy fail and crack paths rotate unpredictably.
* If friction coefficient varies with temperature, inferred dominance of tensile cracking may reverse.
* If strain rate deviates, ductile mechanisms activate, invalidating brittle fracture models and fractal interpretations.

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

### Experimental

* Residual thermal stresses may trigger premature crack nucleation.
* End friction may induce shear bands unrelated to intrinsic material behavior.
* Fragment breakage during screening biases fractal dimension downward.

### Numerical

* 2D confinement suppresses out-of-plane cracking and energy dissipation.
* Parameter non-uniqueness may mask compensating errors.
* Ignoring grain size effects may underpredict crack tortuosity.

### Physical

* At lower temperatures, microcrack coalescence may become unstable, leading to explosive fragmentation not captured here.
* Hydrogen bond embrittlement may trigger cleavage along crystallographic planes.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* Choice of PFC2D favors interpretability over full 3D realism.
* Fractal analysis prioritizes post-mortem fragmentation over in-situ crack topology.
* Temperature range chosen avoids ductile regime complexity but limits generality.
* Calibration accuracy prioritized over parameter transferability.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Load transfer assumed purely axial with no lateral confinement.
* No coupling to surrounding structures, fluids, or thermal reservoirs.
* Implicit assumption that laboratory results extrapolate to sea ice and engineering scales.
* No feedback from crack formation to thermal or moisture fields.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* Damage accumulation assumed monotonic with strain.
* Fatigue and cyclic loading ignored.
* Crack nucleation assumed instantaneous relative to loading time.
* No healing or refreezing of cracks considered.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

* Six repeat tests provide statistical smoothing but no formal safety factor.
* Numerical validation margin of ~6.6 percent masks potential compensating errors.
* Single-point failures include friction coefficient misestimation and temperature nonuniformity.
* No graceful degradation modeled beyond peak stress.

---

## 11. Design Regime vs Operative Regime

### Design Regime

* Homogeneous, equilibrated ice
* High strain rate
* Pure uniaxial compression
* Brittle fracture dominance

### Likely Operative Regime

* Temperature gradients
* Mixed loading modes
* Variable strain rates
* Scale-dependent fracture behavior

### Robustness Assessment

* First-principles framework is robust within the tested regime.
* Extrapolation beyond −12 °C to −28 °C or beyond laboratory scale is fragile without additional coupling physics.
