## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Ice Material Properties

* **Sea ice is modeled as quasi-brittle** with failure dominated by compression and bending.
* **Strength values** are defined as the **90th percentile** of in-situ Arctic summer ice data:

  * Compressive strength: **4.88 MPa**
  * Bending strength: **0.27 MPa**
* **Rate effects** are implicitly fixed by:

  * Uniaxial compression crosshead speed of **0.1 mm/s**
  * No explicit strain-rate dependence in the constitutive law during collisions.
* Ice density is fixed at **900 kg/m³** for solid elements and cohesive elements.
* Ice is assumed **homogeneous and isotropic** at the element scale.

**Consequence**
Ice strength variability beyond the 90th percentile is treated as a tail risk, not a design baseline.

---

### 1.2 Constitutive and Fracture Modeling

* Solid ice uses **MAT_024 (piecewise linear plasticity)** with:

  * Linear elasticity up to yield
  * Plastic softening to a crushing stress of **0.01 MPa**
  * Crushing strain fixed at **0.25**
* Fracture is represented exclusively by **cohesive element failure** governed by:

  * Linear traction-separation law
  * Prescribed fracture energy values:

    * Mode I: **80 J/m²**
    * Mode II: **100 J/m²**
* Cohesive elements are assumed:

  * Massless
  * Volume-negligible
  * Deleted without violating conservation laws

**Consequence**
All macroscopic cracking, fragmentation, and load shedding are assumed to emerge from mesoscale cohesive failure rather than bulk damage evolution.

---

### 1.3 Geometry, Boundary Conditions, and Loading

* Ice thickness, ship speed, and collision duration are treated as **deterministic inputs**.
* Level ice geometry:

  * Width: **80 m**
  * Length: **480 m**
* Hull is modeled as a **rigid body**.
* Constant ship velocity is enforced using **prescribed rigid motion**.
* Ice boundaries:

  * One free edge
  * Three non-reflective boundaries
* Friction coefficients:

  * Hull-ice: **0.2**
  * Ice-ice: **0.1**

**Consequence**
Hull deformation, structural compliance, and feedback between ice loads and ship motion are explicitly neglected.

---

### 1.4 Fluid Interaction

* Seawater effects are approximated using:

  * Archimedes buoyancy
  * High-Reynolds-number drag with fixed coefficient **Cd = 1.05**
* Seawater velocity is assumed **zero**.
* No wave, slush, or turbulence modeling.

**Consequence**
Hydrodynamics influence only post-fracture fragment motion, not ice failure initiation.

---

### 1.5 Statistical Treatment

* Ice load peaks are assumed to follow a **three-parameter Weibull distribution**.
* Peak extraction uses:

  * Fixed thresholds (100 kN measured, 20 kN simulated)
  * Rayleigh separation
* Maximum likelihood estimation is assumed valid for parameter inference.
* Sensor inversion assumes **linear strain-load superposition**.

**Consequence**
Statistical stationarity is assumed over the analysis window.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### 2.1 Manufacturing and Numerical Assumptions

* Mesh quality is assumed sufficient because:

  * Pentahedral elements with face size ≈ **2× ice thickness** are used.
* Fracture energy regularization is assumed to remove mesh dependence.
* Contact erosion does not introduce spurious momentum loss.

**Evidence**
Reliance on prior literature claiming mesh size 2–3× ice thickness yields stable loads.

---

### 2.2 Material Homogeneity and Aging

* Ice porosity, brine volume, salinity gradients, and temperature gradients are ignored.
* Ice properties are assumed time-invariant over the 5-minute simulation.
* No fatigue, creep, or subcritical crack growth is modeled.

**Evidence**
Single set of material parameters used across all conditions.

---

### 2.3 Human and Operational Factors

* Navigation is assumed straight-line and steady.
* No steering, heave, pitch, or yaw motions are included.
* Operator behavior does not affect collision dynamics.

---

### 2.4 Boundary Condition Idealization

* Non-reflective ice boundaries are assumed to eliminate artificial stress waves.
* Adjacent ice fields are assumed mechanically passive.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### 3.1 Conservation of Linear Momentum

**Law**
[
\sum F = \frac{d}{dt}(m v)
]

**Application**
Ice loads arise from momentum exchange between hull and ice elements during collision.

**Validity Regime**

* Explicit dynamics
* No relativistic effects
* Neglects hull compliance

**Boundary Risk**
Rigid hull assumption transfers all momentum change into ice fracture and contact forces.

---

### 3.2 Linear Elasticity and Plasticity

**Law**
[
\sigma = E \varepsilon \quad (\text{elastic regime})
]

**Application**
Solid ice deforms elastically up to yield, then softens plastically.

**Limits**

* Valid only for small strains
* Ignores anisotropy and rate dependence

---

### 3.3 Fracture Mechanics and Energy Balance

**Law**
[
G = \frac{dW}{dA}
]

Fracture occurs when energy release rate equals fracture energy.

**Application**
Cohesive elements dissipate fracture energy during crack growth.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Crack shielding
* Fragment size distribution sensitivity to Gc

---

### 3.4 Contact Mechanics and Friction

**Law**
[
F_t = \mu F_n
]

**Application**
Controls tangential resistance during ice sliding and accumulation.

**Limits**

* Constant friction coefficients ignore pressure, temperature, and melt effects.

---

### 3.5 Fluid Forces

**Buoyancy**
[
F_b = \rho g A h
]

**Drag**
[
F_d = \frac{1}{2} C_d \rho A v^2
]

**Limits**
Assumes high Reynolds number, neglects vortex shedding and unsteady flow.

---

### 3.6 Extreme Value Statistics

**Law**
Weibull distribution models stochastic maxima of ice load peaks.

**Assumption**
Load peaks are independent and identically distributed after thresholding.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Fracture energy vs momentum transfer**
  Higher fracture energy increases contact duration, increasing peak loads.
* **Ice thickness vs bending stiffness**
  Thicker ice increases bending rigidity, shifting failure from bending to compression.
* **Speed vs strain rate**
  Increased speed raises effective strain rate, but no rate sensitivity is modeled.
* **Fragmentation vs hydrodynamics**
  Fragment size affects drag, which affects recontact probability.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Assumption Violated         | Physical Breakdown                                   |
| --------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------- |
| Homogeneous ice             | Stress localization invalidates cohesive calibration |
| Constant friction           | Sliding forces misestimated                          |
| Rigid hull                  | Load sharing and peak reduction ignored              |
| Stationary water            | Drag and accumulation mispredicted                   |
| Weibull parent distribution | Extreme load risk mischaracterized                   |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* Underestimated peak loads if:

  * Ice temperature decreases
  * Brine volume increases brittleness
* Overestimated safety if:

  * Hull flexibility reduces local stresses
* Spurious crack patterns if:

  * Mesh size deviates from cohesive length
* Non-conservative results if:

  * Ice ridges or floes replace level ice

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* **Material choice**
  Simplified plasticity chosen over viscoplastic models for computational tractability.
* **Geometry**
  Ice length chosen to match time window, not physical floe size distribution.
* **Optimization target**
  Load statistics prioritized over detailed stress fields.

What is not optimized:

* Energy efficiency
* Propulsion–ice interaction
* Structural fatigue accumulation

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Hull–ice interface is the sole load path.
* No coupling to propulsion or control systems.
* No feedback from ice loads to ship speed or heading.
* Ice fragments are dynamically isolated from the parent ice field.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* No damage accumulation across impacts.
* No evolution of ice properties with repeated crushing.
* Statistical parameters assumed stable over time.
* No memory effects in fracture behavior.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

### Applied Margins

* 90th percentile ice strength
* Conservative load thresholds

### Gaps

* No explicit safety factor on fracture energy
* No redundancy in boundary condition assumptions
* Single-point failure: cohesive parameter miscalibration

---

## 11. Design Regime vs Operative Regime

### Design Regime

* Straight-line navigation
* Level ice
* Constant speed
* Summer Arctic conditions

### Likely Operative Regime

* Variable speed
* Mixed ice types
* Temperature gradients
* Maneuvers and turning

**Robustness Assessment**
First-principles framework remains valid, but parameter values and statistical conclusions are fragile outside the calibrated regime.

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Propeller Geometry and Scale

* The toroidal propeller is treated as a **closed-loop modification of a conventional Ka4-70 blade** with comparable diameter and rotational speed.
* Performance comparisons assume **geometric similarity** between the toroidal and Ka4-70 propellers except for the tip closure.
* Reynolds numbers achieved in experiments and CFD are assumed representative of **small-scale DPV operation**.

**Consequence**
All observed performance gains are attributed primarily to **tip-closure effects**, not blade camber, pitch distribution, or section shape differences.

---

### 1.2 Flow Regime

* Flow is assumed:

  * **Incompressible**
  * **Newtonian**
  * Predominantly **turbulent**, modeled using SST k-ω DES
* Cavitation is **absent or negligible** in all experimental and numerical cases.
* Free-surface effects are captured via deformation but **no air entrainment or multiphase breakup** is modeled.

**Consequence**
Noise reduction claims are inferred indirectly from vortex suppression and wake smoothness, not from acoustic source modeling.

---

### 1.3 Experimental Measurement (PIV)

* 2D-3C PIV is assumed sufficient to:

  * Resolve dominant wake structures
  * Capture tip vortex evolution and dissipation
* Time-averaged velocity fields are treated as representative of **propulsive momentum transfer**.
* Hollow glass microspheres perfectly follow the flow with negligible slip.

**Consequence**
Out-of-plane vortex stretching and small-scale turbulence intermittency are implicitly neglected.

---

### 1.4 Numerical Modeling of DPV

* DPV hull is rigid with **no structural deformation**.
* Propellers operate at fixed RPM without feedback from motor torque limits.
* Ocean current velocity is steady and uniform at **1 m/s**.
* Roll motion is neglected as hydrodynamically insignificant.
* Propeller-DPV interaction is fully captured via DES without explicit propeller-structure vibration coupling.

**Consequence**
Hydrodynamic forces are decoupled from control, compliance, and diver-induced disturbances.

---

### 1.5 Performance Metrics

* Increased thrust and wake velocity are equated to **improved propulsion performance**, even when efficiency decreases.
* Self-propulsion speed is treated as the primary success criterion, over endurance or power draw.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### 2.1 Reynolds Number Similarity

* It is implicitly assumed that:

  * Wake suppression mechanisms scale favorably at low Reynolds numbers
  * Toroidal advantages observed here will persist across **different DPV sizes and speeds**

**Evidence**
No explicit Reynolds scaling analysis or nondimensional wake comparison is provided.

---

### 2.2 Surface Roughness Effects

* Toroidal propeller roughness is acknowledged as influencing vortex core size, yet:

  * Roughness is not controlled or matched between propellers
  * Its contribution is not isolated from geometry effects

**Hidden Tradeoff**
Some wake changes attributed to toroidal geometry may actually arise from **manufacturing-induced roughness**.

---

### 2.3 Energy Budget Assumption

* Increased thrust is treated as beneficial even though:

  * Torque also increases
  * Efficiency is sometimes lower

**Implicit Constraint**
DPV missions prioritize **maneuverability and speed** over endurance and battery life.

---

### 2.4 Statistical Stationarity

* Wake structures and forces are assumed statistically steady over the observation window.
* Transient burst events, intermittent separation, or low-frequency unsteadiness are not treated as dominant contributors.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### 3.1 Conservation of Momentum

**Law**
[
T = \dot{m} (V_{out} - V_{in})
]

**Application**

* Toroidal propellers generate higher thrust by accelerating a **larger effective mass of water**, both axially and circumferentially.

**Limit**
Momentum conservation does not guarantee efficiency, only force production.

---

### 3.2 Vorticity Transport

**Law**
[
\frac{D\boldsymbol{\omega}}{Dt} = (\boldsymbol{\omega} \cdot \nabla)\mathbf{u} - (\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u})\boldsymbol{\omega} + \nu \nabla^2 \boldsymbol{\omega}
]

**Application**

* Closed tips suppress radial vorticity flux and redistribute circulation circumferentially.
* Increased adverse pressure gradients near toroidal tips accelerate vortex fragmentation.

**Emergent Effect**
Earlier vortex breakup reduces coherent far-wake structures but increases near-wake turbulence intensity.

---

### 3.3 Boundary Layer and Separation Physics

* Tip closure alters:

  * Pressure gradient at blade ends
  * Separation onset location
* Toroidal geometry induces **stronger circumferential induced velocity**, thickening shear layers.

**Boundary**
At higher J, separation-induced losses may offset thrust gains.

---

### 3.4 Free Surface Hydrodynamics

**Law**
Dynamic pressure–surface elevation coupling:
[
\Delta h \sim \frac{p}{\rho g}
]

**Application**

* Toroidal propellers reduce surface disturbance by redistributing momentum below the free surface.
* Faster wake recovery indicates reduced upward momentum flux.

---

### 3.5 Turbulence Modeling Limits

* DES resolves large-scale vortices but:

  * Relies on RANS near walls
  * Is sensitive to grid alignment and y⁺ control

**Risk**
Wake instability and vortex merging may be grid-dependent.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Momentum vs Efficiency**
  Higher thrust increases wake kinetic energy, raising power demand.
* **Vorticity Suppression vs Separation**
  Eliminating tip vortices intensifies pressure gradients, triggering earlier shear-layer breakup.
* **Free Surface Interaction vs Thrust**
  Higher thrust improves speed but can amplify surface-wave coupling if not well submerged.
* **Attitude vs Wake Symmetry**
  Pitch and yaw break wake symmetry, amplifying transverse forces and vortex shedding.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Violated Assumption     | Resulting Breakdown                                 |
| ----------------------- | --------------------------------------------------- |
| Reynolds similarity     | Wake mechanisms may invert at different scales      |
| Negligible cavitation   | Tip benefits may vanish under cavitating conditions |
| Rigid DPV               | Structural vibration could reintroduce noise        |
| Steady current          | Unsteady inflow may destabilize wake suppression    |
| Efficiency not critical | Battery endurance may drop below mission needs      |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* **Battery depletion risk** due to higher torque demand
* **Increased mechanical stress** at toroidal blade roots
* **Bottom-wall interaction amplification** in benthic operations
* **Wake–instrument interference** from intensified near-wake turbulence
* **Control instability** under combined yaw and pitch at high thrust

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

### Why Toroidal Geometry

* Optimized for:

  * Noise suppression
  * Thrust stability
* Not optimized for:

  * Peak efficiency
  * Manufacturing simplicity
  * Fouling resistance

### What Was Not Optimized

* Acoustic spectrum
* Energy per distance traveled
* Diver fatigue from reaction forces

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* DPV is treated as hydrodynamically isolated.
* No coupling to:

  * Diver body
  * Tether effects
  * Control feedback loops
* Surface modeled as deformable but not breaking.
* Bottom treated as rigid, smooth wall.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* No fatigue accumulation in propeller or DPV structure.
* No biofouling or surface degradation over time.
* Wake statistics assumed invariant over mission duration.
* No thermal or salinity gradients considered.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

### Present Margins

* Grid convergence verification
* Experimental validation within ±5 %

### Missing Margins

* No uncertainty quantification for turbulence model choice
* No margin for manufacturing variability
* No allowance for diver-induced disturbances

---

## 11. Design Regime vs Operative Regime

### Design Regime

* Calm water
* Controlled currents
* Fixed RPM
* Clean propeller surfaces

### Likely Operative Regime

* Unsteady currents
* Proximity to divers, rocks, vegetation
* Variable thrust demand
* Fouling and wear

**Robustness Assessment**
The physics framework remains valid, but **performance claims are conditionally bounded** to low-Re, non-cavitating, controlled-attitude regimes.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Vehicle and Mission Model

* The underwater glider operates in **steady, straight-line gliding motion**.
* Turning dynamics and roll motion are neglected.
* Each dive–climb cycle is treated as a **sequence of quasi-steady profiles**.
* Feedback control is assumed to maintain the glider within a vertical plane.

**Implication**
Transient maneuvering, lateral disturbances, and coupled yaw–roll instabilities are outside the model validity.

---

### 1.2 Morphing Wing Degrees of Freedom

* Only two morphing parameters are considered:

  * Arc length γ
  * Bending angle β
* Wing width and thickness are fixed.
* When β = 0, the wing reduces to a rectangular flat plate.

**Implication**
The design space is intentionally constrained. Other influential parameters such as twist, camber distribution, spanwise stiffness variation, and leading-edge curvature are excluded.

---

### 1.3 Structural Mechanics

* The driving mechanism is:

  * Planar
  * Rigid-body dominated
  * Free of backlash and joint compliance
* Structural vibration and dynamic instability are neglected.
* Hydrodynamic loading is approximated as a **uniform pressure of 15 Pa**.

**Implication**
The mechanism is assumed to remain in static equilibrium during operation, with negligible fluid-structure coupling.

---

### 1.4 Hydrodynamic Regime

* Flow is:

  * Incompressible
  * Newtonian
  * Low speed
* Reynolds number is sufficiently high to justify **k-ω SST turbulence modeling**.
* Lift, drag, and pitch moment scale with **V²**.

**Implication**
Laminar–turbulent transition effects and Reynolds-number-dependent profile changes are not explicitly resolved.

---

### 1.5 Mass and Buoyancy Distribution

* Changes in γ and β:

  * Do not alter total mass
  * Do not shift the buoyancy center
* Movable mass block and glider body are treated as rigid bodies.
* Morphing wing mass is negligible relative to the hull.

**Implication**
Structural morphing is decoupled from hydrostatic stability and trim.

---

### 1.6 Energy Consumption Model

* Energy usage is decomposed into:

  * Buoyancy adjustment
  * Attitude adjustment
  * Control-navigation-detection electronics
* Actuator efficiencies and losses are embedded in empirical constants.
* Wing morphing energy consumption is not explicitly modeled.

**Implication**
Morphing is treated as energetically free once configuration is set.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### 2.1 Quasi-Steady Validity

* Hydrodynamic forces derived from steady CFD are assumed valid during:

  * Diving
  * Ascending
  * Configuration transitions

**Hidden Risk**
During active morphing, unsteady separation and transient lift spikes may violate equilibrium assumptions.

---

### 2.2 Polynomial Surrogate Fidelity

* Quartic polynomials are assumed sufficient to represent:

  * Strongly nonlinear fluid–structure interactions
  * Cross-coupled effects of γ, β, and α

**Hidden Tradeoff**
High R² indicates interpolation accuracy, not extrapolation robustness.

---

### 2.3 Structural Load Envelope

* A pressure difference of 15 Pa is assumed to bound all operational hydrodynamic loads.

**Hidden Constraint**
This implicitly assumes:

* Calm ocean conditions
* No wave-induced dynamic pressure
* No rapid pitch excursions

---

### 2.4 Modular Scalability

* Adding or removing deployable modules preserves:

  * Kinematic symmetry
  * Structural stiffness trends
  * Hydrodynamic continuity

**Hidden Assumption**
Scaling effects on stiffness, backlash accumulation, and skin wrinkling are negligible.

---

### 2.5 Material Idealization

* Aluminum alloy properties are assumed:

  * Homogeneous
  * Isotropic
  * Invariant with pressure and temperature
* PMMA prototype behavior is assumed qualitatively representative.

**Hidden Gap**
Creep, fatigue, corrosion, and long-term sealing effects are not considered.

---

### 2.6 Stability Metric Sufficiency

* Static stability is fully captured by the **dimensionless hydrodynamic center arm** lsm.

**Implicit Limitation**
Dynamic stability, limit cycles, and disturbance rejection are not evaluated.

---

## 3. Governing Physical Principles

### 3.1 Force Equilibrium

Steady gliding is governed by:

* Lift–buoyancy balance
* Drag–gravity projection balance
* Zero net pitching moment

This directly constrains α, θ, and V via nonlinear coupling.

---

### 3.2 Hydrodynamic Scaling

[
D, L, M \propto \rho V^2
]

**Consequence**
Small configuration changes in γ and β can amplify force differences quadratically with speed.

---

### 3.3 Stability Physics

[
l_s = \frac{M}{L}
]

* Smaller l_s implies stronger restoring tendency.
* Increasing vertical velocity generally increases lift-induced moments, degrading stability.

This explains the observed contradiction between ηv and ls.

---

### 3.4 Energy Budget Physics

* Buoyancy adjustment energy scales with depth.
* Attitude adjustment energy scales with pitch angle.
* Hydrodynamic efficiency affects required time per profile, not instantaneous power.

**Key Insight**
Energy efficiency emerges from **trajectory shaping**, not propulsion.

---

### 3.5 Structural Mechanics

* Bending deformation scales with:
  [
  \delta \sim \frac{F L^3}{E I}
  ]

Small deformation ratios indicate stiffness adequacy, but not fatigue safety.

---

## 4. Interdependency Map

| Change          | Primary Benefit     | Secondary Cost               |
| --------------- | ------------------- | ---------------------------- |
| Increase γ      | Higher lift         | Higher drag, lower stability |
| Increase β      | Lower drag          | Reduced stability margin     |
| Maximize ηv     | Faster depth change | Larger pitch moment          |
| Minimize ηe     | Endurance           | Reduced maneuver margin      |
| Modular count ↑ | Shape fidelity      | Mass and complexity          |

---

## 5. Failure and Regime-Break Scenarios

* **Unsteady ocean currents** invalidate steady CFD assumptions.
* **Rapid morphing** introduces inertial and unmodeled hydrodynamic transients.
* **Skin wrinkling or delamination** alters effective hydrodynamic shape.
* **Depth-dependent material behavior** changes stiffness and sealing.
* **Biofouling** alters drag and lift distributions.

---

## 6. Design Tradeoffs Revealed

* Adaptability is gained by trading:

  * Structural simplicity
  * Modeling certainty
  * Energy predictability
* Morphing primarily improves **mission-level performance envelopes**, not point efficiency.

---

## 7. What the Paper Proves vs Assumes

### Strongly Supported

* γ and β materially affect lift, drag, and pitch moment.
* Multi-objective optimization is necessary due to inherent tradeoffs.
* Modular morphing can outperform fixed wings in adaptability.

### Assumed but Unverified

* Structural durability under cyclic loading
* Hydrodynamic behavior during active morphing
* Long-term energy cost of morphing actuation
* Performance under disturbed flow conditions

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### 1.1 Regulatory and Operational Context

* Inland shipping is assumed to be a **material contributor to regional air pollution**, justifying electrification.
* Policy instruments such as **DECA, EEDI, SEEMP, and EEOI** are assumed to be effective drivers of technology adoption.
* All-electric ships are assumed to be a **long-term dominant solution** rather than a transitional technology.

**Consequence**
The study implicitly optimizes within a regulatory future where zero-emission operation is mandatory rather than optional.

---

### 1.2 Ship Type and Mission Profile

* The benchmark vessel is:

  * A **low-speed inland tourist ship**
  * Short-route, schedule-constrained
  * Operating primarily in **normal inland waterways**
* Maximum speed, endurance, and voyage length are fixed at:

  * 18 km/h
  * 8 h
  * 100 km

**Consequence**
Results are not intended to extrapolate to high-speed craft, deep-sea vessels, or highly irregular duty cycles.

---

### 1.3 Energy System Architecture

* The ship is powered by:

  * **Lithium iron phosphate (LFP) batteries**
  * A **photovoltaic system** mounted on available deck area
* The electrical architecture is:

  * DC battery → DC-DC boost → DC-AC inverter → PMSM → FPP
* Two propulsion motors and propellers are assumed symmetric.

**Consequence**
Hybrid architectures with generators, fuel cells, or variable pitch propellers are explicitly excluded.

---

### 1.4 Modeling Choices

* Electrical subsystems are modeled using **dimensionless data-driven models**.
* Hull forces and ship motion are modeled using **first-principles hydrodynamics**.
* Battery behavior is represented using a **second-order RC equivalent circuit**.
* PV generation is modeled via a **single-diode equivalent circuit**.
* Ship maneuvering is limited to **3 degrees of freedom**: surge, sway, yaw.

**Consequence**
Internal electrochemical, thermal, and structural dynamics are deliberately abstracted to preserve computational efficiency.

---

### 1.5 Verification Criteria

* Model validity is asserted when:

  * Component-level errors are within **5 %**
  * Integrated system errors are within **10 %**
* Similarity is established using **Froude number equivalence**.

**Consequence**
The model is calibrated for trend fidelity, not high-frequency transient accuracy.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### 2.1 Battery Physics Simplification

* Battery degradation mechanisms are ignored:

  * Capacity fade
  * Internal resistance growth
  * Temperature-dependent aging
* Coulomb counting is assumed sufficient for SOC estimation.

**Evidence**
No thermal or aging state variables appear in the energy management logic.

---

### 2.2 Thermal and Safety Margins

* Electrical components are assumed to:

  * Remain within thermal limits
  * Avoid derating under high load or low SOC
* Fire, venting, and thermal runaway risks are not modeled.

**Hidden Risk**
Energy-optimal strategies may push systems closer to unmodeled safety boundaries.

---

### 2.3 Photovoltaic Reliability

* PV performance degradation due to:

  * Fouling
  * Mechanical vibration
  * Long-term erosion
    is ignored.

**Implication**
Seasonal variability is modeled, but lifecycle variability is not.

---

### 2.4 Hydrodynamic Linearity

* Resistance and maneuvering models assume:

  * No wave-induced added resistance
  * No squat or bank effects
  * Limited shallow-water interaction

**Contradiction**
Later conclusions acknowledge the model is not valid for shallow or restricted waterways.

---

### 2.5 Control System Idealization

* PID controllers are assumed:

  * Perfectly tuned
  * Free of actuator saturation beyond rudder angle limits
* Control energy consumption is negligible.

---

### 2.6 Human Factors

* Crew behavior is abstracted into predefined maneuver sequences.
* No stochastic decision-making or delayed response is considered.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### 3.1 Energy Conservation

[
E_{\text{battery}} + E_{\text{PV}} = E_{\text{propulsion}} + E_{\text{loss}} + E_{\text{residual}}
]

This balance underpins all voyage energy density calculations.

**Limit**
Loss terms are lumped and not state-dependent beyond SOC.

---

### 3.2 Electrical Circuit Laws

* Kirchhoff’s laws govern:

  * Battery equivalent circuits
  * DC-DC and DC-AC conversion
* Switching behavior is time-averaged.

**Regime**
Valid when switching frequency ≫ system mechanical time constants.

---

### 3.3 Motor Electromechanics

PMSM torque:
[
T_e = \frac{3}{2} P \left[\lambda_{af} i_q + (L_d - L_q) i_d i_q \right]
]

**Implication**
Torque production depends directly on current control quality and inverter stability.

---

### 3.4 Propeller Momentum Theory

Thrust and torque derived from open-water characteristics:
[
T, Q = f(J)
]

**Assumption**
Wake interaction, cavitation, and unsteady inflow are negligible.

---

### 3.5 Ship Resistance Physics

* Frictional resistance ∝ Reynolds number
* Residual resistance ∝ Froude number

**Key Dependency**
Environmental flow modifies resistance through relative velocity:
[
V_s = V_a \pm V_{\text{water}}
]

---

### 3.6 Rigid-Body Dynamics

Surge–sway–yaw equations enforce Newton’s second law.

**Boundary**
Heave, roll, and pitch are excluded.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Subsystems

* **Water speed ↔ propulsion power ↔ battery current**
  Higher upstream velocity increases electrical stress nonlinearly.
* **Maneuvering ↔ power factor stability**
  Turning induces reactive power fluctuations.
* **Speed ↔ schedule adherence ↔ energy density**
  Faster travel reduces time but increases energy per km.
* **PV output ↔ voyage profile**
  Low-speed, long-duration segments maximize PV contribution.
* **Control actions ↔ electrical grid stability**
  Acceleration phases dominate power quality risk.

---

| --------------------------- | ----------------------------------- |
| Constant battery parameters | SOC and voltage predictions drift   |

| Calm water resistance       | Energy consumption underpredicted   |
| Ideal PV availability       | Overestimation of energy savings    |
| Stable power factor         | Increased harmonic stress           |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* Accelerated battery aging due to high C-rate during maneuvers
* Increased inverter losses during frequent acceleration
* Rudder-induced electrical oscillations under high flow velocity
* Overconfidence in negative energy density cases if PV output is transiently overestimated
* Loss of control margin under compounded upstream flow and turning

### What Is Not Optimized

* Battery lifespan
* Component fatigue

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* System parameters are time-invariant over a voyage.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

These are directly stated or unambiguously implied in the text.

### Geometry and Structural Configuration

* Floating modules are **simple box-type barges** with identical geometry.
* Modules are connected via **idealized hinge joints** that allow free rotation about the hinge axis.
* Gap distance between modules is fixed at **0.1 m**, assumed sufficient to avoid contact.
* Only **one-, two-, and three-module** configurations are considered.

### Materials and Structural Properties

* Upper plate is **Aluminum 6061**, providing vertical bending stiffness.
* Lower plate is **polyethylene foam**, providing buoyancy.
* Materials are treated as **linearly elastic**, with constant Young’s modulus.
* Bonding between aluminum and foam is assumed to remain intact during testing.

### Hydrodynamic Environment

* Waves are **regular, head-on, long-crested waves**.
* Linear wave theory is assumed at generation.
* Water depth is constant at **4 m**, with no depth-induced nonlinearities.
* Reflections are minimized and considered negligible after stabilization.

### Mooring System

* Moorings are **nylon ropes**, symmetric, pretensioned, and attached at equal elevations.
* Mooring dynamics are assumed to mainly affect **low-frequency motions**, which are excluded.
* Moorings are not designed to significantly constrain first-order wave-frequency motions.

### Measurement and Data Processing

* Motions are decomposed using **FFT**, with focus on **first-order harmonics** for RAOs.
* Higher harmonics are explicitly considered only for hinge forces.
* Transient behavior before 60 to 100 s is excluded from analysis.

---

## 2. Hidden or Implicit Assumptions

These assumptions are not stated but are necessary for the analysis to hold.

### Structural Idealizations

* **Perfect hinge behavior** with no friction, backlash, clearance, or wear.
* Hinges transmit forces but negligible bending moments.
* No local stress concentration or plasticity at hinge pins or brackets.

### Material Behavior

* Aluminum and polyethylene are assumed **homogeneous and isotropic**.
* No rate dependence, creep, or viscoelastic effects in polyethylene.
* No degradation due to cyclic loading or water absorption.

### Manufacturing and Assembly

* Modules are assumed **geometrically identical** within tight tolerances.
* Alignment errors between modules are negligible.
* Sensor placement does not locally stiffen or weaken the structure.

### Environmental Variability

* No temperature variation affecting material stiffness or buoyancy.
* No currents, wind, or wave directional spreading.
* No biofouling, corrosion, or surface roughness effects.

### Load Predictability

* Wave loading is assumed **repeatable and stationary** over the test duration.
* Load paths are assumed consistent across wave cycles.
* Structural response is assumed weakly nonlinear except at hinges.

### Scaling and Representativeness

* Froude scaling is implicitly assumed valid.
* Structural damping and material damping scale appropriately.
* Results are assumed transferable to full scale despite connector fatigue being scale-sensitive.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### Linear Wave Theory

**Law**

* Small-amplitude wave theory governs free-surface elevation and pressure fields.

**Application**

* Used to normalize responses and interpret wavelength dependence.

**Validity Regime**

* Valid for small wave steepness and non-breaking waves.

**Boundary**

* Violated locally by diffraction, radiation, and connector nonlinearities.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Difference-frequency loads excite low-frequency surge via moorings.
* Sum-frequency components excite higher harmonic hinge forces.

---

### Rigid Body Dynamics

**Law**

* Newton–Euler equations for 6-DOF motion.

**Application**

* Surge, heave, and pitch RAOs of individual modules.

**Validity Regime**

* Valid when elastic deformation is small relative to rigid motion.

**Boundary**

* Competes with structural bending when wavelength approaches module length.

---

### Euler–Bernoulli Beam Theory

**Law**

* Bending governed by
  ( EI \frac{d^4 w}{dx^4} = q(x,t) )

**Application**

* Interpretation of hogging and sagging.
* Maximum deformation at mid-length when wavelength ≈ module length.

**Validity Regime**

* Small deflections, linear elasticity.

**Boundary**

* Connector constraints introduce discontinuities not captured by classical beam theory.

---

### Hydroelastic Coupling

**Law**

* Structural deformation modifies hydrodynamic pressure field, and vice versa.

**Application**

* Explains why middle module has low motion but high deformation.

**Emergent Effects**

* Constraint-induced bending amplification.
* Phase differences between modules generate internal stress.

---

### Fatigue and Cyclic Loading

**Law**

* Damage accumulation depends on stress range and cycle count.

**Application**

* High-order harmonics in hinge forces increase fatigue risk.

**Boundary**

* Fatigue not quantified but implicitly critical.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Wave diffraction** alters pressure distribution, increasing bending moments.
* **Hydroelasticity** couples rigid body motion suppression with deformation amplification.
* **Hinge kinematics** reduce global pitch but increase local stress cycles.
* **Mooring dynamics** introduce secondary frequencies affecting surge response.

These interactions explain why adding modules reduces surge but increases connector fatigue risk.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Assumption Violated      | Consequence                                                     |
| ------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Perfect hinge rotation   | Additional bending moments, invalid Euler–Bernoulli assumptions |
| Linear material behavior | Underprediction of deformation and fatigue                      |
| Negligible damping       | Overprediction of resonance peaks                               |
| No clearance or backlash | Missed impact loads and impulse forces                          |
| Head waves only          | Results invalid for oblique or quartering seas                  |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

### Connector-Driven Failures

* High-frequency harmonic forces lead to **fatigue cracking**.
* Clearance growth causes **impact loading**.
* Pin wear induces **nonlinear stiffness jumps**.

### Structural Failures

* Middle module experiences **constraint-induced overbending**.
* Local yielding at aluminum–foam interface.
* Delamination due to cyclic shear.

### System-Level Failures

* Mooring fatigue due to ignored low-frequency drift.
* Progressive misalignment of modules over time.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

### Material Choice

* Aluminum chosen for stiffness-to-weight ratio, not corrosion resistance or fatigue life.
* Polyethylene chosen for buoyancy, not long-term creep resistance.

### Geometry

* Box shape simplifies hydrodynamics but exaggerates bending compared to pontoon shapes.

### Connector Design

* Hinges optimized for motion decoupling, not load attenuation.
* Fatigue life implicitly sacrificed for kinematic simplicity.

### What Was Not Optimized

* Oblique wave response.
* Extreme or survival sea states.
* Connector redundancy or fail-safe behavior.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Modules interact hydrodynamically via **radiation and diffraction**.
* Hinges act as both kinematic constraints and force transmitters.
* Moorings provide weak global restoring but strong low-frequency coupling.
* No active control or damping is present.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* No accumulation of fatigue damage over test duration.
* No stiffness degradation of hinges.
* No change in mooring pretension.
* No sensor drift or calibration loss.

In real deployments, these assumptions would be violated early in service life.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

### Implied Safety Factors

* Linear elastic material limits.
* Ignoring extreme waves.
* Short test durations.

### Insufficient Margins

* Connector fatigue resistance.
* Sensitivity to higher harmonics.
* Lack of redundancy in load paths.

### Single-Point Failures

* Failure of one hinge fundamentally alters system dynamics.
* Loss of one mooring changes surge natural period.

---

## 11. Design vs Operative Regime

### Design Regime

* Regular head waves.
* Linear to weakly nonlinear response.
* Short-term operation.

### Likely Operative Regime

* Directional, irregular seas.
* Combined wave, wind, and current loading.
* Long-term cyclic degradation.

### Robustness Assessment

* Hydrodynamic conclusions are moderately robust.
* Connector force and fatigue conclusions are **highly sensitive** to off-nominal conditions.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### Structural Idealization

* The deep-sea mining riser is modeled as a **slender Euler–Bernoulli beam**.
* **Shear deformation and torsion are neglected**, implying a high slenderness ratio and bending-dominated response.
* The riser is **vertically suspended**, with:

  * **Top boundary**: hinged with prescribed surge motion.
  * **Bottom boundary**: free, with a **lumped intermediate warehouse mass**.
* The intermediate warehouse is treated as a **concentrated mass** affecting axial tension and boundary dynamics.

### Flow and Excitation

* The inflow consists of:

  * A **steady uniform current**.
  * A **harmonic oscillatory component** induced by vessel surge.
* Relative velocity is obtained via **linear superposition** of current and surge velocity.
* Vessel surge is modeled as **pure sinusoidal motion** with fixed amplitude and period.

### VIV Modeling

* Hydrodynamic forces are represented using a **forced van der Pol wake oscillator model**.
* Wake dynamics are assumed to be captured by **fixed empirical coefficients**.
* VIV is decomposed into **cross-flow (CF)** and **in-line (IL)** directions.

### Mathematical Treatment

* Modal decomposition is applied.
* Structural coefficients vary slowly along the length, justifying **WKB approximation**.
* Multi-mode structural response is retained.
* Time integration is performed using **4th-order Runge–Kutta**.

### Validation

* Numerical results are validated against:

  * Steady uniform flow experiments.
  * Oscillatory flow experiments.
* Agreement is judged primarily on **RMS amplitudes, trends, and dominant frequencies**.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### Structural and Material Assumptions

* Material behavior is **linear elastic**, with no yielding or plastic hinge formation.
* No **material damping degradation** or fatigue stiffness reduction occurs.
* The riser is **geometrically straight**, perfectly vertical, and free of initial curvature.
* Axial tension variation due to large deflection is neglected.

### Boundary Condition Idealization

* The bottom warehouse provides **mass but no rotational stiffness**.
* No impact, clearance, or contact occurs between the warehouse and seabed or buffer systems.
* The top hinge transmits no bending moment and exhibits no friction.

### Hydrodynamic Assumptions

* Wake oscillator parameters are **universal**, unaffected by:

  * Reynolds number variation.
  * Oscillatory inflow reversal.
  * Wake memory or turbulence intermittency.
* Vortex shedding is assumed to remain **coherent and quasi-two-dimensional**.

### Flow Regime

* Flow is assumed subcritical, non-breaking, and free of turbulence modulation by surface waves.
* No current shear, stratification, or directional veering is considered.

### Scaling and Representativeness

* Empirical coefficients calibrated on laboratory-scale cylinders are assumed valid for **deep-sea mining risers**.
* KC number is assumed sufficient to characterize oscillatory flow effects.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### Structural Dynamics of Slender Beams

**Law**

* Euler–Bernoulli beam equation
  ( EI w'''' + T(z) w'' + m \ddot{w} + c \dot{w} = F_{fluid} )

**Application**

* Governs modal shapes, frequencies, and deformation patterns.
* Explains reduction in modal order with increased bottom mass.

**Validity Regime**

* Small curvature, small strains, bending-dominated response.

**Boundary of Validity**

* Violated under large deflections or tension-bending coupling.

---

### Vortex Shedding and Fluid–Structure Lock-In

**Law**

* Vortex shedding frequency proportional to relative velocity
  ( f_s = St \cdot U_r / D )

**Application**

* Determines lock-in regions and excitation bands.
* Explains intermittent excitation when surge modulates ( U_r ).

**Emergent Effects**

* Mode switching due to time-varying lock-in condition.
* Coexistence of multiple frequencies near dominant mode.

---

### Nonlinear Oscillator Dynamics

**Law**

* Forced van der Pol oscillator with structural feedback.

**Application**

* Models self-excited, amplitude-limited VIV.
* Explains continuous versus intermittent oscillations.

**Limitations**

* Cannot capture chaotic wake breakdown or broadband turbulence.
* Assumes single dominant wake frequency at each instant.

---

### Parametric and Boundary Excitation

**Law**

* Time-varying boundary motion introduces parametric excitation.

**Application**

* Vessel surge injects energy periodically.
* Explains synchronization of intermittent VIV period with surge period.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Frequency modulation.
* Subharmonic and superharmonic participation.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Surge-modulated relative velocity** governs wake excitation strength.
* **Wake excitation** interacts with **time-varying modal frequencies** due to bottom mass.
* **Dynamic boundary motion** alters lock-in conditions along the riser length.
* **Gravity and bottom mass** suppress IL motion while CF remains wake-dominated.

These couplings generate the observed **distinct and moderate stages** of intermittent VIV.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Failure Mapping

| Assumption Violated                | Consequence                                   |
| ---------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------- |
| Linear superposition of velocities | Underestimation of peak relative velocity     |
| Universal wake coefficients        | Incorrect prediction of intermittency onset   |
| Negligible torsion                 | Missed torsional VIV or mode coupling         |
| Ideal hinge behavior               | Additional stiffness alters modal transitions |
| No fatigue degradation             | Overestimation of long-term stability         |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

### Fatigue-Driven Failures

* Intermittent VIV causes **high stress range variability**, accelerating fatigue damage.
* Low-order frequencies synchronized with surge produce **low-cycle fatigue**.
* Mode switching introduces **non-stationary stress histories**.

### Boundary and Attachment Failures

* Bottom warehouse experiences combined gravity, inertia, and hydrodynamic loading.
* “8”-shaped trajectories imply **biaxial cyclic loading** at the attachment.

### System-Level Instabilities

* Surge-induced parametric excitation may trigger:

  * Mode jumping.
  * Local resonance amplification.
* Loss of motion compensation could cause runaway vibration.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

### Modeling Tradeoffs

* WKB chosen for efficiency over exact spatial resolution.
* Wake oscillator chosen over CFD to enable parametric studies.

### Design Tradeoffs

* Heavier intermediate warehouse reduces modal order but increases:

  * Axial tension.
  * Attachment loads.
* Surge mitigation reduces CF VIV but may amplify IL dynamics.

### What Is Not Optimized

* Extreme surge events.
* Coupled heave–surge–pitch vessel motion.
* Long-term fatigue life and crack propagation.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Top boundary motion acts as **energy injector** rather than passive constraint.
* Bottom mass acts as:

  * Dynamic absorber for some modes.
  * Stress concentrator for others.
* Riser acts as a **distributed transmission line** for boundary-induced energy.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution Assumptions

* No cumulative damage over multiple surge cycles.
* No drift in natural frequencies due to fatigue or wear.
* No degradation of wake coherence over time.

In reality, intermittent VIV is **more damaging** than continuous VIV due to repeated re-excitation.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

### Implicit Safety Margins

* Linear material response.
* Moderate KC numbers.
* Idealized boundary behavior.

### Insufficient Margins

* Fatigue resistance under intermittent excitation.
* Sensitivity to surge period tuning.
* Robustness to multi-directional vessel motion.

### Single-Point Failures

* Failure of motion compensation system.
* Detachment or stiffening of intermediate warehouse.
* Change in surge frequency near structural modes.

---

## 11. Design vs Operative Regime

### Design Regime

* Sinusoidal surge.
* Uniform current.
* Controlled KC numbers.

### Likely Operative Regime

* Irregular surge with broadband spectrum.
* Combined surge, heave, and pitch.
* Time-varying current profiles.

### Robustness Assessment

* Mode trend predictions are robust.
* **Intermittent VIV onset is highly sensitive** to real-world deviations.

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### Geological and Geomorphological Context

* Ambon Bay is treated as a **semi-enclosed basin** whose geometry is capable of supporting tsunami resonance.
* Submarine slopes along the northern bay are assumed to be **composed of weakly consolidated sediments**, similar to those measured in Palu Bay.
* Landslide initiation is assumed to occur primarily along **upper submarine slopes**.

### Landslide Triggering and Geometry

* Submarine landslides are assumed to be **gravity-driven slope failures**, not requiring explicit earthquake forcing.
* All unstable slopes with **Safety Factor < 1** are assumed capable of **simultaneous full-depth failure**.
* Landslide thickness ranges from **1–50 m**, determined directly from SHALSTAB soil depth input.

### Hydrodynamic Modeling

* Tsunami propagation is governed by **depth-averaged, non-dispersive shallow-water equations**.
* A **two-layer system** is sufficient to represent:

  * Upper layer: seawater
  * Lower layer: fluidized sediment
* Water and sediment layers are **immiscible** and remain stratified.
* Sediment behaves as a **depth-averaged granular fluid**, not a solid block or debris flow.

### Numerical and Spectral Analysis

* A **30-m grid resolution** adequately captures bathymetric controls on resonance.
* FFT-based spectral analysis is sufficient to identify dominant resonance periods.
* Resonance periods are assumed to be **governed primarily by basin geometry**, not by landslide rheology.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### Slope Stability and Geotechnical Assumptions

* SHALSTAB, originally designed for **subaerial rainfall-triggered landslides**, is assumed valid for **submarine slope failure**.
* Soil parameters derived from **Palu Bay** are assumed transferable to Ambon Bay.
* **Dynamic earthquake shaking**, cyclic pore pressure buildup, and strain-rate weakening are ignored.
* Failure is assumed **coherent and instantaneous**, not progressive or retrogressive.

### Landslide Dynamics

* Landslide motion is assumed to be **laminar and depth-averaged**, not turbulent.
* No fragmentation, block sliding, or entrainment of additional sediment occurs.
* Simultaneous failure of multiple zones assumes **perfect temporal correlation**, a worst-case but low-probability scenario.

### Hydrodynamics

* Shallow-water approximation neglects:

  * Vertical velocity structure
  * Frequency dispersion
  * Non-hydrostatic pressure
* Wave breaking, bore formation, and turbulence dissipation are ignored offshore.
* Open boundary conditions are assumed to be **perfectly non-reflective**.

### Spectral Interpretation

* FFT assumes **stationarity and linear superposition**, despite landslide tsunamis being impulsive and nonlinear.
* Identified spectral peaks are assumed to represent **physical resonance modes**, not numerical artifacts.
* Edge waves are inferred indirectly, without modal decomposition.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### Slope Stability Mechanics

**Law**

* Force equilibrium along a potential failure plane:
  [
  FS = \frac{c + (\gamma - \gamma_w m) z \cos^2\theta \tan\phi}{\gamma z \sin\theta \cos\theta}
  ]

**Application**

* Determines spatial likelihood of slope failure.

**Validity Regime**

* Shallow, translational failure
* Homogeneous soil
* Quasi-static loading

**Boundary of Validity**

* Violated under seismic acceleration or deep rotational slides.

---

### Mass Conservation and Momentum Transfer

**Law**

* Depth-averaged continuity and momentum equations govern both layers.

**Application**

* Landslide motion displaces water, generating tsunami waves.

**Emergent Effects**

* Negative wave at slide head
* Positive wave at slide toe

**Limitations**

* Cannot capture dispersive short-period waves or vertical jets.

---

### Long-Wave Tsunami Theory

**Law**

* Tsunami speed:
  [
  c = \sqrt{gh}
  ]

**Application**

* Governs propagation, reflection, and resonance.

**Validity**

* Wavelength ≫ water depth

**Boundary**

* Short-period oscillations are under-resolved.

---

### Basin Resonance

**Law**

* Natural period depends on basin length, width, and depth.

**Application**

* Explains prolonged oscillations and amplification.

**Emergent Phenomena**

* Partial standing waves
* Shelf-trapped edge waves

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Slope instability** determines landslide volume and geometry.
* **Landslide kinematics** set initial tsunami spectrum.
* **Basin geometry** selectively amplifies certain frequencies.
* **Edge wave trapping** couples shoreline geometry to resonance.
* **Non-dispersive hydrodynamics** bias energy toward longer periods.

These interactions explain why resonance periods remain stable even when landslide parameters vary.

| ---------------------------- | ------------------------------------- |

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

These are assumptions the authors either state directly or clearly rely on in formulas, simulations, or experimental setups.

### 1.1 Environment and Operating Regime

* Operation is primarily **calm, open water** with negligible current and wind during testing.
* Water is treated as **Newtonian, incompressible**, with constant density (ρ = 1000 kg/m³).
* Air density is fixed at 1.2 kg/m³.
* Free surface effects are modeled using **VOF**, implying well-defined phase separation and no entrained air bubbles or breaking waves beyond what VOF captures.
* Temperature effects are neglected except in the appendix justification of Reynolds number bounds.

**Consequence:** Any deviation toward turbulent wave breaking, debris-laden water, vegetation, or non-calm surface conditions lies outside the validated regime.

---

### 1.2 Structural and Material Assumptions

* The spherical shell, frame, and pendulum are treated as **rigid bodies**.
* Moments of inertia are constant and known precisely.
* Mass properties do not change with battery discharge, temperature, or water ingress.
* The shell is implicitly assumed **axisymmetric and homogeneous**.

**Consequence:** Local shell deformation, internal sloshing, or asymmetrical mass shifts are excluded from the dynamics.

---

### 1.3 Propulsion System Assumptions

* Propeller thrust from open-water tests can be transferred to the full robot via:

  * Direct CFD modeling, or
  * Virtual disk method without loss of fidelity.
* Differential thrust produces zero-radius turning without coupling losses.
* Propeller inflow is assumed sufficiently uniform despite proximity to hull and free surface.

**Consequence:** Interaction losses between propeller wake, hull curvature, and free surface are under-resolved in simplified thrust mapping.

---

### 1.4 Pendulum Propulsion Assumptions

* Only **longitudinal pendulum motion** matters during forward motion.
* Lateral dynamics are negligible.
* Effective rolling constraint: translational velocity is directly linked to shell rotation.
* Buoyancy and gravity cancel for shell and frame during surface rolling.

**Consequence:** This explicitly removes vertical heave, pitch, and coupled roll–yaw instabilities from the model.

---

### 1.5 Experimental Validation Assumptions

* Errors under 5 percent are treated as validation of model correctness.
* Measurement uncertainty is assumed smaller than modeling error at high RPM.
* Field tests are assumed representative of real deployments.

**Consequence:** Agreement is treated as structural correctness, not parameter compensation.

---

## 2. HIDDEN / IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

These are not stated but are necessary for the system to function as described.

### 2.1 Manufacturing and Assembly

* Tight tolerances on:

  * Shell roundness
  * Pendulum pivot alignment
  * Propeller shaft concentricity
* No imbalance-induced vibration significant enough to alter CFD or dynamics.

**Evidence:** Use of rigid-body Lagrangian dynamics and clean thrust curves implies negligible eccentric mass errors.

---

### 2.2 Maintenance and Degradation

* Bearings, seals, and pendulum joints retain performance over time.
* No fouling, corrosion, or sediment ingress affects drag or inertia.
* Propeller blades maintain geometry and surface finish.

**Evidence:** No degradation terms appear in energy or resistance models.

---

### 2.3 Human and Control Factors

* Operators or controllers can:

  * Precisely regulate throttle percentages
  * Maintain pendulum actuation within assumed limits
* No delay, backlash, or actuator saturation significantly alters dynamics.

**Evidence:** Control inputs are treated as ideal continuous signals.

---

### 2.4 Environmental Variability

* Sediment, aquatic vegetation, floating debris are excluded.
* Salinity effects on density and viscosity are ignored.
* No wave reflection or shoreline effects in field testing.

**Evidence:** Constant drag coefficients and fixed F_wave term.

---

### 2.5 Aging and Fatigue

* Repeated pendulum oscillations do not induce:

  * Frame fatigue
  * Shell microcracking
  * Joint loosening
* Structural eigenfrequencies are far from actuation frequencies.

**Evidence:** No fatigue life, damping evolution, or resonance analysis.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Newton–Euler Mechanics

**Law:** ΣF = m a, Στ = I α
**Application:** Overall motion resistance equated to thrust during steady motion.
**Validity Regime:** Quasi-steady, low-acceleration surface locomotion.
**Boundary:** Invalid during rapid throttle changes or wave impacts.

---

### 3.2 Lagrangian Mechanics

**Law:** d/dt(∂T/∂q̇) − ∂T/∂q + ∂V/∂q = Q
**Application:** Coupled shell–pendulum dynamics.
**Validity:** Rigid bodies, holonomic constraints.
**Failure Mode:** Breaks if shell slips significantly or deforms.

---

### 3.3 Hydrodynamic Drag Laws

**Law:** F_d = ½ ρ C_d A v²
**Application:** Viscous drag, air drag, residual resistance.
**Assumption:** Constant drag coefficients (Cf = Cp = 0.47).
**Boundary:** Invalid across Reynolds transitions or wave breaking.

---

### 3.4 Propeller Theory

**Law:** Thrust and torque coefficients depend on advance ratio J.
**Application:** Open-water efficiency curves.
**Boundary:** Violated near free surface, hull interference, or ventilation.

---

### 3.5 Free Surface Hydrodynamics

**Principle:** Wave-making resistance dominates bluff bodies.
**Application:** Explains energy loss and resistance trends.
**Second-Order Effects:** Wave reflection, spray, transient submergence.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Pendulum-induced rotation ↔ Boundary layer control**

  * Increases wall shear stress
  * Suppresses separation
  * Reduces pressure drag
* **Reduced pressure drag ↔ Increased friction drag**

  * Net resistance decreases
  * Energy consumption rises
* **Propeller thrust ↔ Free surface deformation**

  * Alters effective advance ratio
  * Changes wave-making resistance

These interactions explain why hybrid mode reduces resistance but increases energy usage.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTION FAILURES

| Violated Assumption      | Resulting Breakdown                  |
| ------------------------ | ------------------------------------ |
| Effective rolling        | Lagrangian constraint invalid        |
| Constant drag coeffs     | Resistance prediction error explodes |
| Rigid shell              | Energy dissipation into deformation  |
| Uniform propeller inflow | Thrust curves unusable               |
| Calm water               | Wave drag dominates unexpectedly     |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

### 6.1 Structural

* Pendulum joint fatigue leading to loss of torque symmetry.
* Shell cracking from cyclic shear stresses.
* Frame loosening altering inertia tensors.

---

### 6.2 Hydrodynamic

* Propeller ventilation near free surface.
* Flow reattachment failure at higher RPMs.
* Sudden drag crisis if surface roughness changes.

---

### 6.3 Control and System Integration

* Mode coupling instability at specific throttle ratios.
* Resonant oscillations between pendulum and wave-induced roll.
* Energy runaway if hybrid mode is misused at low throttle.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### 7.1 Why a Sphere

* Optimized for:

  * Rolling on land
  * Symmetric mass distribution
* Sacrifices:

  * Hydrodynamic efficiency
  * Payload transport economy

---

### 7.2 Why Pendulum + Propeller

* Pendulum chosen for:

  * Internal protection
  * Terrain adaptability
* Propeller chosen for:

  * Speed
* Not optimized for:

  * Energy efficiency
  * Long-duration cruising

---

### 7.3 Why Fixed Pendulum Throttle in Hybrid Tests

* Simplifies analysis
* Masks optimal control coupling possibilities
* Implies assumption of suboptimal but robust operation

---

* Load paths assume:

  * No torsional compliance between shell and frame
* Thermal dissipation of motors not modeled
* Battery voltage assumed stable
* No interaction with external manipulators or payload shifts

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No cumulative fatigue
* No bearing wear
* No performance drift
* No battery degradation
* No fouling accumulation

The system is treated as **time-invariant**, which is a strong assumption for field robotics.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Environmental and stochastic modeling

* **Seven long-term environmental variables** fully characterize the environment:
  significant wave height, spectral wave period, wave direction, wind speed, wind direction, surface current speed, current direction.
* **Joint probabilistic distribution** of these variables is prescribed and assumed known.
* **Wind–wave directional dependence** is explicitly assumed, with wind direction conditionally centered on wave direction.
* **Current is independent** of wave and wind processes.
* **Sea states are stationary** over 0.5–3 h durations.
* **Short-term wave process** is assumed:

  * Gaussian
  * Zero mean
  * Linear superposition of harmonic components
  * Governed by JONSWAP spectrum
* **Unidirectional waves** within each sea state.
* **Deepwater assumption** for wave kinematics with tanh(kd) ≈ 1.

### Structural and dynamic modeling

* Fully coupled **time-domain dynamic analysis** of vessel, moorings, and risers.
* Wave loads decomposed into:

  * Linear wave-frequency loads
  * Quadratic low-frequency loads
* Wave forces computed using **potential flow diffraction theory**.
* Line forces computed using **Morison’s equation**, implying:

  * Slender body assumption
  * Empirical drag and inertia coefficients
* **Dynamic wind effects neglected**, wind modeled as quasi-steady drag.
* Stress response extracted from **OrcaFlex simulations** is assumed ground truth for training and benchmarking.

### Reliability and fatigue modeling

* Failure defined via **threshold exceedance** for extreme response.
* Short-term responses across sea states are **statistically independent**.
* Annual failure probability derived from short-term probabilities via multiplication.
* Fatigue damage computed using:

  * Rainflow counting
  * Miner's linear damage rule
  * Single-slope S–N curve
* Mean stress effects corrected using **Goodman relation**.
* Non-environmental fatigue uncertainties lumped into a **lognormal multiplicative variable** with median 1 and COV 0.3.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Statistical and probabilistic structure

* **Correctness of joint distributions**
  The entire method presumes the specified JPDF is accurate in the tails. Any mis-specification propagates directly into reliability estimates.
* **Ergodicity**
  Time averages within a sea state are assumed representative of ensemble averages.
* **Stationarity across service life**
  Environmental statistics do not evolve over decades.
* **Independence between sea states**
  No persistence or clustering of storms is modeled.

### Metamodel assumptions

* **Generalization sufficiency**
  360 training sea states are assumed sufficient to generalize across a 7-dimensional stochastic domain.
* **Response smoothness**
  Structural response is assumed to vary smoothly with respect to environmental inputs, enabling interpolation by GE-NARX.
* **No regime switching**
  Structural behavior does not undergo qualitative changes outside the training envelope.
* **Single-output sufficiency**
  Predicting one response quantity does not omit coupled effects that could alter fatigue or extremes.

### Numerical and computational assumptions

* **Rosenblatt transformation fidelity**
  Mapping from normal space to physical space preserves dependence structure accurately.
* **MCMC mixing adequacy**
  Modified Metropolis Hastings chains sufficiently explore conditional failure regions.
* **Subset level probability optimality**
  Chosen conditional probability values ensure efficiency and convergence.

### Human and operational assumptions

* Model calibration, parameter selection, and DOE tuning are assumed to be performed by **expert practitioners**.
* No allowance for **operator error**, mis-parameterization, or incorrect boundary condition specification.

---

## 3. GOVERNING FIRST PRINCIPLES

### Linear wave theory

**Principle**
Small-amplitude gravity waves obey linear superposition.

**Governing relations**

* Wave elevation as Fourier sum
* Particle velocities from linear kinematics

**Validity regime**

* Small wave steepness
* No wave breaking
* Deep water

**Boundary of validity**

* Severe storms
* Shallow water
* Nonlinear wave–wave interactions

**Second-order effects**

* Ignored bound harmonics
* Ignored wave drift forces beyond quadratic LF modeling

---

### Structural dynamics

**Principle**
Newton’s second law applied to a coupled multi-body system.

**Form**
M ẍ + C ẋ + K x = F(t)

**Validity**

* Lumped parameter representation
* Linear stiffness for most components

**Boundary violations**

* Geometric nonlinearity in large riser excursions
* Contact, slack–taut transitions

---

### Fluid–structure interaction

**Principle**
Morison equation balances inertial and drag forces.

**Form**
F = ρ C_M A u̇ + ½ ρ C_D D |u|u

**Validity**

* Slender members
* Low Keulegan–Carpenter numbers
* Empirical coefficients constant

**Failure modes**

* Vortex shedding
* Reynolds-number dependence
* Directional flow complexity

---

### Fatigue damage accumulation

**Principle**
Miner’s rule assumes linear cumulative damage.

**Form**
D = Σ (n_i / N_i)

**Validity**

* No load sequence effects
* Constant material properties

**Known violations**

* Load interaction effects
* Mean stress interaction beyond Goodman correction

---

### Reliability theory

**Principle**
Failure probability is a multidimensional probability integral.

**Form**
P_f = ∫ I[g(X) ≤ 0] f_X(X) dX

**SS regime**

* Rare event probabilities
* High dimensionality

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Wave directionality ↔ structural modal excitation**
  Direction determines which rigid-body modes dominate, altering stress paths.
* **Short-term stochasticity ↔ fatigue accumulation**
  Rare large cycles disproportionately influence damage.
* **Thermodynamic independence assumption ↔ fatigue life**
  Environmental correlation assumptions directly shape damage distributions.
* **Metamodel error ↔ tail estimation**
  Small bias in response prediction amplifies exponentially in extreme tails.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE FAILURE LINKS

| Violated Assumption        | Consequence                        |
| -------------------------- | ---------------------------------- |
| Gaussian wave process      | Incorrect short-term extremes      |
| Stationary environment     | Underprediction of lifetime damage |
| Linear wave theory         | Missed nonlinear crest loads       |
| Independence of sea states | Underestimated clustering risk     |
| Accurate DOE coverage      | Metamodel extrapolation errors     |
| Miner’s rule validity      | Misestimated fatigue life          |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Tail under-sampling in DOE**
  Leads to non-conservative reliability estimates.
* **Directional mis-specification**
  Produces incorrect dominant load cases.
* **Mean stress correction simplification**
  Underestimates damage for asymmetric cycles.
* **Neglected dynamic wind loading**
  Critical for wind-dominated systems.
* **Metamodel blind spots**
  Rare resonance conditions never trained.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Material modeling**

  * Optimized for seawater with cathodic protection
  * Not optimized for corrosion–fatigue coupling
* **Geometry**

  * Slender risers favor Morison modeling
  * Not optimized for vortex-induced vibration
* **DOE limits**

  * Training cost constrains tail resolution
* **Computational trade**

  * Accuracy sacrificed for speed in metamodeling

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Environmental loads enter as **kinematic boundary conditions**.
* Structural response feeds back into relative velocity terms.
* No feedback from structural damage to stiffness or damping.
* No coupling to inspection or monitoring systems despite digital-twin claims.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No stiffness degradation over time.
* No corrosion growth.
* No mooring creep.
* Fatigue damage accumulation assumed linear and memoryless.
* Environmental statistics frozen over service life.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Safety factors embedded via:

  * Design fatigue factor
  * Conservative S–N parameters
* No explicit robustness margin for:

  * Metamodel error
  * Distributional uncertainty
* Single-point failure risk:

  * Incorrect joint distribution
  * DOE omission of critical regime

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed for

* Stationary statistics
* Gaussian waves
* Known distributions
* Smooth response behavior

### Likely operating reality

* Nonstationary climate
* Directional persistence
* Model uncertainty
* Damage-induced response changes

### Robustness

* Strong to dimensionality
* Weak to distributional error
* Weak to physics regime shifts

## 1. Explicit Assumptions

### Ice loading and data treatment

* Ice loads are **random, peak-dominated processes** suitable for probabilistic modeling.
* Only **load peaks above 10 kN** are considered representative of ice interaction.
* Loads below 10 kN are assumed to be dominated by **hydrodynamic and wave effects**.
* Ice load peaks extracted via a **Rayleigh separation algorithm** are assumed to represent independent events.
* Short-term load peaks are suitable as **parent distributions** for long-term extreme value analysis.

### Measurement and instrumentation

* Shear strain gauges on selected hull frames provide **accurate total ice force per frame**.
* Conversion from strain to force is assumed **linear and calibrated** following Suominen et al.
* Measurement noise at high loads is negligible compared to signal.

### Environmental and operational conditions

* Selected cases are assumed to have:

  * Approximately constant ice concentration
  * Approximately constant ship speed and heading
  * Representative ice thickness and floe size categories
* Visual ice observations are assumed sufficiently accurate for statistical correlation.

### Statistical modeling

* Exponential, Weibull, lognormal, truncated, and mixture distributions are assumed valid candidate models.
* Maximum Likelihood Estimation is assumed appropriate when solutions exist.
* Gumbel extreme value theory is assumed valid for long-term maxima.
* Monte Carlo sampling is assumed to converge to correct extreme statistics.

---

## 2. Hidden and Implicit Assumptions

### Measurement physics

* **Frame load is assumed spatially lumped**, ignoring local pressure distributions and stress concentrations.
* Hull steel is implicitly assumed **linear elastic**, isotropic, and homogeneous under peak loads.
* No allowance for **dynamic amplification**, strain-rate effects, or sensor saturation during high-energy impacts.

### Contact mechanics

* Ice–hull interaction is implicitly treated as a sequence of **statistically independent contacts**.
* No explicit modeling of **contact duration, impact velocity, or contact area evolution**.
* Ice failure modes such as crushing, bending, splitting, and ridge interaction are not explicitly parameterized, only inferred statistically.

### Thresholding

* The 10 kN threshold is assumed to:

  * Fully separate wave loads from ice loads
  * Not bias the tail of the ice load distribution
* Implicit assumption that **truncation does not remove physically meaningful low-energy ice events**.

### Exposure and scaling

* Long-term exposure is assumed to scale purely with **number of contacts**, not with:

  * Seasonal clustering
  * Autocorrelation in ice fields
  * Operational strategy changes over vessel lifetime
* Fatigue and extreme loading are treated as **separable problems**, despite sharing the same load population.

### Mixture distributions

* Mixture components are implicitly assumed to correspond to **distinct physical interaction regimes**, even though no direct physical labeling is performed.
* EM convergence is assumed to identify **physically meaningful components**, not numerical artifacts.

---

## 3. Governing First Principles

### Contact and structural mechanics

* **Force equilibrium**
  Ice load equals the reaction force required to arrest or redirect ice momentum and fracture resistance.

* **Linear elasticity (Hooke’s Law)**
  Valid for converting measured strain to force:
  [
  \sigma = E \varepsilon
  ]
  Assumes elastic regime, no yielding at measured peaks.

* **Fracture mechanics of ice**

  * Ice failure governed by flexural strength, compressive strength, and fracture toughness.
  * Strongly strain-rate and temperature dependent, neither explicitly modeled.

### Probability theory

* **Extreme value theory**

  * Maxima of iid samples converge to Gumbel distribution under broad conditions.
  * Assumes independence and identical distribution, marginally satisfied here.

* **Truncation theory**

  * Observed distribution X is a conditional distribution of the true process Y.
  * Truncated PDFs preserve parent parameters if truncation is properly accounted for.

* **Mixture models**

  * Observed PDF is a convex combination of latent PDFs:
    [
    f(x) = \sum w_i f_i(x)
    ]
  * Assumes latent process switching without memory.

### Monte Carlo sampling

* Law of large numbers ensures convergence of simulated extremes.
* Implicit assumption that **n is sufficiently large** and samples are independent.

---

## 4. Interdependencies Between Principles

* **Thresholding ↔ Statistical tail behavior**
  Truncation changes the effective hazard rate, directly affecting extreme predictions.

* **Mixture modeling ↔ Ice mechanics**
  Multiple exponential components likely map to different failure mechanisms, such as bending versus crushing.

* **Elastic response ↔ Load statistics**
  If hull response becomes nonlinear at high loads, statistical inference based on elastic conversion breaks.

* **Extreme value convergence ↔ Distribution class**
  Weibull shape parameter < 1 causes E3 behavior, leading to increasingly flat extreme distributions with n.

---

## 5. Assumption–Principle Fragility Mapping

| Assumption violated         | Physical consequence            | Statistical failure          |
| --------------------------- | ------------------------------- | ---------------------------- |
| Loads are independent       | Contact clustering in dense ice | Extreme value theory invalid |
| Linear elasticity           | Local yielding or buckling      | Underestimated peak loads    |
| 10 kN threshold unbiased    | Loss of low-energy ice contacts | Distorted exposure modeling  |
| Stationary ice conditions   | Regime shifts in loading        | Invalid parent distribution  |
| Single frame representative | Load redistribution ignored     | Non-conservative design      |

---

## 6. Hidden Failure Modes

* **Tail underestimation** leading to structural under-design.
* **Over-conservative design** if truncated Weibull over-predicts extremes.
* **Sensor blind spots** during very high strain rates.
* **Mode-switching fatigue** where different ice mechanisms dominate at different times.
* **Exposure miscounting** leading to incorrect lifetime maxima.

---

## 7. Unstated Constraints and Tradeoffs

* Choice of Weibull and exponential families implies:

  * Preference for analytical tractability over physical fidelity.
* Use of frame-level loads implies:

  * Design optimization around **global strength**, not local plating failure.
* Avoidance of hypothesis testing implies:

  * Engineering judgment prioritized over statistical purity.

---

## 8. Boundary Conditions and System Context

* Loads are transmitted from ice to:

  * Hull plating
  * Frames
  * Longitudinal structure
* No explicit coupling to:

  * Global hull girder bending
  * Propulsion loads
  * Dynamic positioning or maneuvering forces
* Thermal effects on steel and ice are not modeled.

---

## 9. Temporal Evolution

* No explicit modeling of:

  * Corrosion
  * Fatigue crack initiation
  * Material embrittlement
* Assumes **time-invariant load statistics** across vessel life.
* Implicitly assumes no learning or behavioral adaptation by crew.

---

## 10. Safety and Robustness Margins

* Safety relies on:

  * Correct tail modeling
  * Conservative exposure estimates
* Single-point failures:

  * Wrong parent distribution choice
  * Misclassification of distribution class (E2 vs E3)
* No explicit robustness check against:

  * Out-of-distribution ice conditions
  * Ridged ice beyond sampled cases

---

## 11. Design vs Operative Regime

### Design regime

* Short-term, quasi-stationary ice conditions
* Elastic hull response
* iid load events

### Likely operative regime

* Clustered contacts in dense ice
* Mixed interaction mechanisms
* Long-term changes in hull condition and ice climate

**Robustness assessment**
The first-principles basis is sound, but the statistical framework is fragile to violations of independence, stationarity, and tail behavior. The paper correctly identifies that **distribution choice dominates long-term predictions**, which is the core systems-engineering risk.
