## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Plasma and exposure conditions

* **Deuterium plasma** in Pisces-RF is representative of fusion-relevant PMI conditions.
* Dominant ion species is **D⁺**, with negligible impurity concentration from the plasma source.
* **Ion impact energy** is controlled solely by target bias and sheath potential, with ions traversing the sheath collisionlessly.
* Ion energies in the range **40–250 eV** are sufficient to characterize sputtering behavior relevant to first-wall operation.
* Plasma is steady during exposure, with electron density and temperature measured upstream by a Langmuir probe and assumed valid at the target.

### Thermal assumptions

* Target temperature is **uniform laterally** and adequately represented by a thermocouple mounted at the rear surface.
* Front–back temperature gradient is small and quantified, and does not qualitatively affect retention or erosion mechanisms.
* During TPD, the **measured temperature accurately represents the bulk sample temperature**.

### Deuterium retention and release

* Retained deuterium is fully captured by **TPD up to 1200 K**.
* Contributions from desorption of species other than D₂ and HD are negligible.
* Retention is quantified as the **time-integrated desorption flux**, implicitly assuming no significant loss prior to TPD.
* Retention mechanisms are inferred from macroscopic observables without direct microstructural characterization.

### Erosion measurement

* Optical emission spectroscopy ratio **Cr I (425.4 nm) / D I (656.1 nm)** is proportional to instantaneous erosion rate.
* Normalization to D I fully compensates for plasma density, temperature, and window transmission variations.
* Single-energy mass-loss calibration is sufficient to scale the full spectroscopic erosion curve.
* Net erosion yield from mass loss can be converted to **gross erosion yield** via a re-deposition correction.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material and microstructure

* Commercially pure Cr (3N) is assumed to be **representative of fusion-relevant Cr surfaces**, despite known brittleness and impurity sensitivity.
* Polished surface roughness is assumed not to evolve significantly during plasma exposure in ways that alter retention or sputtering.
* No phase changes, recrystallization, or segregation occur within the exposure temperature range.

### Hydrogen transport physics

* Deuterium transport is implicitly governed by **implantation, diffusion, trapping, and thermal release**, with no hydride phase formation.
* Trap site populations are assumed to be stable and repeatable across samples.
* Weak fluence dependence of retention implies **trap saturation or near-surface limited uptake**, an inference not directly measured.

### Spectroscopy and erosion

* Excited Cr neutrals observed by OES originate predominantly from **physical sputtering**, not from thermal evaporation or chemical effects.
* Ionization of sputtered Cr neutrals is dominated by **electron-impact ionization**, neglecting charge exchange or metastable pathways.
* Sputtered neutrals follow a **cosine angular distribution**.
* Sputtering by re-deposited Cr ions is neglected.

### Re-deposition model

* Magnetic confinement of ionized sputtered Cr is perfect within the projected target cylinder.
* Ion gyro-motion is neglected, implying Larmor radii are small compared to system dimensions.
* Ionization mean-free path is spatially uniform and determined by local plasma parameters.
* Thompson energy distribution adequately represents sputtered neutral energies.

---

## 3. GOVERNING FIRST PRINCIPLES

### Plasma sheath acceleration

**Law**
Energy gained by ions traversing a collisionless sheath equals the sheath potential drop.

**Application**
Ion impact energy is set by target bias minus plasma potential.

**Validity regime**

* Low neutral pressure
* Mean free path ≫ sheath thickness

**Boundary violations**

* Elevated neutral pressure
* Charge-exchange within sheath

---

### Physical sputtering

**Law**
Momentum transfer from incident ions ejects surface atoms when energy exceeds binding energy.

**Models referenced**

* Binary Collision Approximation
* SDTrimSP predictions

**Validity**

* Elastic nuclear stopping dominates
* No chemical sputtering

**Second-order effects**

* Surface roughening
* Gas-induced yield suppression
* Near-surface implantation modifying binding energy

---

### Hydrogen isotope transport

**Law**
Fickian diffusion with trapping and detrapping kinetics.

**Implicit form**
∂C/∂t = D∇²C − Σ(trap terms)

**Validity**

* Dilute hydrogen concentration
* No hydride formation

**Failure modes**

* Trap evolution
* Stress-assisted diffusion
* Surface recombination limits

---

### Thermal desorption

**Law**
Arrhenius-type release governed by trap binding energy.

**Application**
TPD peak temperatures infer trap strengths.

**Boundary issues**

* Non-uniform heating
* Re-adsorption
* Surface recombination-limited release

---

### Ionization and re-deposition

**Law**
Exponential survival probability of neutrals with distance traveled before ionization.

P = 1 − exp(−s/λ)

**Validity**

* Single-step electron-impact ionization
* Constant plasma parameters

**Second-order effects**

* Magnetic mirroring
* Ion gyro-orbit displacement
* Multi-step ionization

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Retention ↔ temperature**
  Elevated temperature increases detrapping and diffusion to surface, reducing inventory.
* **High flux ↔ erosion yield**
  Dense plasma enhances re-deposition, reducing net erosion relative to BCA predictions.
* **Near-surface D ↔ sputtering**
  Gas inclusion modifies effective surface binding energy, suppressing yield.
* **Magnetic confinement ↔ re-deposition**
  Strong axial B field couples ionization physics to geometric capture probability.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption     | Consequence                        |
| ----------------------- | ---------------------------------- |
| Collisionless sheath    | Ion energy mischaracterized        |
| Uniform temperature     | Erroneous retention vs T trends    |
| Cosine sputter emission | Incorrect re-deposition fraction   |
| Negligible gyro-motion  | Overestimated re-deposition        |
| Stable trap population  | Misinterpreted saturation behavior |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Underestimated retention** if significant D is lost during post-exposure cooling.
* **Overcorrected erosion yield** if re-deposited Cr re-sputters.
* **Spectroscopic bias** if Cr excitation rates vary nonlinearly with plasma conditions.
* **Scaling failure** when extrapolating linear-device data to tokamak geometry.
* **Surface evolution masking mechanisms**, such as nano-morphology or sub-surface bubble formation.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Pure Cr choice**

  * Optimized for PMI clarity
  * Not representative of structural Cr alloys
* **Linear device geometry**

  * Enables diagnostics
  * Suppresses toroidal transport effects
* **Simplified re-deposition model**

  * Computationally efficient
  * Sacrifices kinetic fidelity
* **TPD upper limit**

  * Chosen for practicality
  * May miss ultra-deep traps

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Plasma parameters measured upstream assumed valid at target.
* No coupling between erosion and plasma impurity radiation modeled.
* Target treated as isolated sink without feedback on plasma composition.
* No mechanical stress evolution despite thermal cycling.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No cumulative damage across exposures.
* No microstructural evolution with fluence.
* No oxidation effects post-exposure.
* Retention behavior assumed time-invariant beyond exposure window.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Conservative interpretation of low retention.
* Re-deposition explicitly accounted for, improving robustness.
* No explicit uncertainty propagation from diagnostics to final yields.
* Single-point sensitivity to ionization mean-free path.

* High-flux, low-energy D plasma
* Actively cooled surfaces
* Strong axial magnetic confinement

### Likely reactor reality

* Mixed D–T plasma
* Transient heat loads
* Evolving surface chemistry
* Neutron damage coupling to PMI

### Robustness

* Strong for comparative screening of PFMs
* Weak for absolute lifetime prediction without neutron effects

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Reactor, mission, and operating regime

* The system is designed for the **EU-DEMO reactor**, operating beyond ITER with a target divertor lifetime of **2 full power years (fpy)**.
* The divertor cassette body lifetime is constrained primarily by **irradiation-induced embrittlement of EUROFER97**, characterized by DBTT shift.
* **Typical PWR coolant conditions** are assumed acceptable and beneficial for EUROFER97 under irradiation:

  * Coolant temperature: **295 °C**
  * Coolant pressure: **15.5 MPa**
* These operating conditions are assumed to mitigate embrittlement sufficiently to enable lifetime extension from 1.5 to 2 fpy.

### Structural and material assumptions

* **EUROFER97** is assumed to retain adequate elastic and yield properties at **350 °C**, with:

  * Yield stress Rp0.2,min ≈ **411 MPa**
  * Elastic modulus E ≈ **200 GPa**
* Material behavior is assumed **linear elastic** for all analyses presented.
* No inelastic deformation, creep, irradiation hardening, swelling, or fatigue damage is modeled.

### Design comparison framework

* Two cassette body configurations are assumed functionally equivalent in system role:

  1. Ribbed box chamber configuration
  2. Solid structure with circular channels
* The comparison is **preliminary**, qualitative, and not RCC-MRx compliant.
* Structural adequacy is judged primarily via **Von Mises stress relative to yield stress**.

### Loading assumptions

* Three load combinations are sufficient to characterize comparative performance:

  * LC-1: Dead weight + coolant pressure + preload + thermal expansion
  * LC-2: Thermal expansion only
  * LC-3: Full combination including EM and ferromagnetic loads from VDE
* Coolant pressure of **15.5 MPa** is assumed steady and uniform.
* Thermal loads are represented by a **uniform mean temperature of 350 °C**, derived from thermal maps.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Materials and irradiation

* **Neutron irradiation effects** are reduced to a single DPA threshold of **2.75 dpa at 6 fpy**, with no explicit modeling of:

  * Spatial gradients in damage
  * Irradiation creep
  * Synergistic irradiation–thermal effects
* EUROFER97 properties used are assumed valid **after irradiation**, despite known degradation and scatter.

### Structural mechanics

* Stress concentrations near constraints, noses, bonded contacts, and welds are treated as **non-physical artifacts** or secondary effects.
* Boundary conditions used in FEM models are assumed representative of real supports, despite their strong influence on stress localization.
* Buckling, plastic collapse, ratcheting, and fracture are implicitly assumed non-governing.

### Thermo-hydraulics

* Hydraulic performance is reduced to:

  * Pressure drop
  * Pumping power
* Flow instabilities, boiling margins, corrosion, erosion-corrosion, and water chemistry effects are not considered.
* Heat transfer coefficients are assumed sufficient to maintain EUROFER97 below **550 °C**, without margin analysis.

### Manufacturing

* Manufacturing feasibility is assumed achievable with future R&D, especially for:

  * Large-scale HIP of EUROFER97
  * Multi-step HIP cycles
  * Hybrid forged + HIP solutions
* Residual stresses from welding, HIP, brazing, or machining are neglected.
* Inspection, repairability, and in-service inspection constraints are not explicitly considered.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Pressure vessel mechanics

**Law**
Thin and thick-walled pressure vessel theory governed by equilibrium and elasticity.

**Application**

* Internal coolant pressure generates membrane and bending stresses.
* Solid structure reduces stress by distributing pressure loads more uniformly.

**Validity regime**

* Linear elastic response
* No plasticity or creep

**Boundary of validity**

* At 15.5 MPa and 350 °C, time-dependent creep may become non-negligible over fpy timescales.

---

### Thermal expansion and constraint

**Law**
Thermal strain ε = αΔT

**Application**

* Uniform temperature rise induces secondary stresses when constrained at supports and interfaces.

**Competing effects**

* Ribbed box: thinner shells, higher flexibility
* Solid structure: higher stiffness, higher thermal constraint stresses

---

### Electromagnetic forces

**Law**
Lorentz force density f = J × B and ferromagnetic body forces.

**Application**

* EUROFER97 magnetization induces additional loads during VDEs.

**Limitations**

* EM loads treated as static resultants at peak time.
* Dynamic amplification and inertial coupling neglected.

---

### Neutron shielding and damage

**Law**
Atomic displacement rate proportional to neutron flux and energy spectrum.

**Application**

* Shielding effectiveness assessed by dpa threshold in VV.

**Hidden coupling**

* Structural thickness, cooling channel layout, and shielding are tightly coupled.

---

### Fluid mechanics

**Law**
Darcy–Weisbach and pump power scaling with pressure drop and flow rate.

**Application**

* Solid structure dramatically reduces pressure drop via smoother circular channels.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Structural stiffness ↔ thermal stress**
  Higher stiffness in solid structure reduces pressure stress but increases thermal constraint stress.
* **Cooling channel geometry ↔ shielding**
  Larger uncooled volumes improve shielding but worsen thermal gradients.
* **Manufacturing method ↔ material properties**
  HIP and PM-HIP alter grain size, DBTT, fatigue resistance, and irradiation response.
* **Pressure increase ↔ embrittlement mitigation**
  Higher coolant temperature reduces DBTT but demands higher pressure, raising structural loads.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated          | Consequence                                           |
| ---------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------- |
| Linear elasticity only       | Underestimation of creep and ratcheting               |
| Uniform temperature          | Underprediction of thermal gradients and local strain |
| Neglect of irradiation creep | Non-conservative lifetime                             |
| Idealized supports           | Artificial stress relief or amplification             |
| Perfect HIP bonds            | Overestimated structural integrity                    |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Creep-fatigue interaction** in EUROFER97 under 350 °C, 15.5 MPa, and neutron flux.
* **Residual stress superposition** with operational loads in HIPed or welded joints.
* **Thermal striping** due to mixing of coolant streams at different temperatures.
* **Local embrittlement hotspots** near cooling channels where dpa gradients peak.
* **Inspection-limited crack growth**, especially in internal channels.

---

* Optimized for:

  * Known ITER heritage
  * Weld-based fabrication
* Sacrifices:

  * Hydraulic efficiency
  * Mass
  * Stress uniformity

* Optimized for:

  * Pressure containment
  * Hydraulic efficiency
* Sacrifices:

  * Manufacturing maturity

  * Thermal flexibility

### What is not optimized

* Maintainability and replaceability
* In-service inspection access

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Mission and operating environment

* The **ITER Test Blanket Module (TBM)** is assumed to be a valid **proxy for DEMO blanket modules** in terms of:

  * Tritium breeding and recovery
  * High-grade heat extraction
  * Structural operating conditions
* Operating conditions are explicitly assumed to be:

  * **High temperature:** 300–500 °C
  * **Neutron irradiation:** sufficient to induce swelling, helium production, and embrittlement
* Structural material must therefore resist:

  * Radiation-induced swelling
  * Helium embrittlement
  * Degradation of mechanical properties at elevated temperature

### Material choice

* **RAFM steel F82H** is assumed suitable as a primary structural material due to:

  * Superior swelling resistance
  * Good high-temperature thermal and mechanical properties
  * Weldability
* The normalized and tempered condition of F82H is assumed to be representative of in-service material state.

### Joining technologies

* **TIG welding** and **HIP joining** are assumed to be viable fabrication routes for TBM structures.
* Post-weld heat treatment (PWHT) at **720 °C for 90 min** is assumed sufficient to:

  * Reduce residual stress
  * Recover toughness and hardness degradation
* HIP conditions (**1100 °C, 100 MPa, 2 h**) are assumed sufficient to create a bonded joint representative of TBM fabrication.

### Mechanical testing

* Room-temperature and elevated-temperature tensile tests and Charpy impact tests are assumed sufficient to characterize joint performance.
* DBTT derived from Charpy tests is assumed to be a meaningful metric for fusion structural integrity.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Irradiation effects

* All mechanical tests are performed **without neutron irradiation**, implicitly assuming:

  * Relative trends between base metal, TIG welds, and HIP joints will persist under irradiation
  * Irradiation-induced helium and defect clustering do not fundamentally alter joint ranking
* Helium embrittlement effects at grain boundaries and interfaces are **not explicitly tested**.

### Microstructural stability

* Post-fabrication microstructures are assumed stable during service:

  * No further grain growth at 300–500 °C
  * No segregation or precipitation under neutron exposure
* Dissolution of TaC during HIP is implicitly accepted as unavoidable and manageable.

### Mechanical relevance

* **Room-temperature Charpy impact energy** is implicitly treated as relevant to service behavior, despite:

  * Service temperatures being far above DBTT
  * Fracture mechanisms under irradiation differing from unirradiated behavior
* Static tensile properties are assumed to dominate design, with:

  * No explicit treatment of creep
  * No fatigue or creep-fatigue interaction analysis

### Manufacturing scalability

* HIP joining of small laboratory blocks is implicitly assumed scalable to **full TBM dimensions**.
* Residual stresses from HIP and TIG are assumed removable or negligible after heat treatment.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### High-temperature deformation

**Principle**
Plastic flow and creep governed by dislocation motion, diffusion, and grain boundary sliding.

**Relevance**

* Above ~450 °C, strength decreases and ductility increases, as observed in tensile tests.
* Long-term TBM operation will inevitably enter creep regimes not captured here.

**Validity limit**

* Short-duration tensile tests do not represent long-term creep under stress and irradiation.

---

### Martensitic microstructure mechanics

**Principle**
Strength–toughness balance controlled by:

* Prior austenite grain (PAG) size
* Carbide dispersion (TaC, WC)
* Martensitic lath structure

**Application**

* TIG + PWHT refines grain size and recovers hardness.
* HIP dissolves TaC, leading to PAG coarsening.

**Second-order effects**

* Coarser PAG increases DBTT and reduces impact energy.
* Irradiation will further amplify grain-boundary sensitivity.

---

### Fracture mechanics and DBTT

**Principle**
DBTT reflects the competition between cleavage fracture and ductile void growth.

**Application**

* TIG weld metal exhibits lowest DBTT (−110 °C), indicating good toughness margin.
* HIP joints show severely degraded Charpy energy, implying brittle interface behavior.

**Limitations**

* DBTT measured without helium, which is known to shift DBTT upward dramatically.

---

### Diffusion and oxide formation

**Principle**
Residual oxygen leads to oxide formation at interfaces during high-temperature joining.

**Application**

* Second-phase particles at HIP interface attributed to oxide inclusions.
* Degassing temperature affects impact energy but not tensile strength.

**Hidden coupling**

* Oxide particles act as crack initiation sites under impact and irradiation.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **HIP temperature ↔ carbide dissolution ↔ grain growth ↔ toughness**
* **Degassing ↔ oxygen content ↔ oxide inclusions ↔ impact energy**
* **Grain size ↔ DBTT ↔ irradiation embrittlement sensitivity**
* **Joining method ↔ residual stress ↔ fracture behavior**

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated         | Physical consequence         |
| --------------------------- | ---------------------------- |
| No irradiation effects      | DBTT shift underestimated    |
| Grain growth acceptable     | Impact toughness collapse    |
| Oxide inclusions benign     | Interfacial brittle fracture |
| Tensile strength sufficient | Creep and fatigue ignored    |
| Lab-scale HIP scalable      | Large-scale defect risk      |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Helium-assisted intergranular fracture** at HIP interfaces.
* **Creep crack initiation** at oxide-decorated HIP boundaries.
* **Irradiation-accelerated DBTT shift** making HIP joints brittle at service temperature.
* **Residual stress + irradiation synergy** causing premature cracking.
* **Loss of fracture toughness margin** under off-normal thermal transients.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### TIG welding

* Pros:

  * Mature, controllable
  * Good toughness recovery after PWHT
* Cons:

  * HAZ complexity
  * Residual stress
  * Geometric constraints

### HIP joining

* Pros:

  * Geometric freedom
  * Potential for complex cooling channels
* Cons:

  * Severe impact toughness degradation
  * Oxide contamination
  * Uncertain irradiation behavior

### What is not optimized

* Creep rupture life
* Irradiation creep
* Fatigue and creep-fatigue
* In-service inspection and repair

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* TBM structures interface with:

  * First wall and side wall cooling channels
  * Helium coolant at high pressure
* Joining regions experience:

  * Multiaxial stress
  * Thermal gradients
  * Neutron flux gradients
* No feedback modeled between:

  * Damage accumulation and material properties
  * Crack initiation and tritium permeation

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* Microstructure assumed static after fabrication.
* No progressive grain growth during service.
* No accumulation of helium at joints.
* No cyclic degradation under plasma transients.

These assumptions are **physically weak** for RAFM steels in fusion environments.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* TIG welds retain:

  * Acceptable tensile strength
  * Favorable DBTT margin
* HIP joints show:

  * ~75% reduction in impact energy
  * Minimal margin against brittle failure
* No explicit safety factors applied for:

  * Irradiation uncertainty
  * Joint defects
  * Scale effects

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed regime

* Static mechanical testing
* Unirradiated material
* Short-term loading

### Actual operating regime

* Neutron irradiation with helium production
* Long-term creep and swelling
* Thermal cycling and transients

### Robustness assessment

* **TIG welding:** structurally conservative, technology-mature
* **HIP joining:** geometry-enabling but **mechanically fragile** without further R&D

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Mission and operating envelope

* The **EU-DEMO divertor cassette** is designed for **steady-state operation** under nominal plasma conditions.
* The divertor adopts a **dual cooling scheme**:

  * **Vertical Targets (VTs)**: low-temperature, high-performance cooling to survive peak heat fluxes up to **20 MW m⁻²**.
  * **Cassette Body (CB)**: high-temperature, high-pressure cooling to mitigate **irradiation-induced embrittlement of Eurofer97**.
* CB coolant operating conditions are explicitly fixed to **PWR-like conditions**:

  * Inlet temperature: **295 °C**
  * Inlet pressure: **15.5 MPa**
  * Target temperature rise: **ΔT = 33 °C**

### Materials and temperature limits

* **Eurofer97 maximum allowable temperature** is assumed to be **550 °C**.
* **TSB candidate materials** are limited to:

  * CuCrZr
  * SS 316 Ti
  * Eurofer97
* Material properties are taken from literature libraries and assumed **valid at irradiation-relevant temperatures**, without in-situ degradation modeling.

### CFD and modeling framework

* **Steady-state conjugate heat transfer (CHT)** is sufficient to assess divertor thermal-hydraulic performance.
* **ANSYS CFX (finite-volume)** with:

  * k-ω SST turbulence model
  * Wall functions
  * IAPWS-IF97 water properties
* **PFU internal swirl tapes** are modeled using the **Orthotropic Virtual Swirl Tape (OVST)** approximation.
* Radiation heat transfer is simplified as **radiation toward the vacuum vessel at 40 °C**.

### Acceptance criteria

The design is evaluated against predefined constraints:

* Eurofer97 temperature ≤ 550 °C
* Total pressure drop per circuit ≤ 1.4 MPa (legacy limit)
* Coolant saturation margin ≥ 20 °C (legacy limit)
* PFU axial velocity ≤ 16 m s⁻¹
* CHF margin ≥ 1.4

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Thermal–structural coupling

* Thermal hot spots are assumed **structurally acceptable** as long as Eurofer97 temperature remains below 550 °C, without explicit stress or creep coupling.
* No **thermal ratcheting**, **creep**, or **creep–fatigue interaction** is modeled despite long-term operation at ~350 °C bulk temperature.

### Steady-state dominance

* **Transient events** (ELMs, VDEs, power ramps, loss-of-flow accidents) are implicitly assumed not to govern design limits.
* CHF correlations are applied under **nominal steady heat flux**, not transient peaks.

### Fluid physics

* Single-phase water flow is assumed except where saturation margin becomes negative.
* Local vaporisation is identified but **not propagated** into flow instabilities, pressure oscillations, or heat-transfer collapse.

### Neutronics–thermal decoupling

* Nuclear heating distributions are prescribed inputs and assumed **independent of temperature-driven material changes**.
* Shielding effectiveness is assumed unaffected by temperature-induced deformation or swelling.

### Manufacturability and inspection

* Cooling circuit redesign to fix stagnation or hot spots is assumed feasible without:

  * Excessive manufacturing complexity
  * Loss of structural robustness
  * Inspection or maintenance penalties

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Energy conservation (CHT)

**Law**
[
\nabla \cdot (k \nabla T) + q''' = \rho c_p \mathbf{u} \cdot \nabla T
]

**Application**

* Conjugate heat transfer couples solid conduction with coolant convection.
* Hot spots arise where conduction paths are long or convection is weak.

**Boundary of validity**

* Assumes steady flow and material properties weakly dependent on temperature.

---

### Pressurised water thermodynamics

**Law**

* Saturation temperature increases strongly with pressure.

**Application**

* At **15.5 MPa**, water saturation temperature ≈ **345 °C**.
* With CB outlet temperature ≈ 328 °C, **maximum theoretical saturation margin ≈ 17 °C**, even with zero pressure drop.

**Implication**

* Legacy **20 °C saturation margin criterion is physically incompatible** with PWR-like operation.

---

### Turbulent forced convection

**Law**

* Heat transfer coefficient scales with Reynolds and Prandtl numbers.

**Application**

* High mass flow in VTs ensures large CHF margins.
* In CB and RPs, low velocities lead to stagnation and thermal runaway.

---

### Critical Heat Flux (CHF)

**Law**

* CHF occurs when vapor blankets the wall, collapsing heat transfer.

**Models used**

* Modified Tong-75 (PFUs)
* Groeneveld lookup tables (SL, RPs)

**Validity limits**

* Developed for steady conditions and well-defined flow regimes.
* Flow reversal and stagnation violate underlying assumptions.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **High coolant temperature ↔ embrittlement mitigation ↔ saturation margin collapse**
* **Complex CB geometry ↔ non-uniform flow ↔ hot spots ↔ vaporisation**
* **Material thermal conductivity ↔ nuclear heating ↔ peak structural temperature**
* **Flow stagnation ↔ CHF margin ↔ local boiling ↔ structural risk**

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated          | Physical consequence             |
| ---------------------------- | -------------------------------- |
| Steady single-phase flow     | Local boiling, flow oscillations |
| Legacy saturation margin     | Non-physical requirement         |
| Uniform cooling              | Thermal stress concentration     |
| Material properties constant | Underestimated creep risk        |
| CHF correlations applicable  | Invalid in stagnation zones      |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Local nucleate boiling → vapor locking** in RP channels with flow reversal.
* **Thermal creep accumulation** in Eurofer97 supports exceeding 550 °C.
* **Coupled thermal–structural failure** of passively cooled SL, RP, and TSB supports.
* **Margin erosion under transients**, where CHF margin collapses faster than predicted.
* **Design dead-ends**, where fixing cooling requires major geometric redesign.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Why high-temperature CB cooling?

* Reduces Eurofer97 DBTT shift under irradiation.
* Improves plant thermal efficiency and integration with blanket systems.

### What is sacrificed?

* Saturation margin
* Cooling robustness
* Geometric simplicity
* Passive safety margin

### TSB material tradeoffs

* **CuCrZr**: best thermal performance, poorest irradiation tolerance.
* **SS 316 Ti**: worst temperatures due to low conductivity.
* **Eurofer97**: consistent material system but tight thermal margin.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* CB interacts thermally and structurally with:

  * SL, RPs, NSs
  * Wishbone and outer rail
* Passively cooled supports form **thermal bottlenecks**.
* No feedback loop modeled between:

  * Temperature and material strength
  * Deformation and coolant distribution

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No progressive:

  * Creep strain
  * Swelling
  * Channel blockage
  * Oxidation or corrosion
* No degradation of CHF margin over lifetime.
* No accumulation of residual heat during off-normal events.

These assumptions are **non-conservative** for DEMO-scale lifetimes.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* VTs cooling circuit: **robust**, high margins.
* CB cooling circuit:

  * Pressure drop acceptable
  * Saturation and CHF margins **structurally fragile**
* Several acceptance criteria are shown to be **internally inconsistent** with new operating conditions.

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed regime

* Steady operation
* Nominal heat loads
* Fully forced convection
* No boiling

### Likely operating regime

* Local boiling in CB and RPs
* Thermal gradients driving creep
* Sensitivity to transients
* Degrading margins over time

### Robustness

* VTs: high
* CB: marginal and geometry-dependent


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Mission role of the WCLL BB

* The WCLL BB is assumed capable of simultaneously fulfilling three core DEMO missions:

  * **Tritium self-sufficiency**
  * **Neutron shielding of VV and TFC**
  * **High-grade heat recovery**
* The design targets **steady-state DEMO operation**, not pulsed ITER-like operation.

### Coolant and breeder choices

* **Pressurized water** is adopted as the structural coolant at:

  * Pressure: **15.5 MPa**
  * Temperature range: **295–328 °C**
* **PbLi eutectic** serves as:

  * Tritium breeder
  * Neutron multiplier
  * Tritium carrier to TER
* Lithium enrichment is fixed at **90% ⁶Li**.

### Structural material

* **EUROFER RAFM steel** is assumed to be the sole load-bearing structural material.
* A **maximum admissible EUROFER temperature of 550 °C** is treated as a hard design constraint.

### Architecture assumptions

* Single-module segment architecture is retained.
* Two independent water cooling circuits are assumed sufficient:

  * FW circuit
  * BZ circuit (via DWTs)
* **Double-Walled Tubes (DWTs)** are assumed to be the correct long-term solution for BZ cooling.

### Analysis framework

* Neutronics: **3D Monte Carlo (MCNP)** with reflective toroidal boundaries.
* Thermal–hydraulics:

  * Hybrid porous-media models for manifolds
  * CFD for local slice analyses
* PbLi is often **treated as a solid** in CFD for conservatism.
* Structural verification is performed using **RCC-MRx** rules.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Irradiation effects

* Material properties (EUROFER, CuCrZr, SS316L) are assumed **time-invariant**, despite:

  * dpa accumulation
  * Helium and hydrogen production
  * Irradiation creep
* EUROFER DBTT evolution is implicitly assumed manageable solely via temperature control.

### Thermal–mechanical decoupling

* Thermal, neutronic, MHD and structural analyses are mostly **weakly coupled**.
* Feedback loops such as:

  * Deformation altering coolant paths
  * Swelling affecting TBR
    are not modeled self-consistently.

### PbLi behavior

* PbLi is frequently modeled as:

  * Isothermal
  * Laminar
  * Conduction-dominated
* **Buoyancy, turbulence, and mixing** are neglected even though they could reduce peak temperatures.

### Manufacturability

* Helical DWTs are assumed:

  * Manufacturable at DEMO scale
  * Weldable with acceptable defect rates
  * Inspectable and replaceable
* Complex manifold routing is assumed compatible with RM constraints.

### Reliability philosophy

* Reduction of weld count is implicitly equated to higher reliability.
* Residual water inventory after draining (20–30%) is assumed acceptable for RM and safety.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Neutron transport and breeding

**Law**

* Neutron transport governed by Boltzmann equation.
* Tritium breeding via:

  * ⁶Li(n,α)T
  * ⁷Li(n,n’α)T

**Application**

* Water moderates neutrons, increasing ⁶Li capture probability.
* Excessive water also absorbs neutrons before reaching breeder.

**Boundary**

* TBR sits at **1.14**, just below required **1.15**, indicating operation near a neutronic cliff.

---

### Heat generation and removal

**Law**
[
\nabla \cdot (k \nabla T) + q''' = 0
]

**Application**

* Nuclear heating dominates in BZ.
* FW surface heat flux adds a strong gradient near plasma side.
* DWT layout governs conduction length scales.

**Second-order effects**

* Thermal conductivity differences dominate over nuclear heating differences in TSB temperatures.

---

### Pressurized water thermodynamics

**Law**

* At 15.5 MPa, saturation temperature ≈ **345 °C**.

**Implication**

* With outlet at 328 °C, **maximum saturation margin ≈ 17 °C**, even ideally.
* Classical subcooling criteria are fundamentally incompatible with this regime.

---

### Magnetohydrodynamics (PbLi)

**Law**

* Lorentz force: **J × B**
* Hartmann number Ha ≫ 1

**Application**

* Flow strongly damped.
* Heat transfer becomes conduction-dominated (Nu ≈ 1–1.2).

**Emergent behavior**

* Flow redistribution up to **300–500% deviation** between cells.
* Manifolds dominate pressure losses.

---

### Structural mechanics

**Law**

* Thermoelastic strain:
  [
  \varepsilon = \alpha \Delta T
  ]

**Application**

* Large thermal gradients induce high secondary stresses.
* RCC-MRx criteria against immediate plastic flow localization are violated in equatorial region.

**Limit**

* Steady analyses do not capture ratcheting or creep-fatigue.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Water fraction ↔ TBR ↔ EUROFER temperature**
* **DWT layout ↔ water inventory ↔ neutronics**
* **Magnetic field ↔ PbLi flow ↔ heat removal**
* **Thermal expansion ↔ attachment stiffness ↔ stress localization**
* **ECH cut-outs ↔ stiffness loss ↔ stress redistribution**

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Violated assumption       | Physical consequence       |
| ------------------------- | -------------------------- |
| EUROFER properties stable | DBTT shift, embrittlement  |
| Solid PbLi conservative   | Overestimated temperatures |
| Uniform flow              | Local overheating          |
| Linear elasticity         | Underpredicted plasticity  |
| Adequate TBR margin       | Tritium deficit            |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Thermal creep localization** in stiffening plates.
* **Intergranular fracture** due to irradiation-assisted creep.
* **MHD-induced flow starvation** in equatorial BZ cells.
* **Residual water freezing or corrosion** after incomplete draining.
* **Structural weakening near ECH cut-outs**, propagating fatigue cracks.
* **TBR shortfall under as-built tolerances**.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Water vs TBR

* Increasing water:

  * Improves cooling
  * Reduces TBR
* Current design already sacrifices TBR margin for thermal safety.

### Helical DWTs

* Pros:

  * Fewer welds
  * Simpler manifolds
  * −8.1% water inventory
* Cons:

  * Complex bending
  * Unproven irradiation behavior
  * Inspection difficulty

### Structural integration

* Attachments restrict global deformation.
* Cut-outs improve system integration but worsen local stresses by ~40%.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* BB interfaces with:

  * PHTS
  * TER
  * VV
  * RM systems
  * ECH wave paths
* All coolant pipes routed through **upper ports**, constraining layout.
* Draining depends on CVCS capacity, not BB-intrinsic design.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No evolution of:

  * DWT ovalization
  * PbLi corrosion
  * EUROFER creep damage
  * Weld degradation
* No cumulative impact of:

  * Load cycling
  * Thermal ratcheting
  * EM transients

These assumptions are **optimistic for DEMO lifetimes**.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Shielding: **robust**
* Thermal margins: **narrow**
* Structural margins:

  * Primary stress acceptable
  * Secondary stress often unacceptable
* Draining:

  * 20–30% water retention is non-negligible for RM and LOCA classification.

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed regime

* Steady plasma
* Nominal heat loads
* Fully pressurized water
* No boiling
* Linear elastic response

### Likely operating regime

* Thermal creep
* Flow maldistribution
* MHD-suppressed convection
* Progressive margin erosion
* Structural relaxation toward equatorial region


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Mission and functional role

* The divertor must simultaneously:

  * Exhaust extreme surface heat flux from SOL particles and radiation.
  * Provide effective helium ash and fuel exhaust via controlled gas conductance.
  * Shield the vacuum vessel and magnets from neutron and gamma loads.
  * Maintain plasma compatibility through tungsten plasma-facing components.
* The divertor is **not classified as a safety-important component**, implying it does not carry containment credit.

### Operating envelope

* **Single-null magnetic configuration** is assumed sufficient for DEMO baseline operation.
* **Pulse length** up to 2 hours with ≥6600 cycles over lifetime.
* **ELMs are assumed suppressed or mitigated**, not an actively dominant design driver.
* Disruptions are assumed to occur but with unspecified frequency.

### Lifetime and replacement

* Nominal divertor lifetime is **1.5 full-power years**, primarily limited by irradiation damage.
* Replacement is assumed to occur via **full cassette exchange**, not in-vessel repair.
* No ex-situ refurbishment is assumed. Recycling requires melting.

### Cooling concept

* **Dual water cooling circuits** are mandatory:

  * Targets at 130 °C, 5 MPa.
  * Cassette body, shielding liner, reflector plates at 180–210 °C, 3.5 MPa.
* Subcooled nucleate boiling is acceptable locally. Film boiling is not.
* CHF margin of **≥40%** at targets is a hard requirement.

### Materials

* Plasma-facing armor is **pure tungsten**.
* Structural material is **EUROFER97** for cassette, shielding liner, reflector plates.
* Cooling pipes are **CuCrZr alloy**, with soft copper interlayer.
* Wishbone fixation uses **Ti-6Al-4V**.
* Material properties are assumed sufficiently characterized for design-by-analysis, despite acknowledged data gaps.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Plasma physics assumptions

* Long-term detached divertor operation is implicitly assumed stable and controllable.
* Strike point sweeping is assumed effective in preventing localized overloads.
* Gas exhaust paths remain effective despite erosion, deformation, or deposition.

### Irradiation behavior

* EUROFER97 irradiated at **180–210 °C** is assumed to behave similarly to material irradiated at 250–350 °C, despite missing data.
* CuCrZr mechanical properties are assumed to saturate early and remain stable up to ~10 dpa.
* Helium bubble formation is assumed not to catastrophically degrade fracture resistance within lifetime.

### Structural design philosophy

* Fracture mechanics-based criteria are assumed sufficient to compensate for:

  * Brittle behavior at low temperature.
  * Singular stress and strain concentrations.
* Residual stresses from fabrication are assumed partially relaxed, but the effective stress-free temperature remains uncertain.

### Manufacturability and tolerances

* Uniform 0.5 mm gaps between tungsten monoblocks are assumed achievable and maintainable.
* Brazed and diffusion-bonded joints are assumed reproducible at scale.
* Ceramic electrical insulation coatings are assumed durable under irradiation and thermal cycling.

### Maintenance and remote handling

* Robotic installation tolerances are assumed compatible with:

  * 100 mm diameter pins.
  * Multi-step engagement sequences.
* Cutting and rewelding of pipes in low-dose regions is assumed feasible despite helium presence.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Heat transfer and CHF

**Governing physics**

* Convective heat transfer in pressurized water with swirl enhancement.
* CHF governed by hydrodynamic instability correlations.

**Application**

* Targets operate near the upper practical limit of water cooling.
* Safety margin relies on velocity uniformity and swirl tape performance.

**Boundary**

* CHF margin collapses rapidly if:

  * Flow distribution degrades.
  * Coolant temperature increases.
  * Pressure drops locally.

---

### Thermoelasticity and thermal stress

**Law**
[
\varepsilon_{th} = \alpha \Delta T
]

**Application**

* Thermal gradients dominate stress fields in cassette and shielding liner.
* Mechanical loads from coolant pressure are secondary.

**Second-order effects**

* Differential expansion between EUROFER97 and VV steel drives support loading.
* Stress redistribution occurs via elastic compliance of the wishbone.

---

### Neutron transport and damage

**Law**

* Neutron slowing down and capture governed by Boltzmann transport.
* Damage quantified as dpa and transmutation helium production.

**Application**

* Nuclear heating reaches up to 8 MW/m³ near plasma-facing regions.
* Helium production reaches 100 appm in steel near front faces.

**Limit**

* Irradiation damage exceeds material test database in low-temperature regime.

---

### Fracture mechanics

**Law**

* Crack driving force governed by stress intensity and strain energy release.
* Thermal loads are strain-controlled rather than force-controlled.

**Application**

* Crack growth tends to self-arrest due to stress relaxation.
* Singular strain concentrations do not necessarily propagate global failure.

**Boundary**

* Multi-axial stress states near free edges can exhaust ductility locally.

---

### Electromagnetics

**Law**
[
\mathbf{F} = \mathbf{J} \times \mathbf{B}
]

**Application**

* Eddy and halo currents induce MN-scale forces during disruptions.
* Electrical insulation dramatically reduces forces in target cooling circuits.

**Emergent behavior**

* Magnetic attraction between cassettes contributes beneficial preloading but degrades with irradiation.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Coolant temperature vs irradiation embrittlement**
  Lower temperature improves CHF but worsens steel brittleness.
* **Thermal gradients vs fixation stiffness**
  Stiffer supports reduce displacement but increase stress concentration.
* **Helium production vs recyclability**
  High He concentration eliminates cutting and rewelding options.
* **Residual stress vs fatigue behavior**
  Stress-free temperature assumptions shift crack initiation regimes.
* **Plasma detachment vs heat flux peaking**
  Divertor survivability relies on plasma control as much as hardware.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated                      | Physical consequence               |
| ---------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------- |
| ELM suppression fails                    | Armor erosion and thermal overload |
| EUROFER toughness at low T overestimated | Brittle fracture risk              |
| Uniform coolant flow                     | Local CHF exceedance               |
| Residual stress relaxed                  | Incorrect crack growth predictions |
| Helium effects mild                      | Loss of weldability and ductility  |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* Brittle fracture of EUROFER97 in cold CB regions after modest dpa.
* Ductility exhaustion in CuCrZr pipes at bond free edges.
* Progressive blockage or maldistribution in cooling channels.
* Helium-induced intergranular failure in steel supports.
* Loss of electrical insulation leading to amplified EM loads.
* Fatigue crack initiation at monoblock gaps evolving into leading-edge overload.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Cooling vs materials reliability

* Low-temperature water cooling protects against boiling but forces operation below optimal steel ductility range.

### Lifetime vs waste

* Short lifetime reduces embrittlement risk but increases radioactive waste.

### ITER inheritance vs DEMO reality

* ITER HHF technology scales thermally but not neutronically.

### Structural simplicity vs stress concentration

* Modular cassette and direct target attachment simplify maintenance but concentrate loads.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Interfaces with:

  * Breeding blanket edges.
  * Lower port pumping systems.
  * Primary heat transfer system.
  * Remote handling tooling.
* Divertor geometry strongly affects:

  * Neutron streaming.
  * Gas conductance.
  * Maintenance access.
* Divertor design is coupled to magnet count and port allocation.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Progressive embrittlement of EUROFER97 and CuCrZr.
* Helium accumulation reducing weldability.
* Possible relaxation of residual stresses over time.
* Cumulative thermal cycling effects on interlayers.
* Decrease in magnetic preload due to permeability loss.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* CHF margin at targets is robust under nominal assumptions.
* Saturation margin in CB is locally exhausted at wall interface.
* Structural margins rely on fracture mechanics rather than ductility.
* EM load mitigation depends critically on electrical insulation integrity.
* VV shielding margin is comfortable with optional inserts.

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed regime

* Stable detached plasma.
* Controlled strike point sweeping.
* Uniform coolant distribution.
* Elastic or shakedown structural response.

### Likely operating regime

* Partial reattachment events.
* Local coolant boundary layer boiling.
* Progressive irradiation embrittlement.
* Increased sensitivity to geometric imperfections.

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Role of PM-HIP in nuclear systems

* PM-HIP is assumed to be a **drop-in or near-drop-in replacement** for cast or forged product forms for:

  * Reactor internals
  * Pressure-retaining components
  * Selected secondary-side components
* PM-HIP components are assumed capable of meeting **ASME BPVC Section III nuclear safety requirements** once sufficient irradiation data exist.

### Manufacturing quality assumptions

* PM-HIP is assumed to:

  * Produce **fully dense material** with negligible porosity
  * Eliminate large casting defects and forging banding
  * Yield fine, homogeneous, equiaxed grain structures
* Gas atomization is assumed to generate powders with **low oxidation and controlled chemistry**.
* HIP consolidation is assumed to suppress macro-segregation and secondary phase clustering.

### Irradiation envelope

* The experiment explicitly targets:

  * Temperatures: **300 °C and 400 °C**
  * Doses: **~1 dpa and ~3 dpa**
* These conditions are assumed to be **representative of LWR and near-term advanced reactor environments** for the selected alloys.
* Irradiation temperatures are assumed acceptable with a tolerance of **±50 °C**, treated as non-fatal to data interpretation.

### Comparative methodology

* A core assumption is that **direct comparison** between PM-HIP and cast/forged materials is valid if:

  * Irradiation temperature
  * Irradiation dose
  * Specimen geometry
  * Post-irradiation examination techniques
    are held constant.

### Specimen representativeness

* Miniature CT specimens are assumed to provide **fracture toughness trends representative of bulk behavior**, despite size effects.
* TEM discs are assumed to capture representative irradiation microstructures despite free surface effects.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Powder chemistry control

* Oxygen, nitrogen, and tramp element levels in powders are assumed:

  * Sufficiently low
  * Uniform across powder batches
* The experiment implicitly assumes that **powder oxygen content will not dominate irradiation embrittlement**, despite historical evidence to the contrary.

### HIP process uniformity

* HIP consolidation is assumed to produce:

  * Uniform bonding across prior particle boundaries
  * No weak interfaces that become preferred helium or defect sinks under irradiation
* Prior particle boundaries are implicitly assumed to behave like grain boundaries post-HIP.

### Irradiation damage scaling

* It is assumed that:

  * **1–5 dpa behavior scales meaningfully to higher doses**
  * Mechanisms observed at these doses remain relevant for lifetime predictions
* Dose rate effects specific to ATR are implicitly neglected.

### Temperature homogeneity

* Axial cosine flux shaping is assumed correctable by specimen placement.
* Temperature gradients within individual specimens are assumed negligible for mechanical interpretation.

### Gas-gap thermal control

* The experiment assumes:

  * Gas gap dimensions are stable throughout irradiation
  * Swelling, creep, or relaxation do not significantly alter gap conductance
* ±39 °C temperature scatter is implicitly assumed not to mask fabrication-dependent trends.

### Structural integrity of capsules

* Capsule welds, honing interfaces, and internal fixtures are assumed not to:

  * Distort temperature fields unexpectedly
  * Introduce local stress concentrations that affect specimens
* Capsules are assumed to remain **leak-tight** and chemically inert for the full irradiation duration.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Neutron damage accumulation

**Law**

* Displacement damage quantified by:
  [
  \text{dpa} = \int \phi(E),\sigma_d(E),dE,dt
  ]

**Application**

* MCNP + NJOY used to compute energy-dependent displacement cross-sections.
* Atomic displacement energies are assumed constant:

  * 40 eV for steels and Ni alloys
  * Lower weighted values for HfAl₃-Al

**Boundary**

* Real displacement efficiency varies with temperature, alloy chemistry, and defect recombination.
* Effective dpa may deviate from calculated values by tens of percent.

---

### Radiation defect kinetics

**Principles**

* Frenkel pair production
* Defect migration, recombination, and clustering
* Sink strength competition (dislocations, grain boundaries, precipitates)

**Application**

* PM-HIP’s finer grain size implies:

  * Higher grain boundary sink density
  * Altered loop evolution kinetics
* Forged materials exhibit:

  * Higher initial dislocation density
  * Different loop to line defect ratios

**Second-order effects**

* Radiation-induced segregation alters local chemistry at sinks.
* Helium stabilizes voids and bubbles, accelerating embrittlement.

---

### Strengthening and embrittlement

**Law**

* Yield stress increase from obstacles:
  [
  \Delta \sigma \propto \sqrt{N} \cdot b
  ]

**Application**

* Both PM-HIP and wrought alloys show irradiation hardening.
* Differences arise from:

  * Initial sink populations
  * Particle boundary chemistry
  * Oxygen-stabilized defect clusters

**Limit**

* Hardening does not uniquely predict fracture toughness loss.

---

### Fracture mechanics

**Law**

* Fracture resistance governed by:
  [
  K_{IC},\ J_{IC},\ \text{and DBTT}
  ]

**Application**

* Miniature CT specimens probe crack initiation and early growth.
* PM-HIP steels historically show:

  * Similar DBTT
  * Larger DBTT shifts
  * Lower upper shelf energy

**Boundary**

* Constraint effects and limited ligament size reduce transferability to large components.

---

### Heat transfer

**Law**

* Gas conduction across gap dominates:
  [
  q \propto \frac{k_{gas}}{g}
  ]

**Application**

* Small dimensional changes produce large temperature shifts.
* Temperature control is extremely sensitive to manufacturing tolerances.

**Emergent behavior**

* Thermal uncertainty competes with irradiation effects in data scatter.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Powder oxygen ↔ irradiation embrittlement ↔ fracture toughness**
* **Grain size ↔ sink strength ↔ loop evolution ↔ hardening**
* **Temperature ↔ defect mobility ↔ DBTT shift**
* **Gas gap tolerance ↔ temperature ↔ microstructure evolution**
* **Specimen size ↔ constraint ↔ measured toughness**

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption violated      | Consequence                            |
| ------------------------ | -------------------------------------- |
| Powder oxygen negligible | Severe toughness loss post-irradiation |
| Grain boundaries benign  | Helium-decorated particle boundaries   |
| dpa scaling linear       | Misleading lifetime predictions        |
| Temperature uniform      | Fabrication effects masked             |
| Mini-CT transferable     | Non-conservative fracture assessment   |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* Inter-particle boundary embrittlement under helium accumulation
* Oxygen-stabilized vacancy clusters in PM-HIP steels
* Accelerated DBTT shift at lower irradiation temperatures
* Loss of strain hardening capacity leading to cleavage-controlled fracture
* Overconfidence in ASME allowables derived from unirradiated PM-HIP data
* Gas gap collapse altering specimen temperature mid-irradiation

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

### Qualification speed vs certainty

* PM-HIP promises shorter lead times and lower cost.
* This comes at the expense of:

  * Long-term irradiation database depth
  * Confidence in fracture behavior under transients

### Microstructural uniformity vs toughness

* Fine grain size increases strength.
* Fine grain size may:

  * Increase defect density
  * Reduce crack tip plasticity after irradiation

### Weld elimination vs inspection risk

* Fewer welds reduce classic weld embrittlement.
* HIP joints introduce **distributed interfaces** that are harder to inspect.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Results are intended to inform:

  * ASME Section III qualification
  * Reactor internals design
  * SMR pressure boundary concepts
* ATR neutron spectrum approximates LWR but differs from:

  * Fast reactors
  * Fusion neutron spectra
* PIE performed in hot cells introduces testing constraints not present in laboratory environments.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* Microstructure assumed stable beyond 3–5 dpa trends.
* No explicit modeling of:

  * Irradiation creep
  * Long-term helium bubble coalescence
  * Environmental cracking post-irradiation
* Annealing during service transients is not considered.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Capsule structural margins are large:

  * DNBR = 4.8
  * FIR = 2.15
* Material property margins are **unknown**, not conservative.
* Fracture toughness margin erosion is the dominant unresolved risk.

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATING REGIME

### Designed experimental regime

* Controlled temperature
* Low to moderate dose
* Stable capsule geometry
* Isolated material behavior

### Real reactor regime

* Multiaxial stress
* Thermal gradients
* Higher cumulative doses
* Helium-driven degradation
* Transient loading and environmental effects


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Role of HIP in ITER PW shield fabrication

* Hot isostatic pressing of **steel powder with solid parts** is assumed to be a **viable full-scale manufacturing route** for ITER primary wall shield modules.
* HIP is explicitly assumed to:

  * Eliminate *all* single seal welds in the completed shield
  * Reduce leak risk by HIP-bonding all potential sealing interfaces
* HIP is treated as a **reliability-enhancing process**, not merely a manufacturing convenience.

### Manufacturing philosophy

* The program assumes that **manufacturing risk can be managed by staging**:

  * Small and medium mock-ups first
  * Full-scale prototype second
* Manufacturing feasibility is assumed transferable from mock-ups to full scale provided:

  * Distortions are measured
  * Tooling is adapted
  * References are re-established post-HIP

### Material assumptions

* ITER Grade (IG) stainless steel is assumed to:

  * Be compatible with combined forged + powder HIP processing
  * Maintain acceptable mechanical and joining properties after HIP at **1400 °C and 1200 bar**
* Steel powder is assumed to:

  * Densify fully
  * Bond reliably to solid forged parts
  * Introduce no unacceptable residual porosity or weak interfaces

### Tolerance control

* Extremely tight tolerances are assumed achievable across:

  * 157 deep drilled holes
  * Slot machining between intersecting cooling channels
  * Post-HIP final machining near embedded tubes
* Dimensional control is assumed maintainable through:

  * Defined drilling sequences
  * Intermediate dimensional inspections
  * Ultrasonic verification prior to high-risk machining

### Inspection effectiveness

* Ultrasonic testing is assumed capable of:

  * Detecting bonding quality after HIP
  * Accurately locating embedded cooling tubes
  * Providing sufficient confidence to proceed with irreversible machining steps

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### HIP bonding completeness

* It is implicitly assumed that **HIP bonding is binary**:

  * Either fully successful or catastrophic
* No partial-bond degradation mode is considered acceptable or detectable.
* Long-term service degradation of HIP interfaces is not addressed.

### Powder behavior assumptions

* Powder flow during filling is assumed to:

  * Fully occupy all voids
  * Avoid local density gradients
  * Avoid segregation during vibration filling
* A correct **powder mass equals defect-free HIP** is implicitly assumed.

### Distortion predictability

* Distortions from:

  * Powder densification (~30% local shrinkage)
  * Tube restraint
  * Furnace support interaction
    are assumed to be:
  * Predictable
  * Measurable
  * Recoverable through reference re-definition
* It is implicitly assumed that distortions **do not compromise internal clearances**, even if external geometry shifts.

### Tooling survivability

* Custom HIP furnace support tooling is assumed to:

  * Survive 1400 °C under 4.5-ton load
  * Not damage furnace walls despite millimeter-scale clearances
* Failure of tooling is treated as unacceptable but implicitly unlikely once “calculated.”

### Inspection sufficiency

* Ultrasonic inspection is assumed sufficient to:

  * Detect lack of bonding
  * Locate tubes with sub-millimeter accuracy
* No alternative volumetric inspection (e.g., radiography, CT) is discussed, implying confidence in UT alone.

### Repair philosophy

* The process implicitly assumes **zero reparability after HIP**.
* All risk mitigation must occur *before* HIP.
* This implies an assumption of **perfect execution**, not just robust design.

---

## 3. FIRST-PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE PROCESS

### HIP densification physics

* Powder densification governed by:

  * Diffusion-controlled creep
  * Pore collapse under isostatic pressure
* At 1400 °C and 1200 bar:

  * Powder shrinks volumetrically
  * Solid–powder interfaces undergo metallurgical bonding

**Implication**

* Differential shrinkage between powder and solid induces internal stresses and distortion.

---

### Thermal–mechanical distortion

* Non-uniform geometry and constraints produce:

  * Anisotropic shrinkage
  * Bending moments in tubes
  * Residual stresses locked in during cooling

**Hidden risk**

* Residual stress fields are not measured, only geometry.

---

### Deep drilling mechanics

* Long, high-aspect-ratio drilling introduces:

  * Tool deflection
  * Thermal expansion
  * Cumulative positional error

**Constraint**

* Later machining assumes drilled hole axes are correct within tight margins.

---

### Ultrasonic inspection physics

* UT accuracy depends on:

  * Acoustic impedance contrast
  * Surface roughness
  * Beam access and coupling
* Internal powder-to-solid interfaces complicate signal interpretation.

**Implicit leap**

* UT resolution is assumed sufficient to guarantee “no overlap” during final drilling.

---

### Welding and cleanliness

* HIP success depends on:

  * Oxide-free interfaces
  * Vacuum integrity
  * Absence of grease or particulates

**Implicit assumption**

* Cleaning procedures are perfectly effective at this scale.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Powder fill quality ↔ HIP bonding ↔ UT detectability**
* **Tube distortion ↔ support design ↔ final machining risk**
* **Deep drilling accuracy ↔ water collector machining ↔ PFW attachment**
* **HIP distortion ↔ reference redefinition ↔ downstream tolerance stack-up**
* **Elimination of welds ↔ reliance on HIP integrity**

---

## 5. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* Local lack of bonding undetectable by UT
* Residual stress-driven cracking during operation
* Tube wall thinning due to post-HIP machining proximity
* Powder segregation causing local property gradients
* Inspection blind spots near intersecting holes
* Accumulated tolerance drift despite intermediate inspections

---

## 6. UNSTATED TRADEOFFS

### Weld elimination vs inspectability

* Fewer welds reduce classic weld defects
* But HIP joints are **distributed, buried, and non-repairable**

### Manufacturing complexity vs operational reliability

* Reliability in service is gained at the cost of:

  * Extreme manufacturing precision
  * High financial risk per component
  * Zero tolerance for execution errors

### Geometry fidelity vs structural predictability

* Geometry can be corrected post-HIP
* Residual stresses cannot

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Choice of material system

* Tungsten is assumed to be a **representative and critical plasma-facing material** for future fusion reactors.
* Hydrogen behavior is assumed to be a **dominant degradation mechanism** despite tungsten’s relatively low bulk H solubility.
* Grain boundaries are explicitly treated as **both beneficial defect sinks** and **potential embrittlement sites**.

### Grain boundary representativeness

* The Σ3(112)[110] and Σ5(310)[100] GBs are assumed to be:

  * Experimentally relevant
  * Structurally stable at fusion-relevant temperatures
  * Representative of broader classes of W GBs
* Results derived from these two GBs are implicitly generalized to **other W GBs**.

### Computational framework validity

* Density Functional Theory with:

  * GGA-PW91
  * PAW potentials
  * 400 eV cutoff
    is assumed sufficient to capture:
  * H–W interactions
  * Charge density redistribution
  * GB cohesion and fracture behavior
* Static first-principles tensile testing (FPCTT) is assumed to meaningfully represent **mechanical strength and fracture trends**.

### Strain modeling

* Uniaxial tensile strain up to ~10–12% is assumed to:

  * Be physically meaningful at the atomic scale
  * Represent strain localization near GBs under irradiation and thermal loading
* Poisson contraction is explicitly neglected, implying:

  * Out-of-plane relaxation is assumed not to dominate fracture physics.

### Hydrogen states

* Hydrogen is assumed to occupy:

  * Interstitial sites
  * Vacancy sites
* Molecular hydrogen, bubbles, and extended defects are **explicitly excluded**.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Time-scale separation

* The study implicitly assumes **thermodynamic equilibrium**:

  * H atoms instantaneously find minimum-energy sites
  * No kinetic barriers limit segregation
* Diffusion, trapping kinetics, and transient irradiation effects are ignored.

### Temperature effects

* All calculations are effectively at **0 K electronic ground state**, with no:

  * Finite-temperature entropy
  * Vibrational contributions
  * Thermally activated migration
* The influence of fusion-relevant temperatures (800–1500 K) is inferred, not modeled.

### Hydrogen chemical potential

* The reference state for H is implicitly fixed by bulk tetrahedral interstitial energy.
* Plasma-driven H influx, recombination, and surface effects are not included.
* The effective hydrogen chemical potential under reactor conditions is assumed compatible with the chosen reference.

### Vacancy population

* Vacancies are introduced **a priori**:

  * Their formation is not coupled to strain, H concentration, or irradiation cascades.
* Vacancy concentration is implicitly assumed to be:

  * Low enough to treat clusters independently
  * High enough to matter for GB cohesion

### Fracture mode

* Fracture is implicitly assumed to be:

  * Mode I
  * Brittle
  * GB-dominated
* Plasticity, dislocation emission, and crack blunting are excluded by construction.

---

## 3. FUNDAMENTAL PHYSICAL MECHANISMS (FIRST PRINCIPLES)

### Electron-density mechanism

* Hydrogen prefers sites where local valence electron density approaches an “optimal” value.
* Tungsten’s bulk interstitial sites have **excessively high electron density** for H.
* Grain boundaries reduce atomic packing → reduced charge density → favorable H sites.

**Key principle**

> H segregation energy is governed more by **local electronic structure** than by classical elastic strain alone.

---

### Effect of tensile strain

* Uniaxial tensile strain:

  * Expands interstitial volume
  * Reduces local charge density
* This systematically lowers H segregation energy at GBs and vacancies.

**Critical implication**

> Mechanical strain is not merely a consequence of embrittlement; it is an *active driver* of H accumulation.

---

### Vacancy–H synergy

* Vacancies act as deep electronic and volumetric traps.
* Multiple H atoms fill progressively less favorable sites within a vacancy.
* Tensile strain stabilizes higher-order vacancy–H clusters.

**Nonlinear effect**

* Vacancy dominates strength loss initially.
* H amplifies damage only once sufficient concentration is reached.

---

### GB cohesion degradation

* H does not form strong directional bonds with W.
* Instead, it:

  * Weakens W–W bonding by charge redistribution
  * Increases W–W bond lengths via local compressive effects
* H at the **GB plane** is far more damaging than H nearby.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES REVEALED

* **Strain ↔ charge density ↔ H segregation**
* **GB structure ↔ atomic density ↔ sensitivity to H**
* **Vacancy presence ↔ H capacity ↔ embrittlement severity**
* **H spatial distribution ↔ fracture initiation plane**

These are not additive effects. They are **strongly coupled**.

---

## 5. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES SUGGESTED

* Strain-assisted H accumulation at GBs preceding visible damage
* Localized GB decohesion without bulk H saturation
* Vacancy-mediated embrittlement at lower global H concentrations than expected
* Preferential failure of “open” GBs under identical loading conditions

---

## 6. UNSTATED TRADEOFFS

### Grain boundary engineering

* Increasing GB density improves radiation damage healing
* But also:

  * Increases H trapping capacity
  * Raises embrittlement risk under stress

### Mechanical robustness vs defect tolerance

* GBs beneficial for defect annihilation
* Harmful when electronic structure favors impurity segregation

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Materials and Microstructure

* Tungsten purity is >99.95 %, implying negligible influence of alloying elements or impurities on sputtering, recrystallization, or fuzz growth.
* The material is **polycrystalline, hot-rolled, mechanically polished**, and **not stress-relief annealed**, implying residual stresses are present but assumed not to dominate sputtering behavior.
* Grain orientations exposed at the surface are assumed representative of the bulk texture.

### Plasma Environment

* Neon ion energies of ~50 to 80 eV are taken as **fusion-relevant** for ITER first wall and divertor conditions.
* Helium plasma conditions used to generate fuzz are assumed representative of ITER helium exposure regimes.
* Plasma flux and energy distributions measured locally are assumed stable and spatially representative over the erosion area.

### Measurement and Diagnostics

* Secondary electron emission is modeled using the Young–Dekker formula parameterized with Walker’s data.
* A fixed ion temperature of 0.03 eV is assumed based on prior Thomson scattering measurements in a similar source.
* Optical emission line intensity ratios (W I / Ne II) are assumed proportional to sputtered flux, enabling indirect sputtering yield estimation.

### Geometry and Boundary Conditions

* Ion incidence is assumed normal.
* Edge effects, shadowing, and macroscopic roughness outside fuzz morphology are neglected.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Microstructural and Manufacturing Assumptions

* Grain interiors are treated as **elastically and crystallographically ideal**, neglecting sub-grain dislocation density, residual rolling textures beyond EBSD resolution, and stress gradients.
* Mechanical polishing is assumed not to induce preferential amorphization or orientation-dependent surface damage that would bias sputtering.
* Fuzz morphology is assumed statistically homogeneous within the analyzed regions.

### Aging and Temporal Effects

* No significant microstructural evolution occurs during Ne exposure except erosion. This neglects:

  * Ne-induced defect accumulation
  * Surface amorphization
  * Local recrystallization under ~900 to 1000 °C exposure
* Fuzz is assumed mechanically stable until fully eroded, ignoring possible collapse, sintering, or brittle fracture of tendrils.

### Human and Operational Factors

* ITER operation is implicitly assumed capable of:

  * Controlling He exposure spatially and temporally to engineer fuzz
  * Maintaining fuzz without unacceptable tritium retention or dust generation

### Modeling Assumptions

* TRIM.SP’s randomized target assumption is implicitly treated as a known limitation, but the experimental results are assumed scalable to ITER despite vastly different exposure times and neutron irradiation.
* Molecular dynamics feasibility estimates assume classical potentials remain valid for fuzz geometries and high defect densities.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Physical Sputtering

**Principle**
Momentum transfer from incident ions ejects surface atoms when transferred energy exceeds surface binding energy.

**Key Relations**

* Threshold behavior:
  ( Y \propto (E_i - E_{th})^n ) for ( E_i \gtrsim E_{th} )
* Near-threshold regime is highly sensitive to crystallography and surface binding anisotropy.

**Application**

* (100) surfaces show higher sputtering than (111) due to more open atomic packing and lower effective surface binding energy.
* Fuzz reduces effective momentum transfer due to multiple collisions and redeposition.

**Validity Regime**

* Low-energy, near-threshold, collision-cascade sputtering.
* Breaks down if chemical sputtering or blistering dominates, which is not assumed here.

---

### 3.2 Crystallographic Anisotropy

**Principle**
Surface atomic density and channeling directions depend on orientation.

**Application**

* Orientation-dependent angular distribution functions alter escape probability of recoils.
* (111) planes minimize sputtering yield due to close packing.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Recrystallization alters surface orientation distribution, feeding back into erosion rate.

---

### 3.3 Secondary Electron Emission

**Principle**
Electron emission modifies sheath potential and effective ion impact energy.

**Application**

* Fuzzy surfaces reduce secondary electron yield, increasing plasma potential and Ne II emission.
* Assumed average secondary electron energy of ~7 eV.

**Boundary**

* Assumes emission spectra from clean W(100) remain applicable to fuzzed, porous morphologies.

---

### 3.4 Transport and Redeosition in Porous Media

**Principle**
Mean free path and line-of-sight probability control escape of sputtered atoms.

**Application**

* Increased fuzz thickness increases probability of redeposition.
* Porosity alone does not uniquely determine yield reduction.

**Emergent Behavior**

* Nonlinear, sigmoidal erosion kinetics due to evolving geometry.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Thermal effects and microstructure**: Elevated surface temperatures promote recrystallization, which biases grain orientation toward (100), increasing sputtering.
* **Fuzz morphology and plasma sheath**: Reduced secondary electron emission modifies sheath potential, altering ion energies and sputtering yields.
* **Porosity and collision cascades**: Increased internal collisions damp cascade energy, reinforcing sputtering suppression.
* **Orientation and erosion feedback**: Preferential erosion exposes subsurface grains, changing the orientation distribution dynamically.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                                              | Violated Outcome                                               |
| ------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------- |
| Randomized target models are sufficient                 | TRIM.SP underpredicts erosion for textured or recrystallized W |
| Stable fuzz morphology                                  | Collapse or sintering would abruptly increase sputtering       |
| Linear relation between line intensity and sputter flux | Yield estimates become invalid at very low signal levels       |
| Fixed ion temperature                                   | Errors in sheath potential and impact energy calculations      |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Accelerated divertor erosion** due to recrystallization-driven (100) texture formation.
* **Dust generation** from mechanically unstable fuzz under transient loads.
* **Tritium retention** in high-porosity fuzz layers.
* **Runaway erosion feedback loop** where erosion promotes recrystallization, which increases erosion.
* **Diagnostic blind spots** where sputtering yields fall below spectroscopic detection but still contribute to impurity accumulation.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Why tungsten**: prioritizes low sputtering over fracture toughness and manufacturability.
* **Why fuzz thicknesses <3 μm**: likely constrained by experimental time and plasma stability, not necessarily optimal performance.
* **What is not optimized**:

  * Mechanical robustness of fuzz
  * Neutron damage resistance
  * Tritium inventory limits

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Assumes constant Ne seeding fraction independent of erosion rate.
* Neglects neutron-induced transmutation and swelling.
* Ignores electromagnetic forces, transient ELMs, and off-normal ion incidence.
* Heat removal is assumed adequate to prevent melting despite >900 °C operation.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Short term**: selective sputtering, fuzz erosion, nonlinear yield evolution.
* **Mid term**: recrystallization, grain reorientation, fuzz depletion.
* **Long term**: texture cycling, accelerated erosion, impurity accumulation.

Fatigue, creep, and neutron damage are implicitly assumed negligible within the experiment but dominate ITER lifetimes.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* No explicit safety factors on erosion rates are stated.
* Single-point failure includes loss of fuzz stability or uncontrolled recrystallization.
* Graceful degradation is not guaranteed once fuzz is fully eroded.

---

## 11. DESIGN REGIME VS OPERATIVE REGIME

* **Design regime**: steady-state, near-threshold Ne bombardment with controlled He exposure.
* **Likely operative regime**:

  * Transient heat loads
  * Mixed ion species
  * Neutron-induced defect evolution
* Robustness of conclusions degrades rapidly outside near-threshold, steady conditions.


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Materials and Structure

* Thin films of **pure W, pure B, and mixed W–B** are assumed representative of plasma-facing layers formed by erosion and redeposition in ITER.
* Film densities taken from prior work are assumed valid and uniform throughout the thickness.
* Film thicknesses are assumed laterally uniform despite long deposition times and an interrupted B deposition.
* Film chemistry is assumed stable up to 1000 °C, except where explicitly observed at interfaces.

### Implantation Conditions

* Neon isotopes 20Ne and 22Ne are implanted at **35–190 keV** with identical fluence (3 × 10¹⁶ at./cm²).
* High implantation energies are assumed acceptable proxies for studying **mobility and retention**, not operational damage states.
* Implantation damage levels of ~8–12 dpa are assumed not to fundamentally alter retention mechanisms in a way that invalidates conclusions.

### Thermal Environment

* Annealing between RT and 1000 °C for 1 h is assumed sufficient to activate any relevant diffusion or desorption processes.
* Vacuum annealing pressures (~10⁻⁷ mbar) are assumed not to introduce significant contamination or oxidation.

### Diagnostics

* ToF-ERDA is assumed capable of resolving isotope-specific depth profiles with sufficient accuracy to detect diffusion, intermixing, or release.
* SRIM is assumed adequate for pre-selecting implantation energies and estimating damage, with known limitations acknowledged.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Damage and Defect Physics

* Implantation-induced defects are implicitly assumed to be **stable trapping sites** rather than pathways for enhanced diffusion.
* Defect evolution during annealing is assumed not to cause bubble coalescence or percolation paths to the surface.
* The defect population created by keV implantation is implicitly treated as comparable, in its trapping effect, to defects formed under plasma exposure.

### Scale and Geometry
