* Thin-film behavior is assumed scalable to bulk or redeposited layers in ITER, despite vastly different thicknesses, stress states, and irradiation histories.
* Lateral diffusion and edge effects are neglected.

### Chemical Interactions

* Ne is treated as chemically inert, with retention governed purely by physical trapping.
* Formation of W–B compounds or borides within the film bulk is assumed negligible or irrelevant to Ne mobility.
* Observed W–Si intermixing is treated as a special case rather than a general concern.

### Time Dependence

* One-hour anneals are assumed sufficient to reveal long-term mobility trends.
* Slow diffusion processes over reactor-relevant timescales are implicitly assumed absent or negligible.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Noble Gas Transport in Solids

**Principle**
Diffusion of inert gases occurs via thermally activated hopping between interstitial sites or via defect-mediated transport.

**Key Relation**
[
D = D_0 \exp\left(-\frac{E_a}{kT}\right)
]

**Application**

* No measurable diffusion up to 1000 °C implies a high activation energy for Ne migration in W, B, and W–B mixtures.
* Retention indicates strong trapping at vacancies, dislocation loops, or implantation-induced clusters.

**Validity**

* Applies to bulk-like regions of films.
* Breaks down if interconnected porosity or open diffusion paths form.

---

### 3.2 Implantation Damage and Trapping

**Principle**
High-energy ions create collision cascades producing vacancies, interstitials, and clusters that act as trapping sites.

**Application**

* Calculated dpa values imply dense defect populations capable of immobilizing Ne.
* Implantation order is designed to avoid disturbing near-surface profiles.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Defect annealing could release trapped Ne, which is not observed, indicating defect stability up to 1000 °C.

---

### 3.3 Thermodynamics of Gas Release

**Principle**
Desorption occurs when thermal energy exceeds binding energy of trapped species.

**Application**

* Absence of desorption peaks implies Ne binding energies exceed thermal energy available at 1000 °C.
* Contrasts with He, which has lower migration and release barriers.

---

### 3.4 Interface Reactions

**Principle**
Chemical reactions at interfaces alter defect structures and diffusion pathways.

**Application**

* W–Si formation above 900 °C modifies Ne depth profiles in W:B/Si samples.
* Indicates chemistry, not temperature alone, can unlock trapped Ne.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Implantation energy vs. retention**: High energies create deeper, more dispersed trapping sites, reducing surface escape probability.
* **Damage vs. diffusion**: Damage increases trapping density, suppressing diffusion despite high temperatures.
* **Film chemistry vs. interfaces**: Stable bulk chemistry contrasts with reactive interfaces that can mobilize Ne.
* **Measurement resolution vs. interpretation**: ERDA depth resolution limits may mask small-scale redistribution.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                                   | Consequence if Violated                        |
| -------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- |
| keV implantation represents plasma retention | Actual plasma-implanted Ne may be less stable  |
| Defects remain trapping sites                | Defect annealing could cause sudden Ne release |
| 1 h anneal is sufficient                     | Long-term diffusion may be underestimated      |
| Thin films represent ITER walls              | Bulk behavior under neutron damage may differ  |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Long-term impurity accumulation**: Stable Ne retention implies potential buildup in redeposited layers.
* **Sudden release during chemistry changes**: Interface reactions or phase changes could trigger Ne bursts.
* **Synergy with neutron damage**: Neutron-induced defects may alter trapping energetics.
* **Bubble formation at higher fluence**: Not explored but could cause blistering or dust formation.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Why keV implantation**: Chosen for profile separation, not realism.
* **What is not optimized**:

  * Plasma-relevant damage states
  * Cyclic thermal loading
  * Simultaneous D, He, and Ne retention
* **Tradeoff**: High damage improves detectability but obscures operational relevance.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No concurrent plasma flux during annealing.
* No neutron irradiation.
* No mechanical stress or thermal gradients.
* Substrate chemistry influences results despite being treated as secondary.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Short term**: Ne immobilized at implantation depth.
* **Medium term**: Stable trapping up to 1000 °C.
* **Long term**: Unknown behavior under neutron damage, cyclic heating, or chemistry evolution.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Retention stability suggests robustness against thermal excursions.
* However, **single-point failures** include:

  * Phase formation
  * Interface reactions
  * Damage saturation
* No engineered release mechanism exists once Ne is trapped.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

* **Design regime**: Static films, high implantation energy, vacuum annealing.
* **Operative regime**: Low-energy plasma implantation, continuous erosion, neutron damage, mixed species.
* Conclusions are robust for **immobility of Ne in damaged W-based films**, but scalability to ITER timescales and conditions remains uncertain.


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Materials and Geometry

* Tungsten-coated graphite tiles with Mo interlayers are assumed to be **representative erosion markers** for full-W divertor behavior.
* The nominal coating thicknesses (≈12 μm W, ≈3 μm Mo interlayer, additional 1–2 μm W marker) are assumed known well enough to back-calculate erosion rates.
* Core-drilled disks are assumed to preserve the **local erosion–deposition history** without significant artefacts from drilling.

### Plasma Exposure

* Integrated plasma exposure times of up to 7.3 h across C1–C5 are assumed sufficient to establish statistically meaningful erosion and deposition patterns.
* Phase-1 WEST plasmas are assumed ITER-relevant in terms of:

  * Material migration pathways
  * Strike-point physics
  * Impurity transport trends
* Deuterium plasmas dominate, with limited helium exposure, and this is assumed not to fundamentally alter the erosion–deposition topology.

### Diagnostics

* SIMS provides high-resolution depth profiling but is treated as semi-quantitative.
* IBA techniques (EBS, RBS, NRA, PIXE) are assumed to provide reliable absolute quantification despite:

  * Surface roughness
  * Layered and porous deposits
* Agreement between SIMS and IBA is taken as validation of conclusions.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Surface Morphology

* Surface roughness, porosity, and cracking are assumed not to invalidate depth-integrated erosion estimates.
* Delamination and flaking are treated as secondary effects rather than primary erosion mechanisms.

### Transport Physics

* Net erosion or deposition at a given poloidal coordinate is assumed to reflect **steady-state balance**, not transient reversals.
* Material transport is assumed dominantly along SOL field lines, with toroidal asymmetries treated as perturbations.

### Chemistry

* Boron oxidation is implicitly assumed to occur primarily **post-mortem** during air exposure, not in-plasma.
* Oxygen co-deposition is assumed to track boron rather than act independently.

### Time Scaling

* Average erosion rates derived from integrated exposure are assumed extrapolatable to longer reactor lifetimes.
* Campaign-to-campaign evolution is assumed monotonic, except where magnetic ripple shifts are invoked.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Physical Sputtering and Erosion

**Principle**
Momentum transfer from plasma ions and impurities removes surface atoms when transferred energy exceeds surface binding energy.

**Application**

* Strong erosion at ISP and OSP reflects peak particle and heat fluxes.
* Measured net erosion rates of ~0.2–0.3 nm/s at ISP are consistent with near-threshold sputtering of W.

**Validity**

* Applies under steady plasma exposure.
* Breaks down when flaking or mechanical detachment dominates.

---

### 3.2 Material Migration and Re-deposition

**Principle**
Eroded atoms are transported along magnetic field lines and redeposited where local flux and temperature decrease.

**Application**

* Thick deposits near ISP towards HFS arise from high upstream erosion and reduced local sputtering.
* Thin deposits at HFS and LFS limits correspond to low plasma interaction regions.

**Emergent Effects**

* Sharp poloidal boundaries between erosion and deposition zones.
* Layered deposits reflecting campaign history.

---

### 3.3 Boronization Chemistry

**Principle**
Injected boron preferentially coats plasma-facing surfaces and getters oxygen.

**Application**

* Strong increase in B and O deposition after C4.
* O content tracks B due to oxidation affinity.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Boron-rich layers are mechanically weak and prone to delamination.
* Oxidation increases porosity and reduces adhesion.

---

### 3.4 Mechanical Stability of Deposits

**Principle**
Thermal cycling and compositional mismatch induce stresses in layered deposits.

**Application**

* Cracking and flaking observed in thick deposits near ISP.
* Partial loss of B-rich layers inferred after C5.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Erosion ↔ deposition coupling**: High erosion at ISP feeds deposition zones nearby.
* **Boronization ↔ oxygen uptake**: Boron increases impurity retention but degrades mechanical stability.
* **Magnetic ripple ↔ deposition topology**: Toroidal ripple shifts local balance between erosion and redeposition.
* **Surface roughness ↔ diagnostics**: Roughness increases uncertainty in IBA, feeding back into erosion estimates.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                              | If Violated                               |
| --------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------- |
| Net erosion dominates over flaking      | Erosion rates are overestimated           |
| Deposits are compositionally uniform    | Local hot spots drive unexpected release  |
| Boron only beneficial                   | Boron layers become dominant failure mode |
| Short campaigns reflect steady behavior | Long-term cyclic effects dominate         |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Deposit delamination** releasing dust into plasma.
* **Runaway impurity accumulation** in thick deposition zones.
* **Sudden exposure of Mo or graphite** once W layers erode.
* **Migration of erosion zones** due to magnetic configuration changes.
* **Underestimated hydrogen retention** in porous, boron-rich layers.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Why W despite erosion**: prioritized over carbon due to tritium retention limits.
* **Why boronization**: trades impurity control for mechanical fragility.
* **What is not optimized**:

  * Deposit adhesion
  * Long-term stability under thermal cycling
  * Dust minimization

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Results depend on WEST geometry and magnetic ripple, not universal.
* No neutron irradiation considered.
* No active cooling effects included for inertially cooled tiles.
* Atmospheric exposure alters post-mortem chemistry.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Early campaigns (C3)**: mild erosion, identifiable markers.
* **Mid campaigns (C4)**: boron-driven deposition surge.
* **Late campaigns (C5)**: erosion intensifies, deposition shifts, partial flaking occurs.

Long-term implication is **non-stationary wall conditions**.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* No explicit erosion safety factor applied.
* Single-point failures include:

  * Loss of W coating
  * Detachment of thick deposits
* System lacks passive self-healing once Mo or graphite is exposed.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

* **Design regime**: steady erosion, predictable migration.
* **Operative regime**:

  * Zonal instability
  * Chemistry-driven layer failure
  * Configuration-dependent migration
* First-principles predictions are only robust locally and temporally.


---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Materials and Geometry

* Tungsten–molybdenum coated CFC tiles from JET ILW campaigns are assumed representative of **ITER divertor plasma-facing components**, despite structural differences between CFC-backed coatings and bulk W monoblocks.
* The W coating thickness (≈10–15 µm) and Mo interlayer (≈3 µm) are assumed sufficient to decouple plasma effects from the CFC substrate.
* Deposition layers (0–58 µm thick) are treated as continuous, laterally representative layers for correlating thickness with tritium retention.

### Tritium Sources

* Tritium retained in samples is assumed to originate predominantly from:

  * 1.01 MeV tritons produced in D–D fusion reactions
  * Residual in-vessel tritium from earlier D–T campaigns
* Neutron-induced trapping is assumed negligible due to low 14 MeV neutron flux during ILW campaigns.

### Thermal Treatment

* ITER-relevant baking conditions are represented by **350 °C for 100 h**.
* Thermal desorption spectroscopy up to ~1300 K is assumed sufficient to recover essentially all retained tritium.

### Diagnostics

* SEM/EDX cross-sections accurately represent deposition layer thickness and composition.
* TDS and full combustion are assumed to capture the full tritium inventory when combined.
* Tritium decay corrections back to 16/10/2016 are assumed accurate.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Retention Mechanisms

* Co-deposition is implicitly assumed to dominate tritium retention wherever thick deposition layers exist.
* Tritium trapped in bulk tungsten defects is assumed secondary relative to deposition-layer trapping.
* Tritium implantation depth is assumed shallow and not rate-limiting for retention.

### Microstructure

* Deposition layers are assumed homogeneous enough that thickness alone is a meaningful proxy for retention capacity.
* Porosity, cracking, and lamination within Be-rich deposits are not explicitly parameterized.
* Chemical state of deposited Be, including oxidation, is assumed to affect retention but is not quantified.

### Time and Scaling

* Retention accumulated over three ILW campaigns is assumed to be additive and not subject to saturation.
* Results from D–D produced tritium are implicitly extrapolated to ITER D–T conditions.

### Baking Strategy

* Baking is assumed to act purely by thermally activated diffusion and desorption.
* Re-trapping of tritium during bake-out is assumed negligible unless explicitly discussed.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Hydrogen Isotope Diffusion and Trapping

**Principle**
Hydrogen isotopes migrate via thermally activated diffusion and are immobilized by traps such as vacancies, dislocations, interfaces, and impurity phases.

**Governing relation**
[
D = D_0 \exp\left(-\frac{E_a}{kT}\right)
]

**Application**

* Tritium mobility increases with temperature, but release requires overcoming **trap binding energies**, not just lattice diffusion barriers.
* Higher desorption temperatures imply deeper or stronger trapping sites.

**Validity**

* Applies in bulk W and Be layers.
* Breaks down when microstructural transport is dominated by pores or cracks.

---

### 3.2 Co-deposition Physics

**Principle**
When eroded material redeposits together with hydrogen isotopes, the isotopes become incorporated into a growing solid with limited diffusion pathways.

**Application**

* Thick Be-rich deposition layers act as **tritium reservoirs**.
* Retention scales with layer growth rate and available trapping sites.

**Emergent effect**

* Retention becomes decoupled from bulk W properties and dominated by deposit chemistry.

---

### 3.3 Thermal Desorption

**Principle**
Desorption occurs when thermal energy exceeds trap binding energy.

**Application**

* Tile 0 shows desorption peaks at 540–640 °C, well above ITER baking temperature.
* Tile 1 shows lower and broader desorption peaks, indicating weaker or more diverse traps.

**Implication**

* A single baking temperature cannot address all retention states.

---

### 3.4 Heat and Mass Transport in Layered Systems

**Principle**
Layered materials introduce thermal gradients, diffusion barriers, and stress concentrations.

**Application**

* Thick deposition layers reduce effective bake-out by:

  * Increasing diffusion path length
  * Promoting re-trapping
  * Altering thermal conductivity

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Deposition thickness ↔ retention**: Strong correlation for Tile 0, weak for Tile 1, indicating multiple retention regimes.
* **Chemistry ↔ desorption temperature**: Be-rich layers raise desorption temperatures relative to bulk W.
* **Microstructure ↔ baking efficacy**: Looser, porous deposits may both increase retention and facilitate partial release.
* **Location ↔ retention**: Upper inner divertor regions dominate total fuel inventory.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                        | If Violated                                 |
| --------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------- |
| Co-deposition dominates retention | Bulk defect trapping may control desorption |
| 350 °C baking sufficient          | Significant tritium remains trapped         |
| Thickness predicts retention      | Chemistry and porosity dominate instead     |
| D–D tritium comparable to D–T     | ITER inventories underestimated             |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Bake-out inefficacy** leading to cumulative tritium inventory.
* **Localized hot-spot retention** in thick Be layers.
* **Delayed release** during off-normal heating events.
* **Dust generation** from thick, stressed deposition layers carrying trapped tritium.
* **Underestimated inventory** due to incomplete desorption even after TDS.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Why W-based walls**: minimize sputtering and steady retention, at the cost of complex deposit behavior.
* **Why 350 °C baking**: engineering limits, not physics-optimal.
* **Tradeoff**:

  * Higher bake temperatures improve tritium removal
  * But stress coatings, joints, and cooling systems

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No neutron damage representative of ITER fluence.
* No simultaneous helium effects.
* Atmospheric exposure alters Be chemistry post-mortem.
* Carrier gas effects introduce uncertainty in TDS interpretation.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Short term**: Tritium trapped in fresh co-deposits.
* **Mid term**: Layer thickening increases retention and desorption temperature.
* **Long term**: Retention becomes dominated by hardest-to-release traps.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Baking removes at most ~30 % of tritium for most samples.
* Single-point failure is reliance on baking alone for inventory control.
* No passive mechanism guarantees tritium exhaustion from thick deposits.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

* **Design regime**: uniform bake-out, thin deposits, predictable desorption.
* **Operative regime**:

  * Thick, chemically complex deposits
  * Spatially varying desorption temperatures
  * Partial and uncertain tritium removal

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material and Structure

* Graphite is modeled as:

  * Pure
  * Amorphous or effectively isotropic
  * Uniform density around 2.2–2.23 g/cm³
* Pyrolytic graphite anisotropy, crystallographic texture, and grain boundaries are **not explicitly resolved**.
* Surface is assumed initially smooth with no pre-existing roughness, fuzz, or redeposited layers.

### Plasma–Material Interaction Regime

* Incident ions He⁺, D⁺, and T⁺ are assumed to dominate erosion physics in the **near-threshold energy regime**.
* Incident energies are assumed sufficiently low that:

  * Binary Collision Approximation remains valid
  * Collective plasma effects, sheath acceleration, and molecular ions are neglected
* Angle of incidence is treated as a controllable independent variable between 0° and 80°.

### Simulation Validity

* RDS-BASIC and SDTrimSP are assumed to capture the essential physics of:

  * Physical sputtering
  * Chemical sputtering
  * Their coupling near threshold energies
* SDTrimSP’s chemical sputtering model is assumed representative despite being a reduced BCA-based proxy for chemistry.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Surface Physics

* The surface binding energy Es is treated as a tunable constant rather than an emergent property dependent on:

  * Hydrogen saturation
  * Surface reconstruction
  * Temperature
* Reduced displacement thresholds for surface atoms implicitly assume:

  * No dynamic surface roughening
  * No evolution of local incidence angle due to erosion

### Chemical Kinetics

* Chemical sputtering is assumed to be governed primarily by:

  * Bond breaking thresholds
  * Local hydrogen concentration
* Real chemical kinetics, including:

  * Reaction pathways
  * Time-dependent diffusion
  * Recombination barriers
    are not explicitly modeled.

### Temporal Effects

* Simulations assume steady-state erosion per incident ion.
* No incubation, saturation, or surface poisoning effects are included.
* Hydrogen inventory is assumed not to saturate trapping sites beyond modeled limits.

### Geometry and Scaling

* Local angle-of-incidence dependence is assumed to scale directly to macroscopic divertor geometries.
* Shadowing, self-redeposition, and evolving topography are neglected.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Binary Collision Physics

**Principle**
Sputtering occurs when momentum transfer from an incident ion exceeds surface binding and displacement energies.

**Application**

* Grazing incidence increases near-surface collision probability.
* Effective sputtering threshold decreases with angle due to reduced normal momentum requirement.

**Validity Regime**

* Low-energy ions
* Short collision cascades
* Breaks down for collective or thermal spike effects.

---

### 3.2 Angle-Dependent Energy Partitioning

**Principle**
At oblique incidence, a larger fraction of ion energy is deposited parallel to the surface.

**Application**

* Explains higher sputtering yields at 60°–80°.
* Explains threshold reduction with increasing angle.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Increased backscattering at very grazing angles.
* Competition between surface skimming and penetration.

---

### 3.3 Chemical Bond Disruption

**Principle**
Chemical sputtering initiates when sufficient energy exists to break C–C bonds and allow volatile hydrocarbon formation.

**Application**

* First threshold around 5 eV corresponds to bond breaking.
* Requires hydrogen isotope availability near surface.

---

### 3.4 Coupling of Physical and Chemical Sputtering

**Principle**
Physical displacement creates reactive dangling bonds that enhance chemical erosion.

**Application**

* Secondary threshold at 8–13 eV aligns with physical sputtering onset.
* Chemical sputtering enhancement above this threshold is displacement-driven.

**Emergent Phenomenon**

* Two-step threshold behavior not predictable from chemistry or mechanics alone.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Angle ↔ threshold coupling**
  Increasing angle lowers physical threshold, which in turn activates chemical enhancement earlier.
* **Energy ↔ mechanism dominance**
  Chemical sputtering dominates near threshold, physical sputtering dominates above ~55 eV.
* **Backscattering ↔ yield peak location**
  Competing effects shift maximum yield angle depending on code assumptions.
* **Ion species ↔ cascade depth**
  He, D, and T differ in penetration depth, modifying surface vs subsurface chemistry balance.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                | Consequence if Violated                        |
| ------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------- |
| Smooth surface            | Real roughness shifts local angle distribution |
| Static target composition | Hydrogen saturation alters Es and Eth          |
| BCA validity              | Collective effects may increase erosion        |
| Constant Es               | Threshold predictions become inaccurate        |
| No redeposition           | Net erosion may be overestimated               |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Localized runaway erosion** at specific angles due to geometric focusing.
* **Accelerated thinning** near divertor strike points with oblique flux.
* **Underpredicted erosion** if redeposition and re-erosion cycles occur.
* **Misleading lifetime estimates** when angular distributions evolve dynamically.
* **Unexpected tritium retention coupling**, since chemical sputtering creates hydrocarbons.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Why graphite despite erosion**
  Low-Z impurity tolerance and helium fuzz immunity outweigh erosion penalties.
* **Tradeoff not optimized**
  Graphite erosion versus fuel retention and dust production.
* **Design implication**
  Accept erosion to preserve plasma stability.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No neutron damage included.
* No thermal gradients or transient heat loads.
* No plasma sheath acceleration effects.
* No mixed-material interfaces modeled.
* Results are local, not system-integrated.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* Early exposure dominated by chemical sputtering.
* As surface roughens, local angle distribution broadens.
* Over time, physical sputtering dominates net erosion.
* Chemical sputtering self-limits at higher energies.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* No explicit safety factor on erosion yield.
* Single-point sensitivity to surface angle distribution.
* Loss of graphite thickness can accelerate exponentially once geometry changes.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

### Design Regime

* Controlled incidence angles
* Smooth surfaces
* Predictable thresholds

### Operative Regime

* Evolving roughness
* Broad angular distributions
* Coupled chemical–physical erosion

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material State and Baseline

* The material is an **11Cr ferritic/martensitic steel** in a *normalized-and-tempered* condition.
* Initial microstructure is assumed to be **well stabilized** by prior heat treatment at 780 °C for 1.5 h.
* δ-ferrite fraction of approximately **15 %** is assumed uniform and representative of bulk behavior.
* Pre-existing precipitate populations (Cr-rich M23C6, Nb-Ta-rich MX, σ-FeCr, σ-FeCrMo) are treated as known, stable reference states.

### Irradiation Conditions

* Ion irradiation with **3.5 MeV Fe13+** ions is assumed to be a valid surrogate for neutron damage.
* Damage level of **0.84 dpa** is treated as “low dose” but physically meaningful.
* Irradiation temperature of **700 °C ± 10 °C** is assumed spatially and temporally uniform.
* SRIM-calculated damage and implantation profiles are assumed accurate.

### Characterization

* TEM, SAED, and EDX are assumed sufficient to:

  * Identify precipitate crystal structures
  * Distinguish multiphase precipitates
  * Quantify elemental ratios meaningfully at the nanoscale

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Surrogacy and Scaling

* **Ion irradiation ≈ neutron irradiation** in terms of precipitate evolution, despite:

  * Strongly non-uniform damage profiles
  * Absence of transmutation products
  * Different cascade morphology and dose rates
* Thin foil behavior is implicitly assumed representative of bulk cladding material.

### Thermodynamic vs Kinetic Control

* Phase formation is implicitly governed by **irradiation-enhanced diffusion**, not equilibrium thermodynamics.
* Local non-equilibrium states are assumed stable enough to be captured post-irradiation.

### Carbon and Nitrogen Availability

* Carbon and nitrogen redistribution is assumed to be:

  * Driven by defect trapping
  * Not globally limited by prior tempering
* Local supersaturation near defects is assumed possible even after high-temperature tempering.

### Defect Structure

* Radiation-induced defects (loops, black dots, dislocations) are assumed:

  * Numerous
  * Stable at 700 °C
  * Effective sinks for solutes

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Radiation Damage and Point Defect Physics

**Principle**
Energetic ions create displacement cascades producing vacancies, interstitials, and defect clusters.

**Application**

* These defects act as:

  * Fast diffusion pathways
  * Solute traps
  * Nucleation sites for new phases

**Validity Regime**

* Low to moderate dpa
* Breaks down if defect recombination dominates at higher temperatures or lower flux.

---

### 3.2 Irradiation-Enhanced Diffusion

**Principle**
Effective diffusion coefficient increases due to defect-mediated transport.

**Application**

* Enables precipitation of M23C6 inside martensite laths where thermal diffusion alone is insufficient.
* Drives transformation and dissolution of NbC-based MX precipitates.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Solute depletion zones
* Local composition inversion near boundaries

---

### 3.3 Phase Stability Under Non-Equilibrium

**Principle**
Under irradiation, metastable phases can form that are thermodynamically unfavorable at equilibrium.

**Application**

* Formation of:

  * NbC (fcc, a = 1.115 nm)
  * Nb2C
  * Nb6C5
  * Fe-Cr intermetallics
* Indicates dominance of kinetic pathways over free-energy minimization.

---

### 3.4 Solute Partitioning Between Phases

**Principle**
Elements partition to phases where chemical potential is locally minimized.

**Application**

* δ-ferrite enriched in Cr and W favors:

  * σ-FeCrW
  * Fe-Cr intermetallics
  * Cr3C2 precipitation
* Martensite favors M23C6 evolution.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **Defects ↔ diffusion ↔ precipitation**
  Radiation defects accelerate diffusion, enabling new precipitate nucleation, which in turn alters solute availability.
* **Carbon availability ↔ phase selection**
  Low C in δ-ferrite biases toward Cr3C2 rather than M23C6.
* **NbC dissolution ↔ secondary Nb phases**
  Partial dissolution of NbC seeds Nb2C and Nb6C5 formation.
* **Boundary chemistry ↔ intermetallic formation**
  δ-ferrite–martensite boundaries act as chemical and kinetic hotspots.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                            | Consequence if Violated                  |
| ------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------- |
| Ion irradiation mimics neutron damage | Bulk reactor behavior may differ         |
| Uniform temperature                   | Local overheating alters phase stability |
| Stable defect population              | Precipitation pathways change            |
| Thin foil equivalence                 | Surface effects exaggerate diffusion     |
| SRIM damage accuracy                  | dpa misestimation skews conclusions      |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Creep strength degradation** due to:

  * Dissolution of fine MX precipitates
  * Formation of brittle intermetallics
* **Embrittlement** from σ-FeCrW and Fe-Cr phases at boundaries.
* **Loss of long-term stability** if irradiation-induced phases coarsen during service.
* **Heterogeneous mechanical response** driven by δ-ferrite-localized transformations.
* **Unanticipated phase reversibility** under thermal cycling or higher doses.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* Steel chemistry optimized for **creep and oxidation**, not radiation stability.
* Nb additions improve creep but introduce complex irradiation sensitivity.
* δ-ferrite improves solidification but becomes a liability under irradiation.
* Tradeoff accepted between:

  * High-temperature strength
  * Radiation-induced microstructural complexity

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No mechanical stress applied during irradiation.
* No coolant chemistry interaction.
* No cyclic thermal loading.
* No helium or hydrogen transmutation effects.
* Results are local microstructural, not component-scale.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Early irradiation**: defect accumulation, solute segregation.
* **Intermediate stage**: irradiation-induced precipitation and phase transformation.
* **Long term (unstudied)**:

  * Coarsening
  * Phase instability
  * Possible dissolution or transformation under creep.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* No explicit margin against irradiation-induced phase instability.
* δ-ferrite acts as a **single-point vulnerability**.
* Nb-rich precipitates show **low robustness** to irradiation.
* Lack of data on long-term thermal stability of Cr3C2 and Nb6C5 phases.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

### Design Regime

* Tempered microstructure
* Stable M23C6 and MX strengthening
* Predictable creep behavior

### Operative Regime

* Radiation-driven non-equilibrium phase evolution
* Boundary-localized intermetallic formation
* Competing strengthening and embrittlement mechanisms

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Plasma Facing Components and Geometry

* All **plasma-facing components are tungsten**, including main chamber, divertor, and inboard limiters.
* Inboard-limited plasmas are assumed to be **ITER-relevant** during current ramp-up.
* WEST geometry and limiter configuration are assumed to be a **meaningful analog** for ITER startup physics.

### Boronization Physics

* Glow Discharge Boronization using **He + B₂D₆** produces a **nanometric, amorphous B-rich layer**.
* Boronization provides three explicit benefits:

  1. Oxygen gettering
  2. Increased wall pumping and reduced recycling
  3. Transient shielding of W from sputtering
* A **non-uniform B layer** is explicitly considered acceptable for plasma operation.

### Diagnostics and Metrics

* Radiated power fraction **Frad** is a valid proxy for impurity contamination and plasma quality.
* UV and visible spectroscopy line intensities are assumed to be **qualitative indicators** of impurity sourcing.
* Matching pulses before and after boronization isolates wall effects from plasma control effects.

### Operational Timescales

* Effects of boronization are assumed to be **transient**, fading with cumulated plasma time and energy.
* Short-term restart performance is prioritized over long-term steady-state optimization.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Wall Inventory and Outgassing

* Tungsten surfaces are assumed to retain **large reservoirs of H, O, N, and C** after venting.
* Bake and D₂ glow discharge alone are assumed **insufficient** to remove these reservoirs.
* Boronization acts as a **chemical cap**, not merely a surface coating.

### Spatial Averaging

* Plasma performance is implicitly assumed to be governed by **global impurity content**, not by localized B-depleted regions.
* Toroidal asymmetry in B thickness is assumed not to trigger localized failure modes during startup.

### Plasma–Wall Coupling

* Detached, cold plasmas are implicitly assumed to be **self-sustaining impurity amplifiers** via radiative collapse.
* Wall conditions dominate plasma behavior more strongly than plasma control parameters during restart.

### Scaling to ITER

* WEST results are assumed scalable to ITER despite:

  * Smaller size
  * Lower pulse energy
  * Different magnetic topology and wall area to volume ratio

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Plasma Radiation and Energy Balance

**Principle**
Total plasma power balance must satisfy
P_ohmic + P_aux = P_rad + P_transport + P_wall

**Application**

* Without boronization, **P_rad ≈ P_ohmic**, causing radiative collapse.
* Boronization reduces impurity radiation, restoring net heating.

**Validity Regime**

* L-mode, cold and dense plasmas
* Breaks down in high-temperature or strong auxiliary heating regimes.

---

### 3.2 Surface Chemistry and Gettering

**Principle**
Reactive surfaces lower impurity partial pressures by forming stable compounds.

**Application**

* Boron binds O and possibly N, forming stable boron oxides and nitrides.
* Reduces recycling and impurity influx from unwetted wall areas.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Reduced impurity sputtering lowers W erosion indirectly.
* Chemical saturation limits gettering lifetime.

---

### 3.3 Sputtering and Shielding

**Principle**
Sputtering yield depends on surface binding energy and incident particle energy.

**Application**

* B layer has lower sputtering threshold than W and is eroded preferentially.
* Acts as a sacrificial shield during early operation.

**Limits**

* Effect is transient in net erosion zones such as divertor strike points.

---

### 3.4 Fuel Retention and Recycling

**Principle**
Hydrogen isotopes are retained via implantation, trapping, and chemical bonding.

**Application**

* Fresh B layers show strong short-term D₂ retention.
* Leads to low density and attached plasmas immediately after boronization.

**Tradeoff**

* Improved impurity control at the cost of transient fuel inventory increase.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **B layer thickness ↔ impurity gettering ↔ Frad**
* **Impurity reduction ↔ reduced W sputtering ↔ lower core radiation**
* **B erosion ↔ plasma time and energy ↔ loss of benefits**
* **Limiter versus divertor exposure ↔ spatial persistence of B effects**
* **Fuel retention ↔ density control ↔ Te0 and attachment state**

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                  | Consequence if Violated                              |
| --------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------- |
| Non-uniform B is sufficient | Localized impurity sources could dominate            |
| Frad reflects wall state    | Core transport changes could confound interpretation |
| WEST mimics ITER startup    | ITER may exhibit stronger asymmetry sensitivity      |
| B layer chemistry stable    | Unexpected compound formation alters behavior        |
| Detached plasmas benign     | Prolonged detachment may damage components           |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

* **Radiative collapse** driven by light impurities without B gettering.
* **Uncontrolled density rise** from hydrogen outgassing in unboronized W.
* **Rapid loss of B in wetted zones**, leading to uneven impurity sourcing.
* **Transient W sputtering spikes** immediately after boronization due to attached plasmas.
* **Fuel retention risks** for ITER tritium inventory control.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* ITER port limitations constrain **toroidal symmetry** of conditioning systems.
* Boronization trades **long-term wall purity** for **short-term restart capability**.
* Non-uniform boronization reduces system complexity but sacrifices predictability.
* B layers mitigate impurities but introduce **fuel retention penalties**.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No runaway electron mitigation active during restart.
* Ohmic and LHCD-dominated scenarios only.
* Divertor predominantly attached during characterization pulses.
* No ICRH or ECRH coupling during main analysis.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

* **Immediately after boronization**

  * Low density
  * Low Frad
  * Strong impurity suppression
* **Within one operational day**

  * Partial B erosion
  * Rising Frad
  * Increased W and Cu signals
* **Longer term**

  * Convergence toward pre-B conditions
  * Residual oxygen and nitrogen suppression persists

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Boronization provides **operational margin against impurity-driven disruptions**.
* Margin erodes with cumulated plasma exposure.
* Non-uniform B layers reduce dependence on full symmetry but increase uncertainty.
* Single-point vulnerability: loss of B in main chamber limiters.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

### Design Regime

* Full-W ITER with controlled startup via boronization
* Assumed impurity gettering and manageable recycling

### Operative Regime

* Asymmetric B deposition
* Transient wall chemistry
* Strong coupling between wall state and plasma stability

---WILLGPTSTART---

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material and Microstructure

* Materials are **bcc tungsten, molybdenum, and iron**, representative of fusion-relevant structural alloys.
* Grain boundaries are modeled as **idealized symmetric tilt or low-angle GBs** with well-defined Σ values.
* GB structures are initially **static, crystalline, and defect-free** prior to SIA loading.
* Nanocrystalline behavior is inferred from **single-GB simulations**, not from full polycrystalline networks.

### Defect Population

* Radiation damage is dominated by **self-interstitial atoms (SIAs)** and vacancies.
* SIAs exist primarily as **crowdions or clusters (SIAn)** rather than isolated point defects.
* Vacancies are assumed immobile at low temperature relative to SIAs.
* Hydrogen and helium are not explicitly included in the core simulations.

### Simulation Framework

* **Embedded Atom Method (EAM)** potentials adequately represent defect energetics and GB behavior.
* Molecular statics at **0 K** captures the dominant trapping and blocking mechanisms.
* Molecular dynamics at controlled temperatures captures qualitative thermal activation trends.
* Periodic boundary conditions are assumed to not artificially bias GB saturation behavior.

---

## 2. HIDDEN AND IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Representativeness of Single GBs

* A single GB segment is assumed to represent **average GB behavior**, despite known variability in:

  * Free volume
  * Structural units
  * Local chemistry
* Triple junctions and GB curvature effects are neglected.

### Defect Flux Balance

* SIA influx to GBs is implicitly assumed to **exceed vacancy influx**, especially at low temperature.
* Continuous SIA injection mimics irradiation, but **cascade spatial correlations** are ignored.

### Mechanical Constraints

* No external stress is applied.
* Elastic interactions are assumed sufficient to describe defect energetics.
* Plastic relaxation or GB migration is not allowed unless thermally activated in MD.

### Timescale Separation

* Structural relaxation is treated as instantaneous relative to defect generation.
* Long-term microstructural evolution is extrapolated from short MD times.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### 3.1 Point Defect Thermodynamics

**Law**
Defect formation energy governs equilibrium stability.

**Application**

* SIAs lower their energy by migrating to GB free volume.
* GBs act as deep potential wells for SIAs.

**Limits**

* Valid under low solute concentration.
* Breaks down under GB saturation.

---

### 3.2 Defect Kinetics and Migration

**Law**
Diffusion rate follows Arrhenius behavior
D ∝ exp(−Em/kT)

**Application**

* SIAs migrate orders of magnitude faster than vacancies.
* SIAs dominate early GB interactions at low temperature.

**Limits**

* At elevated temperature, blocking states dissolve.
* Vacancy mobility eventually competes.

---

### 3.3 Elastic Interaction and Strain Energy

**Law**
Elastic strain energy scales with defect volume and interaction range.

**Application**

* Bulk SIAs generate long-range <111> strain fields.
* Trapped SIAs collapse strain fields at the GB.
* Blocked SIAs preserve extended strain fields.

**Second-Order Effects**

* Long-range repulsion of incoming SIAs.
* Modification of local diffusion landscapes.

---

### 3.4 Free Volume and Structural Unit Saturation

**Law**
Finite free volume limits defect accommodation.

**Application**

* GB structural units accommodate only a finite number of SIAs.
* Excess SIAs increase local energy and induce blocking.

**Limits**

* GB phase transformations may alter capacity.
* High temperature enables reconfiguration.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES

* **SIA accumulation ↔ GB free volume exhaustion**
* **GB saturation ↔ emergence of blocking states**
* **Blocking state morphology ↔ vacancy annihilation volume**
* **Blocking state strain field ↔ SIA repulsion**
* **Temperature ↔ stability of blocking vs trapped states**

These interactions form a **nonlinear feedback loop** that governs defect evolution near GBs.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

| Assumption                         | If Violated                          |
| ---------------------------------- | ------------------------------------ |
| GB is an infinite sink             | Blocking and reflection dominate     |
| SIAs always annihilate vacancies   | Repulsion reduces net annihilation   |
| Trapped SIAs are optimal           | Blocked SIAs outperform in V removal |
| Low temperature preserves trapping | Thermal escape dissolves blocking    |
| Defect flux is uniform             | Local pile-up alters GB behavior     |

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

### Microstructural Degradation

* **GB saturation** reduces long-term radiation tolerance.
* **SIA reflection** increases bulk defect density.
* **Extended strain fields** promote defect clustering away from GBs.

### Mechanical Consequences

* Localized strain near GBs increases:

  * Embrittlement risk
  * Stress-assisted void formation
* Reduced effectiveness of GBs as dislocation sinks.

### Radiation Resistance Breakdown

* Loss of “perfect sink” behavior under sustained irradiation.
* Transition from defect elimination to defect redistribution.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* Nanocrystalline design trades **sink density** for **sink capacity limits**.
* Strong GB trapping improves early-stage tolerance but accelerates saturation.
* Blocked SIAs enhance vacancy annihilation but impair SIA absorption.
* Optimal radiation resistance is **temperature-window dependent**.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* No alloying elements considered.
* No transmutation products included.
* No irradiation-induced segregation of solutes.
* No GB migration or grain growth modeled.

Thus, results apply primarily to **early-to-intermediate irradiation stages** in clean bcc metals.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION

### Early Irradiation

* Rapid SIA migration to GBs.
* Efficient defect annihilation.

### Intermediate Stage

* GB trapping sites saturate.
* Self-blocking emerges.
* Vacancy annihilation peaks near GB.

### Later Stage

* SIA reflection into bulk.
* Reduced net defect removal.
* Potential buildup of bulk damage.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* GBs possess a **finite defect absorption capacity**.
* Blocking provides a secondary annihilation mechanism but is not fail-safe.
* Elevated temperature reduces blocking but risks GB instability.
* Single-point vulnerability exists if GB structural units saturate locally.

---

## 11. DESIGN VS OPERATIVE REGIME

### Design Regime

* Nanocrystalline metals with high GB density.
* Low-temperature irradiation.
* Assumed infinite GB sink efficiency.

### Operative Regime

* Continuous defect injection.
* GB saturation and self-blocking.
* Emergent boundary recombination zones.
* Reduced effectiveness of classical sink models.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Geometry and Configuration

* Castellated plasma-facing components consist of **millimeter-scale gaps** between tungsten monoblocks or tiles.
* The investigated geometry uses:

  * Tungsten armor thickness of **8 mm**
  * Gap width of **0.4 mm**
  * Gap angled **5° relative to surface normal**
* The geometry is assumed to be representative of divertor monoblocks in devices such as **ITER**, **EAST**, **WEST**, and DEMO-class reactors.

### Materials

* Plasma-facing material is **polycrystalline tungsten (W)**.
* Heat sink material is **CuCrZr**.
* Material properties such as diffusion coefficients, sputtering thresholds, and reflection behavior are implicitly taken from established literature values.

### Plasma Conditions

* Edge plasma parameters are explicitly defined:

  * Electron temperature **Te = 2–6 eV**
  * Electron density **ne = 10¹⁶–10¹⁷ m⁻³**
  * Ion temperature **Ti = 0.026 eV**
* Magnetic field strength is **300 G**, oriented **85°** to the surface.
* Incident ion energies are controlled via applied bias in the range **0 to −200 V**, with simulations focused on **−100 V**.

### Operating Regime

* Plasma-driven permeation (PDP) is treated as **steady-state** over experimental and simulation timescales.
* The measured permeation flux at the CuCrZr backside is assumed to be representative of total hydrogen isotope transport through the system.

---

## 2. HIDDEN / IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Manufacturing and Assembly

* Gaps are assumed to have **uniform width, smooth sidewalls, and precise angular alignment**.
* Mechanical pressing between tungsten and CuCrZr is assumed to produce **intimate, void-free contact**, with negligible thermal contact resistance.
* No consideration is given to gap-edge chipping, burrs, or local misalignment from manufacturing tolerances.

### Surface State

* Tungsten surfaces are implicitly assumed to have:

  * Stable oxide or impurity layers prior to plasma exposure
  * Homogeneous crystallographic orientation at the scale relevant to sputtering and reflection
* Bias-induced sputtering is assumed to remove impurities without introducing significant roughness or microstructural damage.

### Plasma Neutral Physics

* Neutral particle transport inside gaps is inferred indirectly from reflected-ion behavior.
* Reflection is assumed to follow a **cosine angular distribution**, independent of surface condition evolution.

### Temporal Stability

* No evolution of:

  * Surface morphology
  * Defect density
  * Trapping site population
  * Thermal fatigue or cracking
    is considered during experiments or simulations.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Electrostatics and Plasma Sheath Physics

**Poisson’s Equation**
[
\nabla^2 \phi = -\frac{\rho}{\varepsilon_0}
]

* Governs self-consistent electric fields in the sheath and gap entrance.
* Valid in collisionless or weakly collisional sheath regimes.
* Strongly geometry-dependent, particularly near sharp corners and gap entrances.

**Debye Shielding**
[
\lambda_D = \sqrt{\frac{\varepsilon_0 k T_e}{n_e e^2}}
]

* Sets the minimum spatial resolution for PIC simulations.
* Assumes quasi-neutral plasma upstream of the sheath.
* Breaks down locally near walls and in narrow gaps where strong gradients exist.

### Space-Charge-Limited Ion Transport

**Child–Langmuir Law**
[
J \propto \frac{V^{3/2}}{d^2}
]

* Applied to explain reduced ion flux under thicker biased sheaths.
* Strictly valid for 1D planar, collisionless, zero-magnetic-field conditions.
* Its use here implicitly neglects strong magnetic obliquity and 2D effects.

### Particle Dynamics

**Lorentz Force**
[
\mathbf{F} = q(\mathbf{E} + \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B})
]

* Governs ion deflection at gap entrances.
* Valid as long as gyro-radius is comparable to or larger than gap dimensions.
* Assumes static magnetic field and no turbulence.

### Surface Interaction Physics

* **Sputtering yield** increases with incident energy above material-dependent thresholds.
* **Reflection probability** increases at high energy and near-normal incidence.
* **Hydrogen recombination and desorption** depend on surface cleanliness and defect states.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Sheath thickening under bias** reduces ion density via Child–Langmuir scaling, but **increases ion energy**, intensifying sputtering.
* **Electric field geometry** interacts with **magnetic field orientation**, determining whether ions impact sidewalls or gap bottoms.
* **Ion energy spectrum** governs the balance between:

  * Retention via implantation and diffusion
  * Loss via sputtering and reflection
* **Surface cleanliness** feeds back into recombination rates, altering effective permeation even at constant flux.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

* If **gap width deviates** from 0.4 mm, sheath overlap and electric field focusing change, invalidating the assumed ion trajectories.
* If **surface roughness increases**, cosine reflection assumptions fail, altering neutral transport and retention.
* If **CuCrZr diffusion properties change** due to radiation damage, the assumption that copper dominates permeation may no longer hold.
* If **plasma parameters fluctuate**, steady-state sheath solutions become invalid, breaking the Child–Langmuir-based flux interpretation.

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

### Material Deviations

* Increased tungsten defect density could shift the system from a reflection-dominated to a trapping-dominated regime.
* CuCrZr embrittlement or oxidation could introduce new trapping reservoirs.

### Environmental Excursions

* Higher-than-assumed ion energies could cross sputtering thresholds for tungsten itself, altering gap geometry over time.
* Temperature cycling could open microcracks at the W–Cu interface, creating new permeation shortcuts.

### Manufacturing Tolerances

* Non-parallel gap walls could focus electric fields, creating localized hot spots and enhanced erosion.
* Misalignment could restore partial line-of-sight, invalidating the assumed shielding effect.

### Maintenance and Aging

* Repeated plasma exposure could smooth or roughen surfaces, shifting reflection statistics.
* Long-term tritium buildup in copper could exceed safety limits despite short-term suppression.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **Tungsten selection** prioritizes high melting point and low sputtering yield over manufacturability and brittleness.
* **Gap angling** sacrifices plasma transparency for reduced direct implantation risk.
* **Biasing strategy** trades increased ion energy for reduced net retention, implicitly accepting higher local erosion.
* The system is **not optimized for transient events** such as ELMs or disruptions.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Upstream plasma is assumed uniform and stationary.
* Downstream boundary assumes immediate detection of permeated species without re-adsorption.
* No coupling is modeled between:

  * Plasma source terms
  * Bulk diffusion and trapping
  * Coolant-side chemistry
* Thermal transport between tungsten and CuCrZr is assumed ideal.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* No fatigue accumulation or creep in tungsten.
* No time-dependent change in diffusion coefficients.
* No progressive tritium inventory buildup considered.
* No radiation-induced segregation or transmutation effects.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Safety margins rely on **reflection-dominated suppression** of permeation under bias.
* Single-point failures include:

  * Loss of bias control
  * Surface condition changes
  * Gap closure or widening
* No graceful degradation mechanism is described if reflection efficiency drops.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design Regime

* Steady plasma
* Clean, well-defined gaps
* Controlled bias and temperature

### Likely Operative Regime

* Fluctuating plasma flux
* Evolving surfaces
* Mixed ion and neutral populations
* Long-term material degradation

### Robustness Assessment

* First-principles analysis is robust for **short-term, steady-state behavior**.
* Predictive power degrades significantly under:

  * Transients
  * Long-term exposure
  * Material evolution
* Full system fidelity requires coupling PIC plasma models with **bulk hydrogen transport solvers** and **surface evolution models**, as correctly acknowledged in the text.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material System

* The breeder material consists of **biphasic ACB pebbles** with a **nominal composition of 70 mol% Li₄SiO₄ (LOS) and 30 mol% Li₂TiO₃ (LMT)**.
* Two batches, ACB-1 and ACB-2, are assumed to have **comparable bulk material properties**, despite acknowledged microstructural variability.
* Cubic Li₂TiO₃ present in pristine samples is explicitly assumed to be **metastable**, formed during rapid cooling in the KALOS process.

### Operating Environment

* Thermal cycling is conducted between **300 and 800 °C**.
* Atmosphere is **He + 0.1 vol% H₂** at **1200 mbar absolute pressure**.
* Oxygen and moisture levels are assumed sufficiently low to prevent oxidation or parasitic reactions.
* The environment is intended to be representative of breeder blanket conditions in fusion systems such as **ITER** and DEMO-class reactors.

### Thermal Loading

* One cycle consists of:

  * 12 h dwell at 300 °C
  * 12 h dwell at 800 °C
* Heating rate during cycling is **10 K/min**.
* Cooling rate is **~0.5 K/min**, limited by furnace capability.
* A total of **30 cycles** is assumed sufficient to probe relevant degradation mechanisms.

### Metrics of Stability

* Stability is evaluated via:

  * Chemical composition by ICP-OES
  * Phase composition by XRD
  * Microstructure by SEM
  * Porosity by He pycnometry and Hg porosimetry
  * Mechanical integrity via single-pebble crush load tests
* Absence of lithium loss, cracking, porosity evolution, or progressive strength degradation is interpreted as acceptable long-term performance.

---

## 2. HIDDEN / IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Thermal Shock Severity

* The study implicitly assumes that **slow cooling rates are representative or at least acceptable proxies** for reactor-relevant thermal cycling.
* High dT/dt transients, asymmetric heating, or local hot spots are not considered.

### Mechanical Loading State

* Pebbles are tested **without compressive preload**, shear, or pebble-pebble contact forces.
* Mechanical interaction within a packed pebble bed under neutron irradiation and thermal gradients is implicitly neglected.

### Chemical Interactions

* Hydrogen is assumed to be chemically benign at 0.1 vol%.
* No consideration is given to:

  * Tritium effects
  * LiOH formation
  * Redox cycling under off-normal oxygen ingress

### Microstructural Representativeness

* Two observed microstructure types are assumed to span the relevant production variability.
* Grain boundary chemistry, point defect populations, and lithium mobility at interfaces are assumed stable after early phase transformation.

### Statistical Sufficiency

* Crush load testing of **40 pebbles per condition** is assumed to adequately capture mechanical variability, despite large standard deviations.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Thermodynamics of Phase Stability

* The cubic to monoclinic Li₂TiO₃ transformation is driven by **free energy minimization**.
* The phase transition implies:

  * Change in crystal symmetry
  * Increase in unit cell volume
* This transformation is thermodynamically favored below ~1155 °C.

### Thermal Expansion and Stress

[
\sigma \approx E \alpha \Delta T
]

* Differential thermal expansion between LOS and LMT generates internal stresses.
* Valid in the linear elastic regime.
* Stress accumulation is mitigated here by slow thermal ramps.

### Diffusion Kinetics

[
D = D_0 \exp\left(-\frac{Q}{RT}\right)
]

* Limited grain growth implies diffusion coefficients for Si and Ti in the opposite phase are low.
* Valid assuming defect-limited diffusion and no radiation enhancement.

### Porosity Conservation

* Open and closed porosities are governed by sintering kinetics and pore coalescence.
* No densification or pore growth indicates the system remains below sintering activation thresholds during cycling.

### Fracture Mechanics

* Crush load reduction reflects changes in flaw population or residual stress state.
* Absence of progressive decline indicates no fatigue crack accumulation under tested conditions.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Phase transformation** directly alters **mechanical strength** via lattice reordering and volumetric strain.
* **Biphasic microstructure** limits diffusion, which stabilizes grain size and porosity.
* **Slow cooling rates** reduce thermal stress, suppressing crack initiation despite large temperature swings.
* **Chemical stability** preserves phase equilibria, preventing secondary phase formation that could embrittle the structure.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

* If cooling rates were increased, thermal stress could exceed fracture toughness, invalidating the assumption of crack-free cycling.
* If hydrogen concentration increased or tritium were present, diffusion and defect chemistry could change, altering phase stability.
* If the biphasic distribution were less uniform, local stress concentrations could dominate failure behavior.

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

### Thermal Transients

* Rapid shutdown or plasma disruptions could induce:

  * Thermal shock cracking
  * Intergranular decohesion
* These modes are implicitly avoided by the slow cooling protocol.

### Mechanical Interaction

* Pebble bed compaction could amplify the observed early-cycle strength loss into fragmentation.
* Load redistribution after crush load reduction is not explored.

### Radiation Effects

* Neutron irradiation could:

  * Enhance diffusion
  * Stabilize or destabilize specific phases
  * Modify porosity via helium bubble formation
* None of these are addressed.

### Chemical Off-Normal Events

* Oxygen ingress could form Li₂CO₃ or LiOH, altering mechanical and thermal properties.
* The slight positive pressure is assumed sufficient to prevent this indefinitely.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* **LOS–LMT ratio** balances lithium density against mechanical robustness.
* **KALOS processing** prioritizes spherical geometry and scalability over microstructural uniformity.
* The system is optimized for **long-term thermal exposure**, not extreme transient resilience.
* No optimization is evident for minimizing early-cycle strength degradation.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* Pebbles are treated as **isolated systems**, not as elements of a stressed, irradiated, flowing breeder bed.
* No coupling is made to:

  * Helium purge flow heat transfer
  * Tritium generation and release
  * Structural constraints from blanket modules
* Results implicitly assume uniform temperature distribution within individual pebbles.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* Most irreversible changes occur within the first two cycles.
* After phase stabilization, properties are assumed time-invariant.
* No cumulative damage, creep, or fatigue is assumed beyond early transformation effects.
* Long-term operation beyond 30 cycles is extrapolated implicitly.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Safety margin relies on:

  * Chemical stability of lithium
  * Absence of fragmentation
  * Stabilization of mechanical strength after early cycling
* Single-point vulnerabilities include:

  * Faster cooling rates
  * Mechanical confinement
  * Radiation-enhanced damage
* No self-healing or load-redistribution mechanism is discussed if pebble failure occurs.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design Regime

* Slow thermal ramps
* Clean reducing atmosphere
* Unconstrained pebbles
* No irradiation

### Likely Operative Regime

* Spatial thermal gradients
* Mechanical confinement
* Neutron and gamma irradiation

### Robustness Assessment

* First-principles behavior is robust for **pure thermal cycling under mild gradients**.
* Applicability degrades as additional coupled fields are introduced.
* The study establishes a **baseline thermochemical qualification**, not a full reactor-equivalent validation.

## 1. EXPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Material State and Processing

* The tungsten is **powder-metallurgy produced and heavily cold-worked** by multi-pass rolling to ∼90 percent thickness reduction.
* No stress-relief annealing is performed prior to plasma exposure, so a **high stored dislocation density** is assumed and intentionally preserved.
* Chemical purity above **99.95 wt percent W** implies impurity drag effects on recrystallization are negligible.

### Plasma Exposure Conditions

* Helium plasma exposure produces **subsurface He bubbles without fuzz formation**, ensured by limiting surface temperature to ∼853 K and ion energy to ∼50 eV.
* The resulting He bubble population is shallow, nanoscale, and initially stable during subsequent annealing.
* The He plasma condition is treated as representative of near-surface helium production in fusion PFCs, such as those foreseen in **ITER**.

### Annealing Regime

* Recrystallization is studied at **1573 K**, a temperature explicitly assumed to lie within the active recrystallization window of tungsten.
* Interrupted annealing steps of **15 min** are sufficient to resolve nucleation and early growth processes.
* Electron-beam annealing is assumed to provide **uniform heating** of the exposed surface with negligible thermal gradients through the probed depth.

### Microstructural Diagnostics

* EBSD-based criteria combining **grain orientation spread and orientation gradient** accurately discriminate recrystallized from deformed grains.
* TEM imaging under underfocus conditions reliably captures the size and spatial distribution of He bubbles relevant to recrystallization physics.
* He atoms not visible as bubbles are assumed to be **mechanically insignificant** after sufficient high-temperature annealing.

---

## 2. HIDDEN / IMPLICIT ASSUMPTIONS

### Spatial and Temporal Scope

* Only **near-surface recrystallization** is considered relevant to PFC performance, implicitly assuming bulk recrystallization dominates later or is less critical.
* The examined area is assumed statistically representative of the whole surface despite known heterogeneity in deformation microstructures.

### Bubble Physics

* He bubbles are assumed to be **spherical, immobile obstacles** to boundary motion within the timescale of annealing.
* Coalescence, shape instability, or pressure-driven migration of bubbles are implicitly neglected at 1573 K.

### Dislocation Structure

* Orientation gradients measured by EBSD are assumed to reflect **true subgrain structures**, not artefacts of recovery or surface damage.
* Subgrain boundaries are treated as classical low-angle boundaries composed of dislocation arrays with well-defined mobility laws.

### Stress State

* Residual stresses from rolling and plasma exposure are assumed not to dominate recrystallization kinetics compared to dislocation-stored energy.
* Thermal stresses induced by rapid electron-beam heating are implicitly assumed insufficient to alter nucleation pathways.

---

## 3. FIRST PRINCIPLES GOVERNING THE SYSTEM

### Thermodynamics of Recrystallization

* Recrystallization proceeds by **reduction of stored strain energy** associated with dislocations.
* The driving pressure for recrystallization nucleation and growth scales with:
  [
  P_\text{drive} \sim \rho , G , b^2
  ]
  where ρ is dislocation density, G the shear modulus, and b the Burgers vector.

### Grain Boundary Mechanics

* Grain boundary motion is governed by force balance between:

  * Stored energy driving pressure
  * Boundary curvature pressure
  * Particle pinning pressure
* Classical **Zener pinning** applies when second-phase particles impede boundary migration.

### Subgrain Growth Kinetics

* Subgrain growth increases misorientation until a **high-angle grain boundary** forms, enabling nucleation.
* Limiting subgrain size limits the maximum achievable misorientation, suppressing nucleation.

### Diffusion and Bubble Stability

* He bubble stability is governed by:

  * Vacancy diffusion
  * Gas pressure inside bubbles
  * Surface and elastic energies
* At sufficiently high temperatures, He desorbs or bubbles dissolve, reducing their mechanical influence.

---

## 4. INTERDEPENDENCIES BETWEEN PRINCIPLES

* **Helium bubble pinning** interacts with **subgrain growth kinetics**, not directly with recrystallized grain growth.
* **Dislocation density** determines both the driving force for recrystallization and the availability of subgrain boundaries.
* **Temperature** simultaneously enhances:

  * Dislocation recovery
  * Bubble mobility or dissolution
  * Boundary mobility
* The balance between these competing effects defines a narrow regime of retarded recrystallization.

---

## 5. ASSUMPTION–PRINCIPLE CONNECTIONS

* If He bubbles were mobile or coalesced rapidly, Zener-type pinning of subgrain boundaries would fail, invalidating the suppression model.
* If dislocation density were lower, stored energy would drop below the threshold needed for nucleation regardless of bubble presence.
* If annealing temperatures exceeded bubble stability limits, recrystallization would revert to conventional kinetics, as observed at 1673 K.

---

## 6. HIDDEN FAILURE MODES

### Thermal Excursions

* Transient overheating could dissolve He bubbles prematurely, eliminating recrystallization retardation.
* Local hot spots could allow localized recrystallization, introducing mechanical heterogeneity.

### Irradiation Coupling

* Neutron irradiation may:

  * Generate additional defects
  * Modify bubble nucleation density
  * Alter subgrain structures
* These effects could either reinforce or negate the observed suppression mechanism.

### Mechanical Degradation

* Retarded recrystallization preserves hardness but may increase residual stress, raising susceptibility to brittle fracture.
* Suppressed nucleation could delay but not prevent catastrophic recrystallization once thresholds are crossed.

---

## 7. UNSTATED CONSTRAINTS AND TRADEOFFS

* The mechanism optimizes **surface-layer stability**, not bulk toughness.
* Helium is treated as beneficial for recrystallization control but is also known to degrade ductility and thermal conductivity.
* The approach assumes controllable plasma exposure conditions, which may not hold during off-normal reactor events.

---

## 8. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS AND SYSTEM CONTEXT

* The study isolates **plasma–material interaction** from:

  * Mechanical constraint by monoblock geometry
  * Thermal gradients across thickness
  * Cyclic thermal fatigue
* No coupling to coolant interfaces or macroscopic stress fields is considered.

---

## 9. TEMPORAL EVOLUTION ASSUMPTIONS

* Recrystallization suppression is strongest during **early annealing stages**.
* Once He bubbles dissolve or shrink below effective size, recrystallization accelerates.
* Long-term stability relies on maintaining bubble populations through continued plasma exposure.

---

## 10. SAFETY AND ROBUSTNESS MARGINS

* Safety margin derives from delaying embrittlement rather than eliminating it.
* Single-point vulnerabilities include:

  * Loss of He bubble stability
  * Excessive annealing temperature
  * Reduced dislocation density after long service
* No intrinsic self-limiting mechanism prevents sudden recrystallization once suppression fails.

---

## 11. OPERATIVE VS DESIGN REGIME

### Design Regime

* Controlled He plasma exposure
* High dislocation density
* Near-surface dominated damage
* Annealing temperatures near 1573 K

### Likely Operative Regime

* Spatially varying temperature
* Concurrent neutron damage
* Cyclic thermal loads and ELMs
* Mixed helium production pathways

### Robustness Assessment

* The proposed mechanism is **physically sound but narrowly bounded**.
* It is robust for near-surface layers under moderate thermal exposure.
* Applicability diminishes for bulk recrystallization, extreme transients, or altered bubble stability.
